Definition of Shart and Its Application to Conditional Divorce
Defining the legal term 'shart' and applying it to a conditional divorce case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'shart' (condition) in Hanafi fiqh and apply it to a case of suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq).

Core Idea
A shart is a future possibility that triggers a ruling; it is not the cause but the condition for effect.

In this passage, the author of Al-Hidayah defines the legal concept of 'shart' (condition) and illustrates it with a case of conditional divorce. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing between different types of legal causes. We'll break down the definition and see how it applies when a husband suspends divorce on his wife's emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of shart
Extract the definition from the text: 'A condition is something that is non-existent with the possibility of existence, and the ruling is attached to it.' Note that it must have a realistic possibility of occurring.
The definition is given in both Urdu and Arabic in the source.
2
Distinguish shart from sabab and illa
In Hanafi usul, a shart is external and its existence triggers the ruling, but the ruling is not attributed to it in terms of obligation. A sabab (cause) directly necessitates the ruling, and an illa (effective cause) is the ratio legis.
This distinction is implicit in the text: 'attributed to it in terms of existence not obligation.'
3
Apply the definition to the divorce case
The husband says: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation of you.' Here, emancipation is the shart (currently absent but possible), and the two divorces are the ruling attached to it.
The divorce is suspended (mu'allaq) on the condition.
4
Analyze the reason for raj'ah
When the master frees the wife, the condition is met. At that moment, she becomes free before the divorce takes effect. Since a free woman is not irrevocably divorced by two divorces (only three cause irrevocability), the husband retains the right of raj'ah.
The divorce occurs after the condition, not before.
5
Note the legal effect of suspension
The text states that the pronouncement of divorce occurs at the time of the condition, as if the husband sent it concurrent with emancipation. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that ta'liq (suspension) is valid in talaq.
The divorce is not considered pronounced until the condition is fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ: شرط وہ چیز ہے جو وجود پذیر ہونے کے احتمال کے ساتھ معدوم ہوتی ہے اور حکم اس کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب کوئی حکم مشروط ہو کسی شرط کے ساتھ تو فی الحال شرط معدوم ہوتی ہے البتہ اس کے وجود پذیر ہونے کا احتمال ہوتا ہے، یعنی ممکن ہے کہ شرط پائی جائے اور حکم بھی شرط کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے یعنی شرط پائی جائے گی تو حکم بھی دائر ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر اپنی اس بیوی سے جو کسی غیر کی باندی ہو کہ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثِنْتَيْنِ مَعَ عِشْقٍ مَوْلَا إِيَّاک تیرے آقا کے تجھے آزاد کرنے کے بعد تو دو طلاق والی ہے، پھر آقا نے اس باندی کو آزاد کر دیا تو شوہر کو رجعت کا حق حاصل ہو گا، اس لئے کہ شوہر نے طلاق کو اعتناق یعنی آزادی پر معلق کیا ہے، آزادی شرط ہے اور طلاق مشروط ہے ( شرط اس چیز کو کہتے ہیں جو فی الوقت معدوم ہولیکن اس کا وجود ممکن ہو اور حکم کے ساتھ اس کا تعلق بھی ہو ) تو یہاں شرط جب آزادی پائی جائے گی تو آزادی کے بعد مشروط یعنی دو طلاقیں پائیں جائیں گی، تو چونکہ آزاد عورت دو طلاق سے مغلظہ نہیں ہوتی اس لئے شوہر کو رجوع کرنے کا اور ایک طلاق کا اختیار ہو گا۔
أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثِنْتَيْنِ مَعَ عِتْقِ مَوْلَاكِ إِيَّاكِ فَأَعْتَقَ لَهُ الرَّجْعَةُ) أَى إِذَا قَالَ الرَّجُلُ لِزَوْجَتِهِ الْامَةِ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثِنْتَيْنِ مَعَ إِعْتَاقِ مَوْلَاكَ إِيَّاكِ فَأَعْتَقَهَا الْمَوْلَى طَلَقَتْ ثِنْتَيْنِ وَيَمْلِكُ الزَّوْجُ الرَّجُعَةَ فَالْعِتْقُ حُكْمُ الْإِعْتَاقِ فَاسْتَعِيرَ لِسَبَبِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَمُلِكُ الرَّجُعَةَ، لِأَنَّهُ عَلَّقَ الطَّلْقَتَيْنِ بِالْإِعْتَاقِ وَالْمُعَلَّقُ يُوجَدُ بَعْدَ الشَّرْطِ فَتَطْلُقُ وَهِيَ حُرَّةٌ وَالْحُرَّةُ لَا تَحْرُمُ بِالطَّلْقَتَيْنِ حُرُمَةً غَلِيظَةً وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا بِأَنَّهُ مُعَلَّقٌ بِهِ لِوُجُودِ مَعْنَاهُ، وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ الشَّرْطَ مَا يَكُونُ مَعْدُومًا عَلَى خَطَرِ الْوُجُودِ وَالْحُكْمُ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ وَيُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ وُجُودًا لَا وُجُوبًا، وَهَذَا الْمَعْنَى قَدْ وُجِدَ فِيهِ فَإِذَا صَارَ مُعَلَّقًا بِهِ يَصِيرُ تَطْلِيقًا عِنْدَ وُجُودِ الشَّرَطِ لِدُخُولِهِ عَلَى السَّبَبِ عِنْدَنَا فَيُوجَدُ التَّطْلِيقُ بَعْدَ الْإِعْتَاقِ كَأَنَّهُ أَرْسَلَهُ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ مُقَارِنَا لِلْعِتْقِ الَّذِى هُوَ حُكْمُ الْإِعْتَاقِ فَتَصِيرُ حُرَّةً بِهِ ثُمَّ يَقَعُ عَلَيْهَا الطَّلَاقُ الَّذِي هُوَ حُكْمُ التَّطْلِيقِ بَعْدَ الْحُرِّيَّةِ فَلَا تَحْرُمُ بِهِ حُرُمَةً غَلِيظَةٌ.
تبيين الحقائق كتاب الطلاق فصل فى إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان، ج ۲ ص ۲۱۰، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
Translation: A condition is something that is non-existent with the possibility of existence, and the ruling is attached to it. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that when a ruling is conditioned upon a condition, at present the condition is non-existent, but it has the possibility of existence, i.e., it is possible for the condition to occur, and the ruling is attached to the condition, meaning when the condition occurs, the ruling will apply. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master', and then the master frees her, the husband has the right of return (raj'ah). This is because the husband suspended the divorce upon emancipation; emancipation is the condition and divorce is the conditioned. When the condition (emancipation) occurs, the conditioned (two divorces) occurs. Since a free woman is not rendered irrevocably divorced by two divorces, the husband retains the right to take her back and has the option to give one more divorce. (The husband says to his slave wife: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation of you', then the master frees her, she becomes divorced twice, and the husband retains the right of return. Emancipation ('itq) is the legal effect of the act of freeing, so it is borrowed for its cause. He only has the right of return because he suspended the two divorces on the freeing, and the suspended occurs after the condition, so she is divorced while she is free, and a free woman is not irrevocably divorced by two divorces. We say it is suspended because the meaning of condition is found here. A condition is something non-existent with the possibility of existence, and the ruling is attached to it, attributed to it in terms of existence not obligation. This meaning is found here. When it becomes suspended by it, it becomes a pronouncement of divorce upon the existence of the condition, because it enters upon the cause according to us. So the pronouncement occurs after emancipation, as if he sent it at that time concurrent with the emancipation, which is the effect of freeing. Thus she becomes free by it, then the divorce, which is the effect of the pronouncement, falls upon her after freedom, so she is not irrevocably divorced by it.)
Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Book of Divorce, Chapter on Adding Divorce to Time, vol. 2, p. 210, al-Matba'a al-Kubra al-Amiriyya edition.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines the legal term 'shart' (condition) and applies it to a specific case of conditional divorce. The author states that a condition is something that is currently non-existent but has the potential to exist in the future, and the ruling (here, divorce) is attached to it such that it takes effect only when the condition occurs. The example given is a man who tells his slave wife: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation of you.' When the master frees her, the divorce takes effect at that moment, but since at that moment she becomes free, she is a free woman when the divorce falls. According to Hanafi fiqh, two divorces do not make a free woman irrevocably divorced (i.e., they are not three divorces), so the husband retains the right of raj'ah (taking her back) and has the option to give one more divorce if he wishes. The reasoning employs the definition of shart to show that the divorce is suspended (mu'allaq) on the condition, and it occurs after the condition is met. The text clarifies that the condition is not the cause (sabab) but a precondition; the divorce is attributed to the condition in terms of existence, not obligation. This is a key distinction in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding ta'liq (suspension).

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition is something that is absent at present but has a realistic possibility of occurring, and the legal ruling is tied to its occurrence such that the ruling becomes effective upon fulfillment of the condition.
معلق (mu'allaq): A suspended ruling, here referring to divorce that is contingent upon a future condition. The divorce is not effective immediately but only when the condition occurs.
عتق (itq): Emancipation, the act of freeing a slave. In this context, it is the condition upon which divorce is suspended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a husband says to his slave wife 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation of you,' and then the master frees her, why does the husband retain the right of raj'ah?
A) Because the divorce is invalid since it was conditional.
B) Because she becomes free before the divorce takes effect, so only two divorces fall on a free woman.
C) Because the slave wife cannot be divorced.
D) Because the husband did not intend to divorce her.

Key Takeaway
A shart is a future possibility that triggers a ruling; conditional divorce (talaq mu'allaq) is valid and the divorce takes effect only when the condition is fulfilled.

Conditional Talaq and Raj'ah After Two Divorces
Explains the principle of ta'liq and its application to a conditional divorce upon freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of ta'liq applied to divorce and determine the husband's right of raj'ah after two conditional divorces upon manumission.

Core Idea
A divorce suspended on a condition takes effect when the condition occurs; two divorces to a free woman do not cause final separation.

This lesson examines a foundational principle in Hanafi family law: how a divorce can be suspended on a future event and what effect that has on the marital bond. Using the example of a husband who tells his slave wife she is divorced twice upon her master freeing her, we will see how the timing of the condition determines the wife's legal status and the husband's right to take her back (raj'ah). By understanding this, you will grasp the critical distinction between conditional and immediate divorces and the consequences for remarriage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of shart
The passage defines a condition (shart) as something currently non-existent but possible to exist, and the ruling is attached to its existence. Note that the condition is not the cause of the ruling but rather the event upon which the ruling is suspended.
Hanafi usul distinguishes shart (condition) from sabab (cause). The divorce itself is the ruling that becomes effective upon the condition.
2
Identify the condition and the suspended ruling
In the case, the husband says: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's freeing you.' The condition is the master's manumission. The suspended ruling is the two divorces.
The Arabic phrase 'anta taliq thintayni ma'a 'itqi mawlaki iyyaki' clearly attaches the divorce to the act of manumission.
3
Determine when the divorce takes effect
According to the principle, the divorce occurs after the condition is fulfilled, not before. So when the master frees her, at that moment the two divorces fall. The wife becomes free at that same instant.
The text says: 'fa-a'taqaha al-mawla taluqat thintayni' - 'then the master frees her, she is divorced twice.'
4
Assess the wife’s status at the time of divorce
At the moment the divorce occurs, the wife is now a free woman because the manumission preceded the divorce (or is simultaneous). A free woman does not become irrevocably separated (baynunah mughallazah) by two divorces; only three cause final separation.
The text states: 'wahiya hurrah' - 'while she is free.' This is the key: her status at the time of divorce matters.
5
Derive the ruling on raj'ah
Since only two revocable divorces occurred, the husband has the right to take her back during her iddah period (raj'ah). He retains one remaining divorce.
The passage concludes: 'falahu al-raj'ah' - 'so he has the right of return.'

ترجمہ: شرط وہ چیز ہے جو وجود پذیر ہونے کے احتمال کے ساتھ معدوم ہوتی ہے اور حکم اس کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب کوئی حکم مشروط ہو کسی شرط کے ساتھ تو فی الحال شرط معدوم ہوتی ہے البتہ اس کے وجود پذیر ہونے کا احتمال ہوتا ہے، یعنی ممکن ہے کہ شرط پائی جائے اور حکم بھی شرط کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے یعنی شرط پائی جائے گی تو حکم بھی دائر ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر اپنی اس بیوی سے یہ کہے جو کسی غیر کی باندی ہو کہ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثِنْتَيْنِ مَعَ عِشْقٍ مَوْلَا إِيَّاک تیرے آقا کے تجھے آزاد کرنے کے بعد تو دو طلاق والی ہے، پھر آقا نے اس باندی کو آزاد کر دیا تو شوہر کور جعت کا حق حاصل ہو گا، اس لئے کہ شوہر نے طلاق کو اعتناق یعنی آزادی پر معلق کیا ہے، آزادی شرط ہے اور طلاق مشروط ہے ( شرط اس چیز کو کہتے ہیں جو فی الوقت معدوم ہولیکن اس کا وجود ممکن ہو اور حکم کے ساتھ اس کا تعلق بھی ہو ) تو یہاں شرط جب آزادی پائی جائے گی تو آزادی کے بعد مشروط یعنی دو طلاقیں پائیں جائیں گی، تو چونکہ آزاد عورت دو طلاق سے مغلظہ نہیں ہوتی اس لئے شوہر کو رجوع کرنے کا اور ایک طلاق کا اختیار ہو گا۔
أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثِنْتَيْنِ مَعَ عِتْقِ مَوْلَاكِ إِيَّاكِ فَأَعْتَقَ لَهُ الرَّجْعَةُ) أَى إِذَا قَالَ الرَّجُلُ لِزَوْجَتِهِ الْامَةِ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثِنْتَيْنِ مَعَ إِعْتَاقِ مَوْلَاكَ إِيَّاكِ فَأَعْتَقَهَا الْمَوْلَى طَلَقَتْ ثِنْتَيْنِ وَيَمْلِكُ الزَّوْجُ الرَّجُعَةَ فَالْعِتْقُ حُكْمُ الْإِعْتَاقِ فَاسْتَعِيرَ لِسَبَبِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَمُلِكُ الرَّجُعَةَ، لِأَنَّهُ عَلَّقَ الطَّلْقَتَيْنِ بِالْإِعْتَاقِ وَالْمُعَلَّقُ يُوجَدُ بَعْدَ الشَّرْطِ فَتَطْلُقُ وَهِيَ حُرَّةٌ وَالْحُرَّةُ لَا تَحْرُمُ بِالطَّلْقَتَيْنِ حُرُمَةً غَلِيظَةً وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا بِأَنَّهُ مُعَلَّقٌ بِهِ لِوُجُودِ مَعْنَاهُ، وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ الشَّرْطَ مَا يَكُونُ مَعْدُومًا عَلَى خَطَرِ الْوُجُودِ وَالْحُكْمُ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ وَيُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ وُجُودًا لَا وُجُوبًا، وَهَذَا الْمَعْنَى قَدْ وُجِدَ فِيهِ فَإِذَا صَارَ مُعَلَّقًا بِهِ يَصِيرُ تَطْلِيقًا عِنْدَ وُجُودِ الشَّرَطِ لِدُخُولِهِ عَلَى السَّبَبِ عِنْدَنَا فَيُوجَدُ التَّطْلِيقُ بَعْدَ الْإِعْتَاقِ كَأَنَّهُ أَرْسَلَهُ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ مُقَارِنَا لِلْعِتْقِ الَّذِى هُوَ حُكْمُ الْإِعْتَاقِ فَتَصِيرُ حُرَّةً بِهِ ثُمَّ يَقَعُ عَلَيْهَا الطَّلَاقُ الَّذِي هُوَ حُكْمُ التَّطْلِيقِ بَعْدَ الْحُرِّيَّةِ فَلَا تَحْرُمُ بِهِ حُرُمَةً غَلِيظَةٌ.
تبيين الحقائق كتاب الطلاق فصل فى إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان، ج ۲ ص ۲۱۰، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
Translation: A condition is something that is non-existent with the possibility of existence, and the ruling is attached to it. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that when a ruling is conditional upon a condition, then at present the condition is non-existent, but there is a possibility of its existence, i.e., it is possible that the condition will be found and the ruling is also attached to the condition, i.e., when the condition is found, the ruling will also apply. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband says to his wife who is a slave of someone else, 'You are divorced twice upon your master's freeing you,' then after the master frees her, the husband has the right of return (raj'ah), because the husband suspended the divorce upon 'itq (freedom), freedom is the condition and the divorce is the conditional. So here, when the condition of freedom is found, after freedom the conditional, i.e., two divorces, will occur. And since a free woman does not become irrevocably separated by two divorces, the husband has the right to return and one remaining divorce.
'You are divorced twice upon your master's freeing you' and he has the right of return after the master frees her. That is, if a man says to his slave wife, 'You are divorced twice upon your master's freeing you,' and then the master frees her, she is divorced twice and the husband has the right of return. So 'itq (freedom) is the ruling of 'i'taq (manumission), and the word is borrowed for its cause. He only has the right of return because he suspended the two divorces upon manumission, and the suspended (divorce) occurs after the condition, so she is divorced while free, and a free woman is not made unlawful with two divorces in a severe manner. And we only say that it is suspended upon it because the meaning is found. This is because a condition is something non-existent with the possibility of existence, and the ruling is attached to it and attributed to it in terms of existence, not obligation. This meaning has been found in it, so when it becomes suspended upon it, the divorcing occurs upon the existence of the condition, because it enters upon the cause according to us. Thus, the divorcing occurs after manumission, as if he sent it at that time coinciding with the 'itq, which is the ruling of manumission, so she becomes free by it, then the divorce which is the ruling of divorcing falls upon her after freedom, so she is not made unlawful by it in a severe manner.
(Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Talaq, Chapter on Adding Divorce to Time, vol. 2, p. 210, Al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah edition)
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling with reasoning, explaining the principle of ta'liq (suspension) in divorce. The author first states the general principle: a condition is something currently non-existent but possible, and the ruling is attached to it. Then he applies it to a specific case: a husband says to his slave wife (owned by another), 'You are divorced twice upon your master's freeing you.' Once the master frees her, the two divorces take effect. However, because she is now free at the moment of divorce, she does not become irrevocably separated (baynunah mughallazah) by two divorces; a free woman requires three divorces for final separation. Therefore, the husband retains the right to take her back (raj'ah) and has one remaining divorce. The Arabic text from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq elaborates the reasoning: the word 'itq is used metaphorically for the cause (manumission), and the divorce is suspended upon the condition. Since the condition is found, the divorce occurs after freedom, so she is divorced as a free woman. This ruling is based on the Hanafi understanding that the condition's existence (wujud) is what triggers the ruling, not the obligation (wujub). The key legal maxim is: al-shart ma yusbah wujuduhu (the condition is something that can be realized). From a Hanafi perspective, this case illustrates that the timing of the divorce's effect is critical; the wife's legal status at the moment of divorce determines the nature of the separation.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that is currently non-existent but possible to exist; the ruling is attached to its existence.
معلق (mu'allaq): A suspended divorce; its effect is deferred until the condition occurs.
رجعت (raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife within the waiting period after one or two revocable divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his slave wife, 'You are divorced twice upon your master freeing you,' and the master frees her, how many divorces occur and what is the husband's right?
A) Three divorces, no raj'ah
B) Two divorces, husband has raj'ah
C) One divorce, husband has raj'ah
D) No divorce because condition invalid

Key Takeaway
When a divorce is suspended on a future condition, the divorce takes effect only upon fulfillment, and the wife's status at that moment determines the number of revocable divorces and the husband's right of raj'ah.

Finger Indication in Divorce with Ambiguous Number
Explains the ruling on finger pointing with 'hākadhā' in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on finger pointing in divorce when the word 'hākadhā' is used, and explain the condition and its proof from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
A finger gesture clarifies an ambiguous number only when accompanied by the word 'hākadhā'.

When a husband pronounces divorce with an ambiguous number indicator such as 'hākadhā' (like this), his finger gesture is decisive in determining the number of divorces. This ruling is grounded in both customary practice and the prophetic Sunnah, as demonstrated by the Prophet's explanation of the month's days. In this lesson, we unpack the condition of the ambiguous word, the proof from the hadith, and the requirement that the fingers be spread.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
Recognize that we are analyzing a pronouncement of divorce accompanied by the ambiguous word 'hākadhā' and a finger gesture. This is a specific scenario where the number of divorces is unclear from words alone.
The act is 'talaq' with an ambiguous number word.
2
Examine the condition: ambiguous number word
Note that the word 'hākadhā' is required to make the number ambiguous. Without this word, the gesture does not convey a number; only one divorce occurs by default.
The condition is 'iqtiran bi-l-'adad al-mubham'.
3
Apply the proof from the Sunnah
The Prophet used finger gestures to demonstrate the number of days in a month, always with the word 'hākadhā'. This establishes that finger pointing is a valid means of indicating number when accompanied by 'hākadhā'.
The hadith is cited as proof (dalil naqli).
4
Distinguish spread vs. closed fingers
The gesture must be with spread fingers (al-manshūrah); closed fingers (al-madhmūmah) do not indicate a number according to custom and Sunnah.
This is based on 'al-urf wa al-sunnah'.
5
State the resulting ruling for each case
If husband points with three spread fingers plus 'hākadhā', three divorces occur; with two, two; with one, one. If the gesture is invalid (closed fingers or missing 'hākadhā'), only one divorce occurs.
The number of fingers indicates the number of divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْإِشَارَةَ بِالْأَصَابِعِ تُفِيدُ الْعِلْمَ بِالْعَدَدِ فِي مَجْرَى الْعَادَةِ إِذَا اقْتَرَنَتْ بِالْعَدَدِ الْمُبْهَمِ. أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ هَكَذَا وَأَشَارَ بِثَلاثِ أَصَابِعَ فَهِيَ ثَلاثٌ لِأَنَّ الْإِشَارَةَ بِالْأَصَابِعِ تُفِيدُ الْعِلْمَ بِالْعَدَدِ عُرُفًا وَشَرُعًا إِذَا اقْتَرَنَتْ بِالِاسْمِ الْمُبْهَمِ قَالَ عَلَيْهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ الشَّهْرُ هَكَذَا وَهَكَذَا وَهَكَذَا وَأَشَارَ بِأَصَابِعِهِ الْعَشَرَةِ يَعْنِي ثَلَاثِينَ يَوْمًا قَالَ الشَّهُرُ هَكَذَا وَهَكَذَا وَهَكَذَا وَخَنَسَ إِبْهَامَهُ فِي الثَّالِثَةِ يَعْنِي تِسْعَةً وَعِشْرِينَ يَوْمًا، وَلَوْ أَشَارَ بِالْوَاحِدَةِ طَلْقَتْ وَاحِدَةً، وَلَوْ أَشَارَ بِالثّنْتَيْنِ طَلْقَتْ ثِنْتَيْنِ وَالْإِشَارَةُ تَقَعُ بِالْمَنْشُورَةِ مِنْهَا دُونَ الْمَضْمُومَةِ لِلْعُرْفِ وَالسُّنَّةِ.
Indeed, pointing with fingers conveys knowledge of the number in the course of custom when it is accompanied by an ambiguous number. 'You are divorced like this' and he pointed with three fingers, so it is three, because pointing with fingers conveys knowledge of the number by custom and law when it is accompanied by an ambiguous word. The Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he pointed with his ten fingers, meaning thirty days. He said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he tucked his thumb the third time, meaning twenty-nine days. If he pointed with one, she is divorced once; if he pointed with two, she is divorced twice. And the pointing occurs with the spread fingers, not the closed, due to custom and Sunnah.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Sunnah. The principle is that when a husband says 'أنت طالق هكذا' (you are divorced like this) and simultaneously points with his fingers, the number of fingers indicates the number of divorces, provided the word 'هكذا' is used (an ambiguous number). If no 'هكذا' is said, only one divorce occurs. The proof is the Prophet's demonstration of the month's days using finger gestures. The ruling specifies that the pointing must be with spread fingers (المنشورة) not closed (المضمومة), based on custom and the Sunnah. This is a Hanafi-specific rule applied in divorce cases.

Key Terms
هكذا (hākadhā): An ambiguous word meaning 'like this', used to indicate a number when accompanied by a gesture.
الإشارة بالأصابع (al-ishārah bi-l-aṣābi‘): Pointing with fingers to convey a numerical meaning.
العدد المبهم (al-‘adad al-mubham): The ambiguous number (e.g., 'hākadhā') that is clarified by the finger gesture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'أنت طالق' and points with three fingers but does NOT say 'هكذا', how many divorces occur?
A) Three
B) One
C) Two
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, finger pointing determines the number of divorces only when the ambiguous word 'hākadhā' is used and the fingers are spread.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Tashbih Divorce: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar
Presents the divergent rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar on tashbih divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar on tashbih divorce and distinguish the basis of their ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Whether a divorce simile produces ba'in or raj‘i depends on the methodology: literal wording, custom, or broad principle.

In this lesson, we explore an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a simile (tashbih) in divorce. The three great imams of the school—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar—differ on whether such a declaration immediately makes the divorce irrevocable (ba'in) or revocable (raj‘i). Understanding this divergence sharpens your ability to identify the operative factors in Hanafi fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the base ruling: according to Abu Hanifa, any tashbih divorce produces a ba'in divorce, regardless of any mention of greatness.
This is the broadest view.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's qualification
Note that Abu Yusuf requires the specific mention of the word 'عظم' (greatness) for the divorce to be ba'in; otherwise it is raj‘i.
Focus on the literal wording.
3
Identify Zufar's qualification
Zufar holds that if the object of comparison is commonly described as great among people, the divorce is ba'in; otherwise it is raj‘i.
Considers custom and common usage.
4
Compare the three positions
Contrast the conditions: Abu Hanifa (no condition), Abu Yusuf (explicit greatness), Zufar (customary greatness).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Recognize that the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, as presented in Al-Hidayah.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصْلُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ شَبَّهَ الطَّلَاقَ بِشَيْءٍ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا، أَيُّ شَيْءٍ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ، ذَكَرَ الْعَظْمَ أَوْ لَمْ يَذْكُرُ، وَعِندَ أَبِى يُوسُفَ إِنْ ذَكَرَ الْعِظَمِ يَكُونُ بَائِنَا وَإِلَّا فَلَا، وَعِنْدَ زُفَرَ إِنْ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ مِمَّا يُوْصَفُ بِالْعِظَمِ عِنْدَ النَّاسِ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ رَجُعِيٌّ .
The principle according to Abu Hanifa is that if he compares divorce to something, it occurs as ba'in (irrevocable), whatever the thing compared to is, whether he mentions greatness or not. According to Abu Yusuf, if he mentions greatness, it is ba'in; otherwise not. According to Zufar, if the thing compared to is such that it is described as great among people, it occurs as ba'in; otherwise it is raj‘i (revocable).
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a simile (tashbih) in divorce. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, whenever a husband compares his wife's divorce to something, the divorce immediately becomes ba'in (irrevocable), regardless of what that thing is or whether he explicitly mentions greatness. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the divorce becomes ba'in only if the husband explicitly mentions the word 'عظم' (greatness) in the simile. Imam Zufar takes a middle position: the divorce is ba'in if the thing compared to is commonly regarded as great in society, otherwise it is raj‘i (revocable). The example given in the commentary is 'أنت طالق مثل الجبل' (you are divorced like a mountain) — according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar it is ba'in, while Abu Yusuf considers it raj‘i since 'عظم' was not uttered. This illustrates how differences in legal methodology (e.g., emphasis on literal wording vs. custom) lead to divergent rulings.

Key Terms
تشبيه الطلاق (tashbīh al-ṭalāq): Comparing the divorce to something, e.g., 'like a mountain', to emphasize its finality.
بائن (bā'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without a right of return unless a new marriage contract is made.
رجعي (raj‘ī): A revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back during the waiting period without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a husband says 'أنت طالق مثل القمر' (like the moon), without mentioning greatness, what type of divorce occurs?
A) Ba'in
B) Raj‘i
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tashbih divorce, the mufta bihi position (Abu Hanifa) holds it is always ba'in, while Abu Yusuf and Zufar add conditions regarding the mention or customary description of greatness.

The Principle of Tashbih in Divorce: Positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar
States the three Hanafi imams' principles on tashbih al-talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the principles of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar regarding tashbih al-talaq and apply them to determine whether a divorce is ba'in or raj'i.

Core Idea
The effect of tashbih in divorce depends on the imam's view: Abu Hanifa always ba'in; Abu Yusuf needs explicit 'azam; Zufar relies on custom.

When a husband says 'You are divorced like a mountain' or 'like a sesame seed', does the divorce become ba'in (irrevocable) or raj'i (revocable)? This question reveals a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute about the role of exaggeration and custom in interpreting a comparison. In this lesson, we will dissect the principles of the three great Hanafi imams and see how a mere word or customary perception can change the legal nature of a divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared issue
What is tashbih al-talaq? It is when a man compares his divorce to something else, e.g., 'You are divorced like the mountain.' The issue is whether such a comparison makes the divorce ba'in or raj'i.
Ba'in means the husband cannot take his wife back without a new marriage; raj'i allows reconciliation during the iddah.
2
State Abu Hanifa's principle and illah
Abu Hanifa: any tashbih, regardless of the object or mention of 'azam, yields a ba'in divorce. His illah: the comparison itself implies exaggeration ('azam), which makes the divorce ba'in.
3
State Abu Yusuf's principle and illah
Abu Yusuf: ba'in only if the husband explicitly mentions the word 'azam; otherwise raj'i. His illah: tashbih can be merely in the essence without exaggeration, so the extra word 'azam is needed for ba'in.
4
State Zufar's principle and illah
Zufar: ba'in if the object of comparison is customarily described as great (e.g., mountain); otherwise raj'i. His illah: custom ('urf) determines whether 'azam is implicit, so no need for explicit mention.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical outcome: e.g., 'like a sesame seed'—Abu Hanifa says ba'in, Abu Yusuf says raj'i, Zufar says raj'i. The mufta bihi (operative position) is Abu Hanifa's view, but the other views are valid ijtihad.
Imam Muhammad's position is inconsistent; sometimes he agrees with Abu Hanifa, sometimes with Abu Yusuf.

الأصْلُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ شَبَّهَ الطَّلَاقَ بِشَيْءٍ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا، أَيُّ شَيْءٍ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ، ذَكَرَ الْعَظْمَ أَوْ لَمْ يَذْكُرُ، وَعِندَ أَبِى يُوسُفَ إِنْ ذَكَرَ الْعِظَمِ يَكُونُ بَائِنَا وَإِلَّا فَلَا، وَعِنْدَ زُفَرَ إِنْ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ مِمَّا يُوْصَفُ بِالْعِظَمِ عِنْدَ النَّاسِ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ رَجُعِيٌّ . فَأَصْلُ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ مَتَى شَبَّهَ الطَّلَاقَ بِشَيْءٍ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا أَيَّ شَيْءٍ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ لِلزِّيَادَةِ وَعِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ إِنْ ذَكَرَ الْعِظَمَ فَكَذَلِكَ وَإِلَّا فَرَجْعِيٌّ أَيَّ شَيْءٍ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ، لِأَنَّ التَّشْبِيهَ قَدْ يَكُونُ فِي التَّوْحِيدِ عَلَى التَّجْرِيدِ وَذِكْرُ الْعِظَمِ لِلزِّيَادَةِ لَا مَحَالَةَ وَعِنْدَ زُفَرَ إِنْ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ مِمَّا يُوصَفُ بِالْعِظَمِ عِندَ النَّاسِ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا وَإِلَّا فَرَجْعِيٌّ ذَكَرَ الْعِظَمَ أَوْ لَا. وَقَوْلُ مُحَمَّدٍ مُضْطَرِبٌ يُرْوَى مَعَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمَعَ أَبِى يُوسُفَ وَثَمَرَتُهُ تَظْهَرُ فِى قَوْلِهِ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ مِثْلَ سِمْسِمَةٍ أَوْ عِظَمَ سِمُسِمَةٍ أَوْ كَالْجَبَلِ أَوْ عِظَمَ الْجَبَلِ.
The principle according to Abu Hanifa is that if he compares divorce to something, it occurs as ba'in, whatever the object of comparison, whether he mentions 'azam or not. According to Abu Yusuf, if he mentions 'azam, it is ba'in; otherwise not. According to Zufar, if the object of comparison is something that people describe as 'azam, it occurs as ba'in; otherwise it is raj'i. So the principle of Abu Hanifa is that whenever he compares divorce to something, it occurs as ba'in, whatever the object of comparison, due to the extra (meaning). According to Abu Yusuf, if he mentions 'azam, then similarly; otherwise it is raj'i, whatever the object of comparison, because the comparison may be in the mere essence (without exaggeration) and mentioning 'azam is undoubtedly for excess. According to Zufar, if the object of comparison is something described as 'azam among people, it occurs as ba'in; otherwise raj'i, whether he mentions 'azam or not. The statement of Muhammad is inconsistent; it is narrated with Abu Hanifa and with Abu Yusuf. Its fruit appears in his saying: 'You are divorced like a sesame seed' or 'like the greatness of a sesame seed' or 'like a mountain' or 'like the greatness of a mountain.'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah presents the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning tashbih al-talaq (comparing divorce to something). The core issue is: when a husband says 'You are divorced like [something]', does the divorce become ba'in (irrevocable) or raj'i (revocable)? The text lays out three principles: (1) Abu Hanifa holds that any tashbih, regardless of the object or mention of 'azam (greatness), results in a ba'in divorce. His reasoning is that the tashbih itself implies exaggeration ('azam), so the divorce is ba'in. (2) Abu Yusuf requires explicit mention of the word 'azam; if the husband says 'like the greatness of the mountain' (mithl 'azam al-jabal), it is ba'in; if he says 'like the mountain' without mentioning 'azam, it is raj'i. He argues that tashbih can be merely in the essence (without exaggeration), so the extra word 'azam is needed to make it ba'in. (3) Zufar looks to custom: if the object of comparison is commonly described as great among people (such as a mountain), the tashbih implies 'azam even without the word, so it is ba'in; otherwise raj'i. He considers the 'urf (custom) to determine whether 'azam is understood. Additionally, the text notes that Imam Muhammad's position is mudtarib (inconsistent) — sometimes narrated agreeing with Abu Hanifa, sometimes with Abu Yusuf. The practical outcome is seen in examples: if the husband says 'like a sesame seed' (small), Zufar would say raj'i (since sesame is not called great), but if he says 'like a mountain', Zufar says ba'in. Abu Hanifa says ba'in in both due to tashbih itself, while Abu Yusuf requires mentioning 'azam for ba'in. The commentary thus clarifies the natures of the dispute and the underlying illa (cause) for each imam.

Key Terms
تشبيه الطلاق (tashbīh al-ṭalāq): Comparing divorce to something else using words like 'mithl' or 'ka', e.g., 'You are divorced like a mountain'.
بائن (bāʾin): A divorce that is irrevocable; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract and new mahr.
رجعي (rajʿī): A revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage.
عظم (ʿaẓam): Greatness or magnificence; in this context, a word that indicates exaggeration or bigness of the object being compared to.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced like a sesame seed' (مثل سمسمة), what is the hukm?
A) Ba'in divorce
B) Raj'i divorce
C) No divorce
D) It depends on whether 'azam is mentioned

Key Takeaway
The nature of a divorce pronounced via tashbih (comparison) depends on the imam's principle regarding the implication of greatness; the operative Hanafi position is that any tashbih results in a ba'in divorce.

Similitude in Talaq: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Analyzing Hanafi imams' positions on talaq with mountain similitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar on talaq using similitude and apply their principles to new examples.

Core Idea
The type of talaq in a similitude depends on whether tashbih implies emphasis (mubalaghah) or not.

When a husband says 'you are divorced like a mountain,' is it a final separation or a revocable divorce? This question reveals a rich internal debate among the early Hanafi imams. Their disagreement stems from differing interpretations of how a similitude (tashbih) affects the divorce wording. In this lesson, we will map each imam's principle and apply it to concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The hub is whether a tashbih (like 'like a mountain') without explicit mention of greatness ('izam) makes the talaq ba'in or raj'i. Note that if greatness is mentioned, all agree on ba'in.
2
State each imam's principle
Abu Hanifa: any tashbih is emphasis, so ba'in. Abu Yusuf: only if greatness is mentioned, else raj'i. Zufar: depends on common perception of the object's greatness. Muhammad: inconsistent narrations.
Summarize the key rule for each.
3
Apply the principles to a mountain
For 'like a mountain' without greatness: Abu Hanifa → ba'in; Abu Yusuf → raj'i; Zufar → ba'in (mountain is great in custom). For 'like the greatness of a mountain', all → ba'in.
Do the same for a sesame seed.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: the wife's status differs (irrevocably divorced vs. revocable) and what the husband can do during iddah.
Focus on the wife's marital status and the husband's right to take her back.
5
Recognize Muhammad's inconsistency
Muhammad's position is reported both with Abu Hanifa and with Abu Yusuf. This shows the complexity of relying on a single narration.
This underscores the need to know the mufta bihi (operative view) in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذکورہ صورت میں اس لئے طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی کہ مذکورہ صورت میں طلاق کو ایسی چیز سے تشبیہ دی ہے جو مما يوصف بالعظیم کے قبیل سے ہے اس لئے کہ ”جبل “ ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جو لوگوں کے عرف میں عظم ہے، اور ان کے اصول کے مطابق ایسی طلاق سے طلاق بائن واقع ہوتی ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی اور امام ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک مذکورہ صورت میں اس سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی اس لئے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں مشبہ بہ کو عظم“ کی صفت سے متصف نہیں کیا اور ان کے اصول کے مطابق ایسے لفظ سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ اور اگر شوہر نے کہا کہ مِثْلَ عِظَمِ الْجَبَلِ “ تو سب کے نزدیک طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں دیگر ائمہ کی شروط و قیود بھی پائے جاتے ہیں ۔ فَأَصْلُ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ مَتَى شَبَّهَ الطَّلَاقَ بِشَيْءٍ يَقَعُ بَائِنَا أَيَّ شَيْءٍ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ لِلزِّيَادَةِ وَعِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ إِنْ ذَكَرَ الْعِظَمَ فَكَذَلِكَ وَإِلَّا فَرَجْعِيٌّ أَيَّ شَيْءٍ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ، لِأَنَّ التَّشْبِيهَ قَدْ يَكُونُ فِي التَّوْحِيدِ عَلَى التَّجْرِيدِ وَذِكْرُ الْعِظَمِ لِلزِّيَادَةِ لَا مَحَالَةَ وَعِنْدَ زُفَرَ إِنْ كَانَ الْمُشَبَّهُ بِهِ مِمَّا يُوصَفُ بِالْعِظَمِ عِندَ النَّاسِ يَقَعُ بَائِنًا وَإِلَّا فَرَجْعِيٌّ ذَكَرَ الْعِظَمَ أَوْ لَا. وَقَوْلُ مُحَمَّدٍ مُضْطَرِبٌ يُرْوَى مَعَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمَعَ أَبِى يُوسُفَ وَثَمَرَتُهُ تَظْهَرُ فِى قَوْلِهِ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ مِثْلَ سِمْسِمَةٍ أَوْ عِظَمَ سِمُسِمَةٍ أَوْ كَالْجَبَلِ أَوْ عِظَمَ الْجَبَلِ. تبيين الحقائق كتاب الطلاق فصل فى إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان، ج ۲ ص ۲۱۲، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
In the mentioned case, talaq ba'in will occur because the divorce is compared to something that is of the type that can be described as great, since 'mountain' is among things that are considered great in people's custom, and according to his principle such divorce results in talaq ba'in. Therefore, in the mentioned case, talaq ba'in will occur. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, in the mentioned case, talaq raj'i will occur because the object of comparison is not described with the attribute of greatness, and according to his principle such a word results in talaq raj'i. So in the mentioned case, talaq raj'i will occur. If the husband said 'like the greatness of the mountain', then according to all, talaq ba'in will occur because in that case the conditions and restrictions of the other imams are also present. The principle of Abu Hanifa is: whenever he compares divorce to something, it falls as ba'in, no matter what the object of comparison is, for emphasis. According to Abu Yusuf: if he mentions greatness, then likewise; otherwise it is raj'i, no matter what the object of comparison is, because the comparison may be in essence without addition, and mentioning greatness is inevitably for emphasis. According to Zufar: if the object of comparison is among things that are described as great in people's custom, it falls as ba'in; otherwise it is raj'i, whether he mentions greatness or not. The statement of Muhammad is inconsistent; it is narrated with Abu Hanifa and with Abu Yusuf. Its practical effect appears in his saying: 'You are divorced like a sesame seed or like the greatness of a sesame seed, or like a mountain or like the greatness of a mountain.' (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Talaq, Fasl fi Idafat al-Talaq ila al-Zaman, vol. 2, p. 212, al-Matba'at al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah)
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the type of talaq (divorce) that occurs when a husband uses a similitude (tashbih) for divorce. The specific scenario involves comparing the divorce to a mountain (jabal) or to the greatness of a mountain ('izam al-jabal). The author first explains the ruling in Urdu: if the husband says 'anti taliqun mithla al-jabal' (you are divorced like a mountain), then according to the imam whose principle is that any similitude with something considered great results in talaq ba'in, it is ba'in. But according to Abu Yusuf, since the word 'greatness' is not mentioned, it is talaq raj'i. Then if 'mithla 'izam al-jabal' is said, all agree on talaq ba'in because all conditions for ba'in are met. Then the author quotes the Arabic principle from the Hidayah or Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Abu Hanifa's principle is that whenever divorce is compared to any object (for emphasis), it becomes ba'in. Abu Yusuf says if greatness is mentioned then ba'in, otherwise raj'i, regardless of the object. Zufar says if the object is commonly described as great, then ba'in, otherwise raj'i, regardless of mentioning greatness. Imam Muhammad's position is reported inconsistently: sometimes with Abu Hanifa, sometimes with Abu Yusuf. The practical application is illustrated with examples: sesame seed (small) vs mountain. This dispute shows how differences in usul (principles) lead to different rulings.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): A type of divorce that is irrevocable; the husband cannot take his wife back without a new marriage and her intervening marriage to another.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce; the husband can take his wife back during the iddah without a new marriage.
تشبیہ (tashbih): A similitude or comparison used in the wording of divorce, e.g., 'you are divorced like a mountain'.
مشبه به (mushabbah bihi): The object of comparison in a tashbih, e.g., mountain or sesame seed.
عظم (al-'izam / 'azm): Greatness or magnitude; explicitly mentioning it in the tashbih changes the ruling for Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a husband says 'you are divorced like a mountain' (without mentioning greatness), what type of talaq occurs?
A) Talaq ba'in
B) Talaq raj'i
C) Talaq bid'i
D) No talaq

Key Takeaway
The type of talaq in a similitude depends on whether the imam emphasizes the mere tashbih (Abu Hanifa), the mention of greatness (Abu Yusuf), or the custom (Zufar); there is no single answer.

The Grammatical Rule of 'Qabl' in Talaq Statements
Defining grammatical rule for 'qabl' in talaq and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting 'qabl' (before) in talaq pronouncements to determine the number of divorces that take effect.

Core Idea
When 'qabl' is not attached to a pronoun, it qualifies the first mentioned, so only the first talaq occurs.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a key grammatical rule used in Hanafi fiqh to resolve ambiguity in divorce statements. The rule hinges on whether the adverb 'qabl' is connected to a pronoun (ha' al-kunaya) or not. Understanding this rule allows the mufti to correctly determine the number of divorces when the husband uses phrases like 'anti taliqun wahidatan qabla wahidatin.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Recognize that the husband mentions two things (e.g., two numbers of divorces) with 'qabl' (or 'ba'd') placed between them.
2
Check for pronoun attachment
Determine whether the adverb 'qabl' is attached to a pronoun (e.g., 'qablahu') or not (e.g., 'qabla'). This is the key condition.
The pronoun is the ha' al-kunaya (pronominal 'ha').
3
Apply the rule to talaq
Without pronoun: adverb qualifies the first mentioned → only the first divorce takes effect, making the wife ba'in (irrevocable). With pronoun: adverb qualifies the last mentioned → the second divorce would be the one that takes effect, but note that if the first divorce already ends the marriage, subsequent divorces are void.
The Hidayah example shows only the case without pronoun; the outcome is a single ba'in talaq.

Source Text

فصل في الطلاق قبل الدخول (٢٦) إِنَّهُ مَتَى ذُكِرَ شَيْئَيْنِ وَأُدْخِلَ بَيْنَهُمَا حَرُفُ الظُّرُفِ إِنْ قَرَنَهَا بِهَاءِ الْكِنَايَةِ كَانَ صِفَةً لِلْمَذْكُوُر آخِرًا وَإِلَّا أَوَّلا. ترجمہ: جب دو چیزوں کو ذکر کیا جائے اور ان کے درمیان حرف ظرف ( قبل، بعد ) کو لایا جائے تو اگر حرف ظرف ہائے کنایہ ( مثلا قبلہ میں ضمیر ) کے ساتھ متصل کردے تو حرف ظرف اس جملے کی صفت ہو گا جو آخر میں مذکور ہے ، ورنہ حرف ظرف شروع میں ذکر کردہ جملے کی صفت ہو گا۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب دو چیزیں ذکر کی جائیں اور ان کے درمیان حرف ظرف کو لایا جائے ، مثلاً قبل، بعد “ وغیرہ ، اب دیکھا جائے گا کہ متکلم نے اس ظرف کو ہاء کنایہ کے ساتھ مقترن کیا ہے یا نہیں یعنی ظرف کو ہاء ضمیر کی طرف مضاف کیا ہے یا نہیں ، اگر اس نے ظرف کو ہاء کنایہ کے ساتھ ملایا ہے تو اس وقت یہ ظرف اس جملے کی صفت ہو گا جو آخر میں مذکور ہے،مثلاً’جاء نی زيد قبله عمر و “یہاں چونکہ ضمیر حرف ظرف سے متصل ہے اس لئے ظرف بعد والے کلمے کی صفت واقع ہوگا اور مطلب ہوگا میرے پاس زید سے پہلے عمر و آیا ، اور اگر متکلم نے حرف ظرف کو ہاء کنایہ کے ساتھ نہیں ملایا تو اس وقت یہ ظرف شروع میں ذکر کردہ جملے کی صفت ہو گا، مثلا’جاء نی زید قبل عمر و “ یہاں چونکہ ظرف ضمیر سے متصل نہیں ہے اس لئے پہلے جملے کی صفت ہو گا اور مطلب ہو گا میرے پاس عمر و سے پہلے زید آیا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہا انتِ طَالِقٌ وَاحِدَةً قَبلَ وَاحِدَةٍ “تو پہلی ہی طلاق سے عورت بائنہ ہو جائے گی دوسری طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی، کیونکہ شوہر نے دو چیزیں ذکر کر کے درمیان میں حرف ظرف یعنی ”قبل “ داخل کیا ہے اور پھر ظرف کو ہاء کنایہ کے ساتھ نہیں ملایا اور ہر وہ کلام جو اس شان کا ہو تو اس صورت میں ظرف مذکورہ اول کی صفت ہوتا ہے، لہذا شوہر کے کلام میں بھی ” قبل “ جو کہ ظرف ہے یہ اس واحدة‘ کی صفت ہوگی جو قبل سے پہلے ہے، جب معاملہ ایسا ہے تو پہلی طلاق سے عورت بائنہ ہو جائے گی اس لئے کہ یہ الهداية كتاب الطلاق، فصل في الطلاق قبل الدخول، ج ۲ ص ۳۸۸، ط: رحمانيه
Section on divorce before consummation: (26) When two things are mentioned and between them is inserted a particle of the adverb (such as 'before' or 'after'), if he connects it with the pronominal 'ha' (i.e., attaches it to a pronoun), then it qualifies the last mentioned; otherwise, it qualifies the first mentioned. Translation: When two things are mentioned and between them a particle of the adverb (e.g., 'before', 'after') is brought, then if the particle is connected with the pronominal 'ha' (e.g., 'qablahu' with a pronoun), it qualifies the sentence that is mentioned last; otherwise, it qualifies the sentence mentioned first. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that when two things are mentioned and between them a particle of the adverb (like 'qabl' or 'ba'd') is brought, it is seen whether the speaker has connected this adverb with the pronominal 'ha' or not, i.e., whether he has added it to a pronoun. If he connected it with the pronominal 'ha', the adverb qualifies the last mentioned, e.g., 'ja'ani Zaydun qablahu 'Amrun' – because the pronoun is attached to the adverb, so the adverb qualifies the later word, meaning 'Amr came to me before Zayd'. If the speaker did not connect the adverb with the pronominal 'ha', then the adverb qualifies the first mentioned, e.g., 'ja'ani Zaydun qabla 'Amrin' – because the adverb is not connected to a pronoun, so it qualifies the first sentence, meaning 'Zayd came to me before Amr'. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, informs that if the husband said to his wife 'anti taliqun wahidatan qabla wahidatin', then the first divorce alone makes the woman ba'in, and the second divorce does not occur, because the husband mentioned two things and inserted the particle of the adverb 'qabl' between them, and he did not connect the adverb to a pronominal 'ha'; any speech of such nature causes the adverb to qualify the first mentioned. Therefore, in the husband's speech, 'qabl' which is an adverb qualifies the 'wahidah' that is before 'qabl'. When such is the case, the woman becomes ba'in by the first divorce, because this is... (al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Talaq, Fasl fi al-Talaq Qabl al-Dukhul, vol. 2, p. 388, Rahmaniyyah ed.)
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hidayah on a grammatical rule affecting the number of divorces. The section is specifically about talaq before consummation (qabl al-dukhul). The author first states a general Arabic grammatical principle: when two things are mentioned with an adverb of time/place like 'qabl' (before) or 'ba'd' (after) between them, the adverb qualifies the last mentioned if it is attached to a pronoun (ha' al-kunaya), and the first mentioned if not attached. Then the commentary explains this with examples. The application to talaq: if a husband says 'anti taliqun wahidatan qabla wahidatin' (you are divorced one before one), the word 'qabl' is not attached to a pronoun, so it qualifies the first 'wahidah'. Thus, only the first divorce takes effect immediately, making the woman ba'in, and the second divorce does not occur because the first divorce already ended the marriage, so there is no basis for a second talaq. The ruling is specific to talaq qabl al-dukhul, where a single talaq is ba'in (irrevocable) according to the Hanafi school. This example shows how linguistic rules directly impact fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
حرف ظرف (harf zarf): A particle of the adverb, such as 'qabl' (before) or 'ba'd' (after), used to indicate time or place.
هاء کنایہ (ha' al-kunaya): The pronominal 'ha' (i.e., the attached pronoun) that is suffixed to the adverb, e.g., 'qablahu' (before him).
طلاق قبل الدخول (talaq qabl al-dukhul): Divorce pronounced before the marriage is consummated. In Hanafi fiqh, a single talaq in this case is ba'in (irrevocable), and no iddah is required except if the wife is pregnant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One divorce, ba'in
B) Two divorces, both valid
C) No divorce
D) One divorce, raj'i

Key Takeaway
The grammatical rule for 'qabl' in talaq statements determines that without a pronoun attachment, only the first divorce takes effect, ending the marriage.

Time Particle Rule in Talaq: 'One Before One'
Grammatical principle applied to talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi grammatical rule for time particles between two nouns, apply it to a talaq statement, and derive that only the first divorce takes effect when the particle is unconnected.

Core Idea
Unconnected time particle describes the first item: one divorce before another counts only one.

This passage establishes a precise grammatical-legal principle: when two things are separated by a time particle like 'before' or 'after', the particle's connection to a pronoun determines which item it qualifies. The author then applies this to divorce law, showing how a husband's utterance 'you are divorced one before one' results in only one effective divorce. This demonstrates how Hanafi fiqh relies on the rules of Arabic language to derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical rule
Read the first sentence: when two things are mentioned with a time particle between them, if the particle is connected to the pronoun 'ha' al-kinayah', it describes the second; otherwise, it describes the first.
This is a general linguistic rule, not specific to divorce.
2
Analyze the linguistic example
Look at the examples: 'Zayd came before Amr' (unconnected: describes Zayd) vs. 'Zayd came before him, Amr' (connected: describes Amr). Understand how the pronoun changes the reference.
The pronoun 'hu' attaches to the particle.
3
Apply to the talaq case
Take the divorce statement: 'anti taliqun wahidatan qabla wahidatin' (you are divorced one before one). The particle 'qabla' is between two nouns 'wahidatan' and 'wahidatin' with no pronoun, so it describes the first 'wahidatan'.
The first 'one' is the first divorce, described as occurring before the second.
4
Derive the legal effect
Since the first divorce is described as before the second, it occurs first. Because the wife is non-consummated, she becomes bainunah (irrevocably divorced) immediately upon the first divorce. Thus, the second divorce cannot occur because the marriage has already ended.
Bainunah means the husband cannot revoke; a new marriage contract is needed.
5
Confirm with the 'ba'daha' case
If he says 'ba'daha wahidah' (after it one), the particle is connected to the pronoun 'ha' (referring to the first), so it describes the second. The second is described as after, but the first still occurs first, leading to bainunah, so the second again does not occur.
This confirms consistency: only the first divorce takes effect regardless of the particle used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّهُ مَتَى ذُكِرَ شَيْئَيْنِ وَأُدْخِلَ بَيْنَهُمَا حَرْفَ الظَّرْفِ إِنْ قَرَنَهَا بِهَاءِ الْكِنَايَةِ كَانَ صِفَةً لِلْمَذْكُوُر آخِرًا وَإِلَّا أَوَّلا. وَالْأَصْلُ أَنَّ الظَّرْفَ مَتَى كَانَ بَيْنَ اسْمَيْنِ فَإِنْ لَمْ يُقْرَنُ بِهَاءِ الْكِتَابَةِ كَانَ صِفَةً لِلْأَوَّلِ تَقُولُ جَاءَنِي زَيْدٌ قَبْلَ عَمْرٍو فَالْقَبْلِيَّةُ فِيهَا صِفَةٌ لِزَيْدِ، وَإِنْ قَرَنَ بِهَاءِ الْكِنَايَةِ كَانَ صِفَةً لِلثَّانِي تَقُولُ جَاءَنِي زَيْدٌ قَبْلَهُ عَمْرُو فَإِذَا قَالَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَاحِدَةً قَبْلَ وَاحِدَةٍ فَقَدْ أَوْقَعَ الْأُولَى قَبْلَ الثَّانِيَةِ فَبَانَتْ بِهَا فَلَا تَقَعُ الثَّانِيَةُ وَلَوْ قَالَ بَعْدَهَا وَاحِدَةٌ فَكَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ وَصَفَ الثَّانِيَةَ بِالْبَعْدِيَّةِ وَلَوْ لَمْ يَصِفُهَا بِهِ لَمْ تَقَعُ فَهَذَا أَوْلَى.
Indeed, when two things are mentioned and between them is inserted a particle of time: if he connects it with the pronoun of allusion (ha' al-kinayah), it is a description of the last mentioned; otherwise, of the first. And the principle is that when the particle of time is between two nouns, if it is not connected with the pronoun of allusion, it is a description of the first. You say: 'Zayd came to me before Amr' – so the priority (of coming) is a description of Zayd. And if he connects it with the pronoun of allusion, it is a description of the second. You say: 'Zayd came to me – before him – Amr'. So when he says, 'You are divorced one before one,' he has effected the first before the second, so she becomes separated (ba'in) by it; thus the second does not occur. And if he said, 'After it, one,' it is similarly so, because he described the second as after; if he had not described it with that, it would not occur – so this is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from a Hanafi usul commentary. It first states a grammatical-legal principle: when two items are separated by a time particle (e.g., 'before' or 'after'), the particle's connection to the pronoun 'hu' (he) determines which item is qualified. If the particle is connected to the pronoun (e.g., 'qablahu'), it describes the latter; if not, it describes the former. The proof is demonstrated through linguistic examples. The passage then applies this to divorce: if a husband says to his wife 'anti taliqun wahidatan qabla wahidatin' (you are divorced one before one), without connecting the pronoun, the first divorce occurs first, and since she is non-consummated, she becomes irrevocably divorced (ba'in), so the second cannot occur. Similarly, if he says 'ba'daha wahidah' (after it one), the second is described as after, so again only the first occurs. This is a precise Hanafi ruling relying on linguistic usage.

Key Terms
حرف الظرف (harf al-zarf): A particle indicating time or place (e.g., before, after) that is used to connect two nouns.
هاء الكناية (ha' al-kinayah): The pronoun 'hu' (he/him) used as a referent; when attached to a particle, it indicates that the particle qualifies the second noun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both divorces occur; the wife becomes divorced after the second.
B) Only the first divorce occurs, and the wife becomes bainunah immediately.
C) Neither divorce occurs because the phrase is ambiguous.
D) The first divorce occurs, but the second is suspended until after the iddah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband says 'one divorce before another' without the connecting pronoun, only the first divorce occurs because the grammatical rule makes the time particle describe the first item.

The Husband's Word with an Oath in Kinayah Divorce
Stating the principle with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the husband's statement about his intention in kinayah divorce is accepted only when accompanied by an oath, and justify this using the hadith of Ibn Rukanah.

Core Idea
The husband is trustworthy for his inner intention, but must swear an oath to remove suspicion.

In Hanafi fiqh, a husband who uses ambiguous (kinayah) words for divorce may later claim he did not intend divorce. Since his inner intention is known only to him, his word is accepted—but only with an oath. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof from the Prophet's ﷺ practice with Ibn Rukanah, and its application to all kinayah expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression as kinayah
First, confirm that the husband used a word that is ambiguous for talaq, such as 'I'taddi' (observe the waiting period) or 'al-battah' (the irrevocable). These are not explicit divorce words.
Explicit words (sarih) like 'Anti taliq' do not require oath; divorce occurs immediately.
2
Note the husband's claim
The husband asserts that he did not intend divorce. Since intention is internal, the default is to accept his statement, but only if he takes an oath.
The oath is wajib; without it, his claim is not accepted and divorce takes effect.
3
Understand the legal basis: amin and yamin
The husband is 'amin' (trustworthy) regarding his innermost thoughts, but the oath is required to 'remove suspicion'. The oath is not because he is dishonest, but to formalise legal acceptance.
This principle is summarised as 'al-qawlu qawlu al-amini ma'a al-yamin'.
4
Analyse the dalil: Hadith of Ibn Rukanah
The Prophet ﷺ made Ibn Rukanah swear an oath when he uttered 'al-battah' and intended only one divorce. The phrase 'Do you not see' (a-lā tarā) introduces the proof.
The hadith is ahad but considered sufficient for this ruling.
5
Generalise to all preceding kinayah words
The ruling applies to all kinayah expressions listed earlier in the text (e.g., 'I'taddi', 'al-battah') – whenever the husband claims no divorce intent, he must swear.
If the expression is explicit (sarih), no oath is needed; divorce occurs regardless of intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْقَوْلَ قَوْلُ الْأَمِيْنِ مَعَ الْيَمِينِ. وَلَوْ قَالَ لَهَا اعْتَدِي وَقَالَ لَمْ أَنْوِ الطَّلَاقَ فَهِيَ امْرَأَتُهُ بَعْدَ أَنْ يَحْلِفَ، وَكَذَلِكَ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْمُتَقَدِّمَةِ إِذَا قَالَ لَمْ أَنْوِ الطَّلَاقَ فَعَلَيْهِ الْيَمِينُ لِأَنَّهُ أَمِينٌ فِيمَا يُخْبِرُ عَنْ ضَمِيرِهِ وَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْأَمِينِ مَعَ الْيَمِينِ، وَالْيَمِينُ لِنَفْيِ التُّهُمَةِ عَنْهُ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ حَلَّفَ ابْنَ رُكَانَةَ فِي لَفْظِ الْبَيَّةَ [؟] لَمَّا كَانَ الثَّلَاثُ مِنْ مُحْتَمَلَاتِ لَفْظِهِ.
Indeed, the statement is the statement of the trustworthy person with the oath. And if he said to her, 'I'taddi' (observe the waiting period), and he said, 'I did not intend divorce,' then she is his wife after he takes an oath. Similarly, in all the previous expressions, if he says, 'I did not intend divorce,' then the oath is incumbent upon him, because he is trustworthy in what he reports about his innermost thoughts, and the statement of the trustworthy person is accepted with the oath. The oath is to remove suspicion from him. Do you not see that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) made Ibn Rukanah take an oath regarding the word 'al-battah' [؟] because the three [divorces] were among the possibilities of his word?
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof establishing a key Hanafi rule: in cases of kinayah (ambiguous) divorce expressions, the husband's claim about his intention is accepted, but he must take an oath (yamin). The reasoning is that the husband is amin (trustworthy) regarding what is in his heart (damir), and the oath is required to remove suspicion. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Rukanah, who uttered the word 'al-battah' (which could imply divorce) and the Prophet (ﷺ) required him to swear an oath that he intended only one divorce, not three. The commentator applies this to the husband saying 'I'taddi' – if he claims he intended her to count her menstrual periods and not divorce, his statement is accepted with an oath, and no divorce occurs. This applies to all kinayah words.

Key Terms
أمين (amin): A trustworthy person; here, the husband is considered trustworthy regarding his inner intention in kinayah expressions.
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by the husband to confirm his claimed intention, required to remove suspicion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Rule of 'Before' and 'After' in Talaq Utterances
States a linguistic principle for interpreting 'before' and 'after' in talaq utterances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi grammatical rule for 'before' and 'after' in talaq utterances to determine which divorce takes effect first.

Core Idea
When 'before' or 'after' stands between two nouns without a pronoun, it describes the first; with a pronoun, it describes the second.

In Hanafi fiqh, the precise wording of a divorce (talaq) can determine which of two sequential divorces takes effect first. This lesson unpacks a key linguistic principle from Al-Hidayah: how the words 'qabla' (before) and 'ba'da' (after) function when placed between two nouns, and how the presence or absence of the pronominal suffix changes the meaning. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly interpreting common talaq utterances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two nouns and the zarf
In the talaq utterance, locate the two nouns (e.g., 'one [divorce]' and 'one [divorce]') and the temporal word (qabla/ba'da) between them.
The zarf is a word indicating time or location, here used temporally.
2
Check for the pronominal suffix (haa al-kinaayah)
Determine whether the zarf is attached to a pronominal suffix like 'hu' (e.g., qablahu vs. qabla). This decides whether the zarf attributes to the first or second noun.
The suffix is a single letter (هاء) attached to the end of the zarf.
3
Apply the rule: no suffix → describes first noun; with suffix → describes second
If no suffix, the temporal relationship (e.g., 'before-ness') qualifies the first noun, meaning the first divorce occurs first. If a suffix is present, it qualifies the second noun, reversing the order.
This ensures the speaker's intended sequence is understood.
4
Derive the talaq consequence
Once you know which divorce occurs first, apply the rule that after the first divorce (if it is a revocable divorce), the second may or may not take effect. In the examples given, the first divorce makes the wife irrevocably separate (ba'in), so the second does not occur.
The key is that the first divorce, once effective, prevents the second from being effective.

وَالْأَصْلُ أَنَّ الظَّرْفَ مَتَى كَانَ بَيْنَ اسْمَيْنِ فَإِنْ لَمْ يُقْرَنُ بِهَاءِ الْكِنَايَةِ كَانَ صِفَةً لِلْأَوَّلِ تَقُولُ جَاءَ نِي زَيْدٌ قَبْلَ عَمْرٍو فَالْقَبْلِيَّةُ فِيهَا صِفَةٌ لِزَيْدِ، وَإِنْ قَرَنَ بِهَاءِ الْكِنَايَةِ كَانَ صِفَةً لِلثَّانِي تَقُولُ جَاءَ نِي زَيْدٌ قَبْلَهُ عَمْرُو فَإِذَا قَالَ أَنْت طَالِقٌ وَاحِدَةً قَبْلَ وَاحِدَةٍ فَقَدْ أَوْقَعَ الْأُولَى قَبْلَ الثَّانِيَةِ فَبَانَتْ بِهَا فَلَا تَقَعُ الثَّانِيَةُ وَلَوْ قَالَ بَعْدَهَا وَاحِدَةٌ فَكَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ وَصَفَ الثَّانِيَةَ بِالْبَعْدِيَّةِ وَلَوْ لَمْ يَصِفُهَا بِهِ لَمْ تَقَعُ فَهَذَا أَوْلَى.
البحر الرائق كتاب الطلاق، فصل فى إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان، ج۳ ص۳۱۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
And the principle is that when the word of time/location (zarf) occurs between two nouns, if it is not connected with a pronominal suffix (haa al-kinaayah), it describes the first noun. You say: 'Zayd came before Amr' – the 'before-ness' is a description of Zayd. If it is connected with a pronominal suffix, it describes the second noun. You say: 'Zayd came before him, Amr' – so if he says 'you are divorced one [divorce] before one [divorce]', he has effected the first [divorce] before the second, so she becomes irrevocably separate by it, and the second does not occur. And if he says 'after it one [divorce]', it is similar, because he described the second with 'after-ness', and if he had not described it with it, it would not occur. So this is more appropriate. Al-Bahr al-Raiq, Book of Divorce, Section on Adding Divorce to Time, vol. 3, p. 317, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami ed.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is a definition ruling: it provides a grammatical-legal rule for interpreting the words 'before' (qabla) and 'after' (ba'da) in divorce utterances. The principle: when a time/location word (zarf) appears between two nouns, if it has no pronoun suffix (like 'hu'), it describes the first noun; if it has a pronoun suffix, it describes the second noun. The example: 'Zayd came before Amr' – 'before' describes Zayd. 'Zayd came before him, Amr' – 'before' describes Amr. Applied to talaq: if a husband says 'you are divorced one before one', the first divorce occurs before the second, so the wife becomes irrevocably separate (ba'in) after the first, and the second does not occur. Similarly, if he says 'after it one', it means the second divorce occurs after the first (and the first is implied), but since she is already ba'in, the second is ineffective. This rule ensures clarity in sequential talaq utterances.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): A word indicating time or location, here used as a temporal preposition ('before' or 'after').
هاء الكناية (hā' al-kīnāyah): The pronominal suffix (e.g., 'hu' in 'qablahu') that connects the zarf to a following noun.
صفة (sifah): A descriptive attribute; here the zarf attributes a temporal relationship to the preceding or following noun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the utterance 'you are divorced one after it one' (with suffix on 'after'), which divorce occurs first?
A) The first divorce occurs first.
B) The second divorce occurs first.
C) Both occur simultaneously.
D) Neither occurs because the utterance is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of the pronominal suffix on 'before' or 'after' in a talaq utterance determines which divorce is described by the temporal relationship, thereby establishing the sequence of divorces.

Tamlikat Require Acceptance in the Same Sitting
Establishes the rule that tamlik requires acceptance in the same sitting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that tamlik (delegation of ownership) requires acceptance in the same sitting and apply it to find when a delegated divorce takes effect.

Core Idea
Tamlikat require immediate acceptance; the option lapses when the sitting ends.

Delegating a right — like giving someone the power to divorce themselves — is not an open-ended offer. The Hanafi school holds that such delegations (tamlikat) demand a response in the very same sitting. This lesson explores the proof: consensus of the Companions and analogy to sale and gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Locate the overarching rule: all tamlikat require a response in the same sitting (majlis).
The term 'tamlikat' includes sale, gift, and here, delegation of divorce.
2
Apply to Delegated Divorce
Understand that when the husband says 'Ikh tārī' (choose), he makes his wife the owner of the divorce decision. Her right to accept or reject is confined to the sitting.
If she delays, the option ends when the sitting physically ends.
3
Examine the Proof
Two proofs: (1) Consensus of the Companions that a woman given choice has only the sitting, and (2) analogy (qiyas) to sale and gift, where acceptance must occur in the same sitting.
The illa is that both are tamlikat requiring a response in the same sitting.
4
Contrast with Other Views
Note that some schools may grant wider latitude, but the Hanafi mufta bihi is the sitting restriction.
This restriction is for tamlik specifically, not for other forms of delegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ جملیکات مجلس میں ہی جواب کا تقاضا کرتی ہیں۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جتنی بھی تملیر کات ہیں یعنی ایک آدمی دوسرے کو کسی چیز کا مالک بنادے تو یہ مجلس میں جواب کا تقاضا کرتی ہیں، یعنی جب تک مجلس قائم ہے اس وقت تک اس کو قبول کرنے اور رد کرنے کا اختیار ہے لیکن جب مجلس بدل گئی تو اختیار ختم ہو جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہا کہ اخْتَارِی" کہ تجھے اختیار ہے، اب عورت جب تک مجلس میں ہو تو اس کو طلاق دینے اور ر دکر نے کا اختیار ہو گا لیکن جب مجلس بدل جائے تو اس کا اختیار ختم ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ یہ از قبیل تملیکات ہے یعنی شوہر بیوی کو طلاق کا مالک بنارہا ہے اور جتنی بھی تملیکات ہیں وہ مجلس میں جواب کی متقاضی اور منتظر ہوتی ہیں، جس طرح بیج میں جس مجلس میں ایجاب ہو، اتمام بیع کے لئے اسی مجلس میں قبول کرنا شرط ہے، کیونکہ مجلس کی تمام ساعتیں ساعت واحدہ کے درجے میں ہے، لہذا اختتام مجلس سے پہلے پہلے جب بھی عورت اپنے آپ کو طلاق دے گی اس پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔
الهداية : كتاب الطلاق، باب تقويض الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۱، ط: رحمانیه
وَلَوْ قَالَ لَهَا : اخْتَارِى يَنْوِى الطَّلَاقَ فَلَهَا أَنْ تُطَلَّقَ نَفْسَهَا فِي مَجْلِسِ عِلْمِهَا فَإِنْ كَانَتْ حَاضِرَةً فَبِسَمَاعِهَا، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَائِبَةٍ فَبِالْإِخْبَارِ لأَنَّ الْمُخَيَّرَةَ لَهَا الْمَجْلِسُ بِإِجْمَاعِ الصَّحَابَةِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمْ، وَلَأنَّهُ مَلَكَهَا فِعْلَ الاخْتِيَارِ ، وَالتَّمْلِيكَاتُ تَقْتَضِي جَوَابًا فِي الْمَجْلِسِ كَالْبَيْعِ وَالْهَبَةِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا.
الاختيار لتعليـل الـمـخـتـار : كتاب الطلاق، كنايات الطلاق، ج ۳ ص ۱۳۳، ط: مطبعة المجلس القاهرة
28. Indeed, tamlikat (delegations of ownership) require a response in the same sitting. Translation: Tamlikat demand a response in the sitting. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that whatever tamlikat are, meaning one person makes another owner of something, they demand a response in the sitting, meaning as long as the sitting continues, there is an option to accept or reject, but when the sitting changes, the option ends. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if the husband said to his wife 'Ikh tārī' (choose), meaning you have a choice, then as long as the woman is in the sitting, she has the option to give divorce or to reject, but when the sitting changes, her option ends. Because this is of the category of tamlikat, i.e., the husband is making the wife the owner of divorce, and all tamlikat require and await a response in the sitting, just as in a sale, in whichever sitting the offer is made, acceptance is a condition in that same sitting for completion of the sale, because all moments of the sitting are considered as a single moment. Therefore, whenever the woman divorces herself before the end of the sitting, divorce will occur. Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter on Delegation of Divorce, vol. 2, p. 391, Rahmaniyyah ed. And if he says to her 'Ikh tārī' intending divorce, then she may divorce herself in her sitting of knowledge; if she is present, then by hearing it, and if she is absent, then by notification, because the one given a choice has the sitting by consensus of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them), and because he made her the owner of the act of choice, and tamlikat require a response in the sitting like sale and gift and similar. Al-Ikhtiyar li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Book of Divorce, Kinaya of Divorce, vol. 3, p. 133, Matba'at al-Majlis al-Qahira ed.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The principle states that all tamlikat (delegations of ownership) require a response within the same sitting. The Urdu commentary explains that if a husband says 'اخْتَارِي' (choose), the wife has the option to divorce herself only within that sitting; if the sitting ends, the option lapses. This is because tamlikat, like sales and gifts, require acceptance in the same meeting. The Arabic quotes from Al-Hidayah and Al-Ikhtiyar provide the proof: the wife's right to divorce herself in the sitting is established by the consensus of the Companions, and the analogy (qiyas) to sale and gift confirms that tamlikat require a response in the same sitting. The Hanafi position is that the wife's exercise of the delegated power is limited to the sitting in which the offer was made, unless specific conditions state otherwise.

Key Terms
تملیک (tamlīk): Giving ownership or delegation of a right (here, the right to divorce) to another person.
مجلس (majlis): The sitting or meeting in which the offer is made; the time frame within which acceptance must occur.
اختیار (ikhtiyār): Choice; here it refers to the wife's option to divorce herself when given the choice by the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tamlikat, including delegated divorce, require acceptance in the same sitting; otherwise the offer lapses.

The Ambiguous Cannot Explain the Ambiguous
States a maxim: the ambiguous cannot explain the ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that an ambiguous term cannot explain another ambiguous term and apply it to a talaq scenario.

Core Idea
An unclear word cannot be interpreted by another unclear word—only clear expressions clarify.

This lesson presents a fundamental maxim from the Hanafi school regarding the interpretation of ambiguous divorce utterances. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly applying the rules of talaq when the husband uses vague language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its textual source
Locate the exact wording of the maxim in Arabic and note its source (Al-Hidayah, Book of Divorce).
The maxim is: إِنَّ الْمُبْهَم لَا يَصْلُحُ تَفْسِيرًا لِلْمُبْهَم.
2
Define 'mubham' in the context of talaq
Mubham refers to an expression that lacks a single definite meaning, such as 'something' or 'a thing'.
In talaq, mubham utterances do not create a binding divorce unless clarified clearly.
3
Apply the maxim to a practical scenario
If a husband says 'You are divorced with something' and later claims 'I meant [another ambiguous term]', the second statement cannot serve as a valid tafsir because it is also mubham.
The original ambiguity remains, so no divorce is effective.
4
Contrast with valid clarification
Valid tafsir must use a clear, unambiguous term. For example, 'I meant three divorces' (if 'three' is clear) would be accepted.
Clarity is required for legal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْمُبْهَم لَا يَصْلُحُ تَفْسِيرًا لِلْمُبُهم . .
الهداية : كتاب الطلاق، باب تقويض الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۱، ط: رحمانیه
Indeed, the ambiguous is not suitable as an explanation for the ambiguous. Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter on Delegation of Divorce, vol. 2, p. 391, Rahmaniyyah ed.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This is a ruling statement (a legal maxim). It states that an ambiguous (mubham) expression cannot serve as an explanation for another ambiguous expression. In the context of talaq, this means that if the husband uses an ambiguous word for divorce, his later ambiguous statement cannot clarify it. For example, if he says 'I divorced you with something' without specifying, and then says 'I meant by that something else ambiguous', it will not be accepted as a valid interpretation. The principle is cited in Al-Hidayah in the section on delegation of divorce, emphasizing that clarity is required when interpreting ambiguous divorce utterances.

Key Terms
مبهم (mubham): An ambiguous or unclear term that does not have a single definite meaning.
تفسير (tafsīr): An explanation or interpretation that clarifies the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The clarification is not accepted because it is also ambiguous.
C) The husband must take an oath to clarify.
D) The wife chooses the interpretation.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous divorce term cannot be clarified by another ambiguous term; only a clear expression suffices.
