6 min
An Ambiguous Word Cannot Interpret Another Ambiguous Word
Stating the principle that mubham cannot interpret mubham and its application in ikhtiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that an ambiguous (mubham) word cannot interpret another ambiguous word and apply it to determine the validity of talaq delegation.

Core Idea
In talaq delegation, ambiguous words from both sides fail to effect divorce—clarity requires specification of the self (nafs).

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a critical usul al-fiqh principle applied to the chapter of talaq: an ambiguous expression cannot clarify another ambiguous expression. The context is the delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq) where the husband says 'choose' (ikhtari) without specifying what. Understanding this principle helps you determine when such delegations are valid and avoid common mistakes in issuing fatwas on ambiguous divorce scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the husband's delegation of choice using the word 'ikhtari' and the wife's response 'ikhtartu'. The ruling is that no divorce occurs (batil for talaq).
The act is a delegation (tafwid), not a direct pronouncement.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahaba) as proof that mention of 'nafs' or its indicator is required.
Ijma' is a primary source; here it establishes the need for specification.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The consensus proves that without specifying 'nafs', the delegation is ambiguous, and since one ambiguous word cannot interpret another, the statements are legally ineffective.
The principle 'al-mubham la yasluhu tafsiran li al-mubham' is applied.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school generally holds that 'ikhtari' is kinayah for divorce, so no specification is needed. But the Hanafi position requires 'nafs' or equivalent.
This difference affects practical outcomes.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that without explicit mention of 'nafs', no separation occurs. This is established in Zahir al-Riwayah and upheld by later authorities.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah explicitly states this rule.

اصول ہدایہ جلد ثانی

ترجمہ مہم مہم کے لئے تفسیر بنے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا۔

تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ایک مبہم لفظ دوسرے مبہم لفظ کے لئے تفسیر نہیں بن سکتا، کیونکہ جب اس میں خود ابہام ہے تو یہ دوسرے مہم کے ابہام کو کس طرح دور کرے گا، بلکہ مہم
کے لئے مفسر وہی بننے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے جس کے اندرا بہام نہ ہو، اور وہ اس کی تفسیر کرے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اختیار کے باب میں یعنی جب شوہر اختاری کے لفظ سے بیوی کو طلاق کا اختیار دے تو کلام میں لفظ نفس کا ذکر کرنا ضروری ہے، اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا کہ ”اختاری اور جواب میں بیوی نے کہا ” اخترت تو کلام لغو ہوگا ، اس لئے کہ شوہر کا کلام ” اختاری مبہم ہے اور بیوی کا کلام اخترت “ بھی مہم ہے اور مہم مہم کے لئے مفسر بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا، لہذا عورت کا کلام بھی شوہر کے کلام کے لئے تفسیر بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھے گا۔ جانین سے نفس کی صراحت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی ، اس لئے کہ معلوم نہیں کہ کس چیز کا اختیار سپر دکیا ہے۔ (٧٠) إِنَّ مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الْإِعْرَاضِ فَهُوَ يُبْطِلُ الْخِيَارَ وَمَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الْإِقْبَالِ فَهُوَ
لا يُبْطِلُ .
وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ ذِكْرِ النَّفْسِ أَوْ مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَيْهِ فِي كَلَامِهِ أَوْ كَلَامِهَا) مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ: اخْتَارِي نَفْسَكِ فَتَقُولُ: اخْتَرْتُ ، أَوْ يَقُولُ لَهَا: اخْتَارِى فَتَقُولُ: اخْتَرْتُ نَفْسِي لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ عُرِفَ بِإِجْمَاعِ الصَّحَابَةِ، وَأَنَّهُ الْمُفَسَّرُ مِنْ أَحَدِ الْجَانِبَيْنِ، وَلَانَّ الْمُبْهَمَ لَا يَصْلُحُ تَفْسِيرًا لِلْمُبْهَمِ، حَتَّى لَوْ قَالَ لَهَا : اخْتَارِي، فَقَالَتِ اخْتَرْتُ فَلَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ ، لأنَّ الِاخْتِيَارَ لَيْسَ مِنْ أَلْفَاظِ الطَّلَاقِ وَضْعًا، وَإِنَّمَا جُعِلَ بِالسُّنَّةِ فِيمَا إِذَا كَانَ مُفَسَّرًا، فَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذَلِكَ لَا يَقَعُ بِهِ شَيْءٌ، وَلَأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ اخْتَارِي، وَقَوْلَهَا اخْتَرْتُ، لَيْسَ لَهُ مُخَصِّصٌ بِهَا فَلا يَقَعُ الطَّلَاقُ، فَإِذَا ذُكِرَتِ النَّفْسُ مَخَصَّصَ الاخْتِيَارُ لَهَا فَيَقَعُ.
الاختيار لتعليل المختار: كتاب الطلاق، كنايات الطلاق، ج ۳ ص ۱۳۴، ط: مطبعة المجلس القاهرة
الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب تقويض الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۴، ط: رحمانیه
Page 82 of Usool-e-Hidayah Volume 2. Translation: An ambiguous word does not have the capacity to become an interpretation for another ambiguous word. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that one ambiguous word cannot serve as an interpretation for another ambiguous word, because when it itself has ambiguity, how will it remove the ambiguity of another ambiguous word? Rather, only that which has no ambiguity has the capacity to become an interpreter for the ambiguous, and it is that which interprets it. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that in the chapter of ikhtiyar (delegation of choice), i.e., when a husband gives his wife the option of divorce with the word ikhtiyari, it is necessary to mention the word 'nafs' (self) in the speech. If the husband says to his wife 'ikhtari' and the wife replies 'ikhtartu', the speech will be void because the husband's statement 'ikhtari' is ambiguous and the wife's statement 'ikhtartu' is also ambiguous, and an ambiguous thing does not have the capacity to be an interpreter for another ambiguous thing. Therefore, the wife's speech will not serve as an interpretation of the husband's speech. Because there is no explicit mention of 'nafs' from either side, divorce will not occur, since it is not known what option was delegated. (70) Indeed, that which indicates turning away nullifies the option, and that which indicates turning towards does not nullify it. And it is necessary to mention the self or what indicates it in his speech or her speech, such as if he says: 'Choose yourself (nafsaki)' and she says: 'I choose', or he says to her: 'Choose (ikhtari)' and she says: 'I choose myself (nafsi)', because that is known by the consensus of the Companions, and because it is the interpretation from one of the two sides, and because the ambiguous does not serve as an interpretation for the ambiguous. So if he says to her: 'Choose (ikhtari)' and she says 'I choose', it is nothing, because ikhtiyar is not from the words of divorce by linguistic convention, but rather it was made [effective] by the Sunnah only when it is interpreted. So when it is not like that, nothing occurs by it. And because his statement 'ikhtari' and her statement 'ikhtartu' have no specification for her, so divorce does not occur. So when the self (nafs) is mentioned, the choice is specified for her, so divorce occurs. References: Al-Ikhtiyar Li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar, Kitab al-Talaq, Kinayat al-Talaq, Vol. 3, p. 134, Matba'at al-Majlis al-Qahira; Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab Taqwid al-Talaq, Vol. 2, p. 394, Matba'a al-Rahmaniyya.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school, specifically from Al-Hidayah. It establishes a fundamental principle of usul al-fiqh applied to the chapter of talaq: an ambiguous (mubham) expression cannot clarify another ambiguous expression. The context is the delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq) through the word 'ikhtiyar' (choice). The author explains that for the delegation to be valid, the husband must specify what the wife is choosing. If he merely says 'ikhtari' (choose), the statement is ambiguous because it could refer to anything. If the wife then responds 'ikhtartu' (I choose), her response is equally ambiguous. Since neither clarifies the matter, no divorce takes effect. The required specification is the word 'nafs' (self), following the practice established by the consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahaba). The passage also mentions the related rule about actions indicating turning away or turning towards, but that is elaborated in the next lesson.

Key Terms
مُبْهَم (mubham): An ambiguous word or expression whose meaning is not clear without context; here specifically referring to the husband's statement 'ikhtari' (choose) without specifying what is being chosen.
مُفَسَّر (mufassar): An expression that has been clarified or interpreted, either by explicit wording or by context; here the statement 'ikhtari nafsaki' is mufassar because it specifies the self.
إِجْمَاع الصَّحَابَة (ijma' al-sahaba): The consensus of the Companions of the Prophet, used as a proof that the mention of 'nafs' is necessary for delegation of talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does no divorce occur when a husband says 'ikhtari' and the wife says 'ikhtartu' without mentioning 'nafs'?
A) Because the word 'ikhtari' is not a word of divorce at all.
B) Because the husband's statement 'ikhtari' is ambiguous and the wife's 'ikhtartu' is also ambiguous, and one ambiguous word cannot interpret another.
C) Because ijma' says that divorce only occurs with the word 'talaq'.
D) Because the wife must say 'talaq' explicitly.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous word cannot interpret another ambiguous word; thus, in talaq delegation, the wife's ambiguous acceptance does not validate an ambiguous offer.

How a Wife's Actions Affect Her Option After Delegation of Talaq
Ruling with proof on iqbal and i'rad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a wife's actions after delegation of talaq as iqbal or i'rad and state whether the option remains valid.

Core Idea
The wife's option of divorce remains valid only if she acts with iqbal; i'rad nullifies it.

When a husband delegates the option of divorce to his wife, her subsequent actions determine whether the option continues. The Hanafi school distinguishes between iqbal—actions showing serious consideration—and i'rad—actions indicating disinterest. This lesson explains that distinction with clear examples and a proof from the Prophet's advice to Aisha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of actions
Understand the definitions of iqbal (turning towards) and i'rad (turning away). The ruling hinges on which category the wife's action falls into.
Iqbal indicates serious deliberation; i'rad shows disinterest or distraction.
2
Apply the categories to specific examples
Given a wife's action after delegation (e.g., sitting down, eating, calling witnesses), classify it as iqbal or i'rad using the criteria from the text.
Refer to the list of examples in the passage.
3
Examine the proof from the Sunnah
The Prophet (ﷺ) advised Aisha to consult her parents before responding to a marriage proposal. This shows that seeking counsel is a sign of iqbal, preserving the option.
The proof is analogical: consultation indicates engagement in decision-making.
4
Determine the outcome for the option
Based on the classification, conclude whether the option remains (if iqbal) or is nullified (if i'rad). If the wife later exercises the option after i'rad, the divorce does not occur.
The option is voided at the moment of i'rad; subsequent acceptance is ineffective.

Source Text

اصول ہدایہ جلد ثانی
۸۳

ترجمہ: جو چیز اعراض پر دلالت کرتی ہے وہ اختیار کو باطل کر دیتی ہے اور جو متوجہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتی ہے وہ اختیار کو باطل نہیں کرتی ۔

تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب ایک آدمی دوسرے کو کسی چیز کا اختیار دیتا ہے تو اب مخیر کی طرف سے اگر ایسا فعل پایا جائے جو اعراض پر دلالت کرتا ہوتو اس سے اختیار باطل ہو جائے گا، مثلا مجلس سے کھڑا ہونا اور اگر ایسا فعل پایا جائے جو مخیر کے متوجہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتا ہو تو اس سے اختیار باطل نہیں ہوگا، مثلا مخیر کھڑا تھا پھر بیٹھ گیا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا طلقی نَفْسَكِ یعنی طلاق کا اختیار بیوی کے سپر د کر دیا جبکہ بیوی کھڑی تھی پھر بیٹھ گئی یا سواری پر تھی کہ اُسے روک لیا تو اس صورت میں اختیار باقی رہے گا، کیونکہ اُن دونوں صورتوں میں عورت سے ایسا فعل صادر ہوا کہ وہ اقبال یعنی متوجہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتا ہے اور ہر وہ فعل جو متوجہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتا ہو وہ اختیار کو باطل نہیں کرتا۔ اور اگر عورت سے ایسا فعل صادر ہوا جو اعراض پر دلالت کرتا ہے تو اختیار کو باطل ہو جائے گا، جس طرح عورت کا مجلس سے اُٹھ کر چلا جانا، یا کسی اور کام میں مشغول ہو جانا یہ اعراض پر دلالت کرتا ہے، جیسے غسل کرنا ، خضاب لگانا، کھانا کھانا ،شوہر سے ازدواجی تعلق قائم کرنا ، اس سے اختیار ختم ہو جائے گا۔
وَكَذَا لَوْ كَانَتْ قَائِمَةً فَقَعَدَتْ ، لأنَّهُ دَلَالَةُ الْإِقْبَالِ إذْ الْقُعُودُ أَجْمَعُ لِلرَّأْيِ لِأَنَّهُ سَبَبُ الاسْتِرَاحَةِ، وَكَذَا لَوْ كَانَتْ قَاعِدَةً فَاتَّكَاتْ أَوْ كَانَتْ مُتَّكِتَةً فَاسْتَوَتْ قَاعِدَةً، لأنَّهُ دَلَالَةُ الْجدّ فِي التَّأَقُلِ كَمَا إِذَا كَانَتْ مُحْتَبِيَةً فَتَرَبَّعَتْ وَكَذَا إِذَا ادَّعَتْ أَبَاهَا لِلْمَشُورَةِ أَوْ شُهُودًا لِلْإِشْهَادِ لَا يَبْطُلُ، لِأَنَّ الِاسْتِشَارَةَ لِتَحَرِّى الصَّوَابِ، وَلِهَذَا أَمَرَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عَائِشَةَ بِمُشَاوَرَةِ وَالِدَيْهَا قَبْلَ أَنْ تُجِيبَهُ وَالْإِشْهَادُ لِلتَّحَرُّزِ عَنْ الْجُحُودِ فَصَارَ دَلِيلَ الْإِقْبَالِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا ادَّعَتْ بِطَعَامٍ فَأَكَلَتْ أَوْ قَامَتْ أَوْ اغْتَسَلَتْ أَوْ امْتَشَطَتْ أَوْ اخْتَضَبَتْ أَوْ جَامَعَهَا زَوْجُهَا حَيْثُ يَبْطُلُ خِيَارُهَا لِاشْتِغَالِهَا بِعَمَلِ آخَرَ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ فَيَكُونُ إِعْرَاضًا عَنْ تِلْكَ الْجِهَةِ.
تبيين الحقائق: كتاب الطلاق فصل فى الأمر باليد، ج ۲ ص ۲۳۴، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
Usool-e-Hidayah Volume 2, page 83. Translation: That which indicates turning away (i'rad) nullifies the option (ikhtiyar), and that which indicates turning towards (iqbal) does not nullify it. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that when one person gives another the option of something, then if from the person given the option an act occurs that indicates turning away, the option will be nullified, for example, standing up from the gathering. And if an act occurs that indicates the person given the option is turning towards, then the option is not nullified, for example, the person was standing and then sat down. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the husband said to his wife 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself), i.e., he delegated the option of divorce to her, and she was standing and then sat down, or she was on a ride and stopped it, then in both cases the option remains, because in both situations an act occurred from the woman that indicates turning towards, and every act that indicates turning towards does not nullify the option. And if from the woman an act occurs that indicates turning away, then the option will be nullified, such as the woman getting up from the gathering and leaving, or engaging in some other work; that indicates turning away, like taking a bath, applying henna, eating food, having marital intercourse with her husband. From that, the option ends. And similarly, if she was standing and sat down, because that is an indication of turning towards, since sitting is more conducive to reflection, as it is a cause of relaxation. And similarly, if she was sitting and leaned, or was leaning and sat upright, because that is an indication of seriousness in deliberation, like if she was sitting on her heels and then sat cross-legged. And similarly, if she summoned her father for consultation or witnesses for testimony, the option does not become void, because seeking counsel is for finding the correct course, and for that reason the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered Aisha to consult her parents before answering him. And taking witnesses is for safeguarding against denial, so it becomes evidence of turning towards. As opposed to if she called for food and ate, or stood up, or took a bath, or combed her hair, or applied henna, or her husband had intercourse with her, in which case her option is nullified because she engaged in another unnecessary action, which is turning away from that matter. Reference: Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Talaq, Fasl fi al-Amr bi al-Yad, Vol. 2, p. 234, al-Matba'a al-Kubra al-Amiriyya.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and commentary from the Hanafi school regarding the effect of the wife's actions after being given the option of divorce by her husband. The principle, based on iqbal (turning towards) and i'rad (turning away), determines whether the option remains valid. The author of Al-Hidayah explains that actions indicating the wife's seriousness and engagement with the decision (like sitting down, seeking counsel, calling witnesses) preserve the option, as they show she is actively considering the matter. Conversely, actions that indicate disinterest or distraction (like standing up to leave, bathing, eating, or having intercourse) nullify the option, as they show she has moved on. The proof includes a reference to the Prophet (ﷺ) advising Aisha to consult her parents, indicating that seeking counsel is a sign of iqbal. The ruling is derived from the understanding that the wife must exercise the option only while she is still in a state of considering it.

Key Terms
إِعْرَاض (i'rad): Turning away or disinterest; actions indicating the wife is not seriously considering the option.
إِقْبَال (iqbal): Turning towards; actions showing serious deliberation, preserving the option.
الْخِيَار (al-khiyar): The option given to the wife to divorce herself; its validity depends on her subsequent actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife is given the option of divorce by her husband. She then calls for food and eats. What happens to her option?
A) The option remains valid because she is nourishing herself.
B) The option is nullified because eating indicates i'rad.
C) The option is nullified only if she eats with others.
D) The option remains valid if she says 'bismillah' before eating.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wife's option of divorce is voided the moment she performs an action of i'rad, even if she later intends to exercise it.

Effect of Wife's Actions on Delegated Option of Divorce
Ruling statement with examples on wife's actions after tafwid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actions indicating attention (iqbal) and those indicating aversion (i'rad) in the context of delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq), and state the consequence for the validity of the option.

Core Idea
Actions showing disinterest nullify the delegated divorce option; actions showing engagement keep it valid.

When a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife (tafwid al-talaq), her subsequent actions determine whether the option remains valid. This lesson from the Hidayah establishes a key principle: actions indicating iqbal (attention) preserve the option, while actions indicating i'rad (aversion) nullify it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the rules of delegation in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the wife's behavior after being given the option (ikhtiyar). The ruling: actions of iqbal keep the option valid; actions of i'rad nullify it.
2
Classify actions as iqbal or i'rad
From the examples: iqbal includes sitting after standing, stopping a ride, leaning back, sitting upright, calling father for consultation, calling witnesses. I'rad includes leaving the session, bathing, dyeing hair, eating, intercourse.
The key is whether the action shows engagement with the option or turning away.
3
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Given a hypothetical action, determine its category by considering whether it reflects attention to the option or disinterest. For example, if she starts praying, does that show attention or aversion? (Prayer is an act of worship, but it may be considered i'rad if it is unrelated to the option; the source mentions similar acts like bathing as i'rad.)
4
Understand the rationale
Actions like sitting are deemed iqbal because they facilitate reflection (al-qa'du ajma'u li al-ra'y). Actions like bathing are i'rad because they engage the wife in an unnecessary activity, showing disinterest in the option.
The rationale helps in extrapolating to unlisted actions.

ترجمہ: جو چیز اعراض پر دلالت کرتی ہے وہ اختیار کو باطل کر دیتی ہے اور جو متوجہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتی ہے وہ اختیار کو باطل نہیں کرتی ۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب ایک آدمی دوسرے کو کسی چیز کا اختیار دیتا ہے تو اب مخیر کی طرف سے اگر ایسا فعل پایا جائے جو اعراض پر دلالت کرتا ہوتو اس سے اختیار باطل ہو جائے گا، مثلا مجلس سے کھڑا ہونا اور اگر ایسا فعل پایا جائے جو مخیر کے متوجہ ہونے پر دلالت کرتا ہو تو اس سے اختیار باطل نہیں ہوگا، مثلا مخیر کھڑا تھا پھر بیٹھ گیا۔

وَكَذَا لَوْ كَانَتْ قَائِمَةً فَقَعَدَتْ ، لأنَّهُ دَلَالَةُ الْإِقْبَالِ إذْ الْقُعُودُ أَجْمَعُ لِلرَّأْيِ لِأَنَّهُ سَبَبُ الاسْتِرَاحَةِ، وَكَذَا لَوْ كَانَتْ قَاعِدَةً فَاتَّكَاتْ أَوْ كَانَتْ مُتَّكِتَةً فَاسْتَوَتْ قَاعِدَةً، لأنَّهُ دَلَالَةُ الْجدّ فِي التَّأَقُلِ كَمَا إِذَا كَانَتْ مُحْتَبِيَةً فَتَرَبَّعَتْ وَكَذَا إِذَا ادَّعَتْ أَبَاهَا لِلْمَشُورَةِ أَوْ شُهُودًا لِلْإِشْهَادِ لَا يَبْطُلُ، لِأَنَّ الِاسْتِشَارَةَ لِتَحَرِّى الصَّوَابِ، وَلِهَذَا أَمَرَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عَائِشَةَ بِمُشَاوَرَةِ وَالِدَيْهَا قَبْلَ أَنْ تُجِيبَهُ وَالْإِشْهَادُ لِلتَّحَرُّزِ عَنْ الْجُحُودِ فَصَارَ دَلِيلَ الْإِقْبَالِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا ادَّعَتْ بِطَعَامٍ فَأَكَلَتْ أَوْ قَامَتْ أَوْ اغْتَسَلَتْ أَوْ امْتَشَطَتْ أَوْ اخْتَضَبَتْ أَوْ جَامَعَهَا زَوْجُهَا حَيْثُ يَبْطُلُ خِيَارُهَا لِاشْتِغَالِهَا بِعَمَلِ آخَرَ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ فَيَكُونُ إِعْرَاضًا عَنْ تِلْكَ الْجِهَةِ.
تبيين الحقائق: كتاب الطلاق فصل فى الأمر باليد، ج ۲ ص ۲۳۴، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
Translation: Whatever indicates aversion invalidates the option, and whatever indicates attention does not invalidate the option. Explanation: The meaning of the aforesaid principle is that when a person gives another an option over something, if an action appears from the one given the option that indicates aversion, the option becomes invalid, e.g., standing up from the session; and if an action appears that indicates attention of the one given the option, the option does not become invalid, e.g., the person was standing then sat down. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the husband says to his wife 'Talqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) while she was standing, then she sat down or was on a ride and stopped it, the option remains valid because in both cases her action indicates attention (iqbal), and every action that indicates attention does not invalidate the option. If she performs an action indicating aversion, the option becomes invalid, such as leaving the session, engaging in another task like bathing, dyeing, eating, sexual intercourse with the husband. Similarly, if she was standing and sat down, because that indicates attention since sitting is more conducive to reflection and rest; likewise if she was sitting and leaned back or was leaning and sat upright, because that indicates seriousness in pondering, like if she was sitting with knees drawn and then sat cross-legged. Also if she calls her father for consultation or witnesses for testimony, the option does not become invalid because consultation is to seek correct opinion (and the Prophet commanded Aisha to consult her parents before answering), and testimony/proof is to guard against denial, so it indicates attention. In contrast, if she calls for food and eats, or stands up, bathes, combs her hair, dyes, or her husband has intercourse with her, her option becomes invalid because she engages in another unnecessary action, which is aversion from that matter.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the effect of the wife's actions after being delegated the option of divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The principle is that actions indicating i'rad (turning away, aversion) nullify the option, while actions indicating iqbal (attention, engagement) preserve it. The commentator explains the principle with examples from the Hidayah: if the wife sits after standing, stops her ride, leans back or sits upright, calls her father for consultation or witnesses, these show attention and the option remains. Actions like leaving the session, bathing, dyeing hair, eating, or intercourse show aversion and nullify the option. The Arabic quote from Tabyin al-Haqaiq provides further details, including the rationale that sitting is more restful and conducive to reflection, and consultation or seeking witnesses indicates seriousness. The passage also notes that if the wife says 'I have separated myself' (abantu nafsi), the divorce occurs because the word 'abantu' is a word of divorce, but the added description of ba'inunah is disregarded as it does not match the delegation. If she says 'I have chosen myself' (ikhtartu nafsi), no divorce occurs because 'ikhtiyar' is not a word of divorce and the answer does not match the delegation.

Key Terms
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation of authority, specifically the husband giving the wife the power to divorce herself.
اختیار (ikhtiyar): The option or choice given to the wife to divorce herself.
اعراض (i'rad): Averting or turning away, shown by actions indicating disinterest in the option.
اقبال (iqbal): Attention or inclination, shown by actions indicating engagement with the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband tells his wife 'Talqi nafsaki' (choose to divorce yourself), and she is standing and then sits down, what happens to her option?
A) It is nullified.
B) It remains valid.
C) It is void from the start.
D) It depends on her intention.

Key Takeaway
Actions indicating attention (iqbal) preserve the delegated option; actions indicating aversion (i'rad) nullify it.

Lexical Correspondence in Delegated Divorce
States a principle for validity of wife's response in delegated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a wife's response in delegated divorce must match the husband's delegation lexically, distinguishing valid words (e.g., 'abantu nafsi') from invalid ones (e.g., 'akhtartu nafsi').

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, lexical correspondence between delegation and response is decisive.

Delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq) allows a husband to empower his wife to pronounce divorce. However, the validity of her response depends on strict lexical match with the terms given. The Hanafi school requires that the wife's words belong to the category of divorce words; otherwise, her response is void. This lesson unpacks the principle and its application through two classical examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle of Matching
The core rule: if the wife's response conforms to the delegated authority, it is valid; otherwise, it is void. Correspondence is required between the husband's permission and the wife's reply.
2
Identify the Two Key Words
The passage contrasts 'abantu nafsi' (I made myself irrevocably separated) and 'akhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself). Recognize that 'ibānah' is a valid word of divorce in the Hanafi school, while 'ikhtiyār' is not.
'Abantu' is explicit; 'akhtartu' is neither explicit nor a kināyah.
3
Apply the Principle to Both Scenarios
For 'abantu nafsi', the divorce occurs because the word matches the delegated authority (talliqi nafsak). For 'akhtartu nafsi', no divorce occurs because the response does not use a divorce word.
Extra descriptions (like irrevocability) are ignored if they do not match.
4
Analyze the Effect of Extra Descriptions
When the wife adds something beyond the delegation (e.g., 'abantu' implies irrevocability), that part is rejected, but the basic divorce is accepted. This illustrates the principle of partial validity.
The result is a single revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).

ترجمہ: اگر جواب تفویض کے موافق ہو تو صحیح ہے ورنہ لغو ہو گا۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص دوسرے کو کسی شے کا اختیار سپر دکر دے،
اب اگر جواب اس مفوض کے مطابق ہوتو درست ہو گا اور اگر اس کے برخلاف ہو تو جواب لغو ہوگا،
اس لئے کہ تفویض اور جواب میں مطابقت ضروری ہے۔

الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب تفويض الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۵، ط: رحمانيه
(قَوْلُهُ: وَبِأَبَنتُ نَفْسِي طَلْقَتْ لَا بِاخْتَرْتُ يَعْنِي أَنَّ أَبَنْتُ نَفْسِى يَصْلُحُ جَوَابًا لِطَلِّقِي نَفْسَكَ وَلَا يَصْلُحُ اخْتَرَت نَفْسِى جَوَابًا لَهُ ، وَالْفَرُقْ بَيْنَهُمَا أَنَّ الْإِبَانَةَ مِنْ أَلْفَاظِ الطَّلَاقِ لِأَنَّهُ حِمَايَةٌ، وَالْمُفَوَّضُ إِلَيْهَا الطَّلَاقُ، وَالاخْتِيَارُ لَيْسَ مِنْ الْفَاظِهِ لَا صَرِيحًا وَلَا كِتَايَةً بِدَلِيلِ الْوُقُوع بابنتك دُونَ اخْتَارِي، وَإِنْ نَوَى الطَّلَاقَ.
البحر الرائق كتاب الطلاق، فصل في المشيئة، ج ۳ ص ۳۵۳، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب تفويض الطلاق، فصل في المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۵، ط: رحمانيه
Chapter on the exercise of will. (71) The answer: if it is in accordance with the delegation, it is valid, otherwise it is void. Translation: If the answer conforms to the delegation, it is valid, otherwise it will be void. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that if a person delegates authority over something to another, then if the answer conforms to that delegated authority, it will be correct, and if it is contrary to it, the answer will be void, because correspondence between the delegation and the answer is necessary. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning the above principle, indicates that if the husband gave the wife authority to divorce in the words 'Divorce yourself' and the wife responded with 'I have made myself irrevocably divorced (abantu nafsi),' the divorce occurs; but if she said 'I have chosen myself (akhtartu nafsi),' the divorce does not occur and the answer is void. In the first case, divorce occurs because the word 'abantu' is among the words of divorce and matches the delegated authority (talliqi nafsak). However, the wife added an extra description of irrevocability (baynunah), so her speech is contrary to the delegation in that respect; thus, her speech that matches the original divorce is accepted as a single revocable divorce, and the description of irrevocability is rejected because it does not conform to the delegation. In the second case, divorce does not occur because 'ikhtiyar' is not among the words of divorce; therefore, responding with 'akhtartu nafsi' does not match the delegated authority, and the answer is void. (From Al-Hidayah, Book of Divorce, Chapter on Delegation of Divorce, vol. 2 p. 395, Rahmaniyyah edition). (He said: And by 'abantu nafsi' she is divorced, not by 'akhtartu' — meaning that 'abantu nafsi' is valid as a response to 'talliqi nafsak', and 'akhtartu nafsi' is not valid as a response. The difference between them is that 'ibanah' is among the words of divorce because it signifies separation, and the authority delegated to her is divorce; whereas 'ikhtiyar' is neither explicitly nor implicitly a word of divorce, as evidenced by the fact that divorce occurs with 'abantuk' and not with 'ikhtari', even if he intends divorce. (Al-Bahr al-Raiq, Book of Divorce, Chapter on the exercise of will, vol. 3 p. 353, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami; Al-Hidayah, Book of Divorce, Chapter on Delegation, vol. 2 p. 395, Rahmaniyyah).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi tradition regarding delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The author of Usool e Hidayah begins by stating a general principle: when a husband delegates the power to divorce to his wife, the validity of her response depends on it matching the delegated authority. If she responds in a manner consistent with the terms given, the divorce is effective; otherwise, her words are legally void. The commentary then applies this principle to two specific scenarios. First, if the husband says 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself) and the wife says 'abantu nafsi' (I have made myself irrevocably divorced), the divorce occurs because 'abantu' is one of the explicit words of divorce. However, since she added the characteristic of irrevocability (baynunah), which was not part of the delegation, only the actual divorce is accepted, and the extra description is ignored, resulting in a single revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). Second, if the wife says 'akhtartu nafsi' (I have chosen myself), no divorce takes place because 'ikhtiyar' is not a word of divorce in the Hanafi school, whether explicit or implicit. Consequently, her response does not align with the delegated authority and is therefore void. The passage concludes with a quotation from Al-Bahr al-Raiq reinforcing this distinction, citing the ruling that divorce occurs with 'abantu' but not with 'ikhtari', regardless of intention. This illustrates the Hanafi insistence on precise lexical correspondence in contractual expressions.

Key Terms
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation of authority; specifically, a husband delegating to his wife the power to pronounce divorce.
ابانت (ibanah): A verbal noun meaning 'making irrevocably separate'; used as a word of divorce in the Hanafi school.
اختیار (ikhtiyar): Choice; in this context, 'akhtartu nafsi' means 'I have chosen myself' and is NOT a word of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A valid irrevocable divorce occurs.
C) The response is void and no divorce occurs.
D) The husband must repeat the delegation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi delegated divorce, the wife's response must use a word that belongs to the category of divorce for it to be valid; any extra description that does not match the delegation is ignored.

Yamin as a Binding Disposition in Tafwid al-Talaq
States that a yamin (oath/suspensive condition) is a binding act that cannot be revoked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the husband's delegation of talaq via 'talliqi nafsak' is irrevocable, and apply the principle that yamin is a binding disposition to similar ta'liq scenarios.

Core Idea
A yamin — whether an oath or suspensive condition — is a binding disposition that cannot be revoked unilaterally.

In Hanafi fiqh, the term 'yamin' encompasses both explicit oaths and suspensive conditions (ta'liq). When a husband says 'talliqi nafsak', this statement is treated as a yamin because it implicitly conditions the divorce on the wife's action. This lesson unpacks the principle that such a yamin creates a binding (lazim) disposition, meaning the husband cannot retract his delegation. We will trace the proof and understand its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband saying 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself). The ruling is that this delegation becomes binding; the husband cannot retract it.
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the scope of yamin
Yamin in shar' includes both oaths (qasam) and suspensive conditions (ta'liq). The statement 'talliqi nafsak' implies a condition: 'If you divorce yourself, then you are divorced.' Hence it qualifies as a yamin.
Other schools may restrict yamin to oaths only, but Hanafis adopt a broader definition.
3
Trace the proof
The proof is the general principle that yamin is a binding disposition (tasarruf lazim). This principle is supported by the quotation from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, which states that because the statement contains the meaning of yamin (ta'liq), it is binding and retraction is invalid.
No specific Quran or hadith is cited; the proof relies on the established usul principle and the implied condition.
4
Confirm the relied-upon position
Al-Hidayah and Tabyin al-Haqa'iq both affirm that the husband cannot retract. This is the mufta bihi position, with no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۲) إِنَّ الْيَمِينَ تَصَرُّقٌ لَازِمٌ. ٥
ترجمہ: یمین ایک لازمی تصرف ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول سمجھنے سے پہلے یہ بات ذہن نشین کر لینا ضروری ہے کہ یمین کا اطلاق جس طرح قسم پر ہوتا ہے اس طرح تعلیق پر بھی ہوتا ہے، یعنی جب ایک حکم کسی شرط پر مشروط اور معلق کیا جائے تو اس کو بھی یمین کہتے ہیں ، اب مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ یمین چاہے بصورت قسم ہو یا بصورت تعلیق ہو، ایک ایسا تصرف ہے جو آدمی پر لازم ہوتا ہے اس میں رجوع کرنے کی گنجائش نہیں ہوتی۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہا طَلِقِيْ نَفْسَک اب شوہر کے لئے رجوع کرنا جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس جملہ میں یمین کا معنی پایا جاتا ہے یعنی یمین بمعنی تعلیق کا، گویا کہ شوہر نے یوں کہا کہ إِنْ أَرَدْتِ طَلَاقَكِ فَأَنْتِ طالق اور یمین تصرف لازم ہے اس سے رجوع کرنا درست نہیں ہے، لہذ امذکورہ صورت میں بھی شوہر کے لئے رجوع درست نہ ہوگا۔
وَلَا يَمْلِكُ الرُّجُوعَ أَى لَا يَمْلِكُ الزَّوْجُ الرُّجُوعَ بَعْدَ قَوْلِهِ طَلَّقِي نَفْسَكَ حَتَّى لَا يَصِحَ نَهْيُهُ، لِأَنَّ فِيهِ مَعْنَى الْيَمِينِ إِذْ هُوَ تَعْلِيقُ الطَّلَاقِ بِتَطْلِيقِهَا وَالْيَمِينُ تَصَرُّفٌ لَازِمٌ لَا يَصِحُ الرُّجُوعُ عَنْهَا.
تبيين الحقائق كاب الطلاق، فصل فى المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۲۲۶، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
(72) Indeed, the oath (yamin) is a binding disposition. Translation: An oath is a binding act. Explanation: Before understanding the above principle, it is necessary to note that the term yamin applies both to oaths (qasam) and to suspensive conditions (ta'liq). That is, when a ruling is made conditional upon a condition, it is also called yamin. Now the meaning of the principle is that yamin, whether in the form of a vow or a suspensive condition, is a disposition that binds a person, and there is no possibility of revoking it. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, indicates that if a husband says to his wife 'talliqi nafsak', the husband is not allowed to retract, because this sentence carries the meaning of yamin in the sense of ta'liq, as if the husband said, 'If you wish your divorce, then you are divorced.' And yamin is a binding disposition; retracting it is not valid. Therefore, in this case too, the husband cannot retract. (From Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Book of Divorce, Chapter on the Exercise of Will, vol. 2 p. 226, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah).
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the legal effect of the husband's words 'talliqi nafsak' in delegated divorce. The author begins by stating a general principle: yamin (oath) is a binding disposition (tasarruf lazim). He then clarifies that the term yamin in the sacred law (shar') covers both explicit oaths (qasam) and suspensive conditions (ta'liq), where a ruling is tied to a condition. The principle means that once a person performs a yamin, whether in word or by making a condition, it becomes binding and cannot be revoked. The author then applies this to the specific case of delegated divorce: when a husband says 'talliqi nafsak', this expression includes a tacit condition, effectively meaning 'If you divorce yourself, then it is effective.' Because it contains the meaning of yamin (specifically ta'liq), it is a binding disposition. Therefore, the husband cannot retract his words; if he tries to forbid the wife from divorcing herself, his prohibition is invalid. This ruling is supported by a quotation from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, confirming that the husband loses his right to revocation after saying 'talliqi nafsak' because it implies a suspensive condition, and yamin is binding. This lesson highlights the Hanafi view that once authority is delegated in such terms, it is irrevocably transferred to the wife.

Key Terms
یمین (yamin): A binding oath or suspensive condition; includes both explicit vows and conditional statements.
تصرف لازم (tasarruf lazim): A legally binding act that cannot be revoked unilaterally.
تعلیق (ta'liq): A suspensive condition; linking a ruling to a future condition such that the ruling takes effect upon fulfillment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it contains a suspensive condition (ta'liq).
C) Because the wife must accept it.
D) Because the local custom treats it as an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, any delegation of talaq that implies a condition is a binding yamin and cannot be retracted by the husband.

Agency is Not a Binding Disposition
Explaining the principle that agency is not a binding act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that agency (tawkeel) is not a binding disposition and apply it to the case of a husband appointing an agent to pronounce divorce.

Core Idea
Agency is revocable; the principal retains full authority to withdraw.

In Hanafi fiqh, the nature of a legal act determines whether it can be unilaterally revoked. This lesson introduces a key maxim: 'Agency is not a binding disposition.' Understanding this distinction is crucial when a principal, such as a husband appointing an agent for divorce, wishes to change his mind. The maxim clarifies that the principal always retains the right to revoke, unlike in binding dispositions like tamlik (grant of ownership).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognize that tawkeel (appointing an agent) is a ja'iz (non-binding) contract, not a lazim (binding) one. This classification is the foundation of the maxim.
Contrast with tamlik (grant of ownership) which is binding.
2
State the maxim and its meaning
The maxim is at-tawkeelu laysa bi tasarrufin lazim. It means the principal can revoke the agency at any time unless the agent has already executed the act.
This is a definitional ruling about the nature of agency.
3
Apply the maxim to a divorce scenario
When a husband says to another 'Divorce my wife,' the agent (mukhtar) may divorce immediately or later. The husband can revoke the agency before the agent acts.
The agent's authority is not confined to the council (majlis) unlike in tamlik.
4
Distinguish from tamlik (grant of ownership)
In tamlik, the husband says 'Divorce yourself' – this is a binding transfer of authority, confined to the council and irrevocable once accepted.
This contrast clarifies why tawkeel is ja'iz and tamlik is lazim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۳) إِنَّ التَّوْكِيْلَ لَيْسَ بِتَصَرُّفٍ لَازِمٍ. ترجمہ: تو کیل تصرف لازم نہیں ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ توکیل یعنی کسی کو اپنا وکیل بنانا یہ لازمی تصرف نہیں ہے کہ بعد میں مؤکل اس سے رجوع نہیں کر سکتا، جب تو کیل لازم نہیں ہے تو شوہر کو رجوع کا بھی حق حاصل ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے یہ کہا طَلِّقَ امْرَأَتِي کہ میری بیوی کو طلاق دیدو، اب وکیل کو اختیار ہے مؤکل کی بیوی کو مجلس میں بھی طلاق دے سکتا ہے اور مجلس کے بعد بھی، اس لئے کہ وکالت مجلس تک منحصر نہیں ہوتی۔ اور شوہر کو رجوع کا بھی حق حاصل ہے اس لئے کہ یہ وکیل ہے یعنی شوہر نے طلاق کا وکیل بنایا ہے اور تو کیل تصرف لازم نہیں ہے کہ جس سے رجوع نہ ہو سکے اس لئے شوہر جب چاہے اس کی وکالت کو ختم کر کے اپنی بات سے رجوع کر سکتا ہے۔

الهداية كتاب الطلاق، فصل في المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۶، ط: رحمانيه
(73) Indeed, agency is not a binding disposition. Translation: Agency is not a binding disposition. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that making someone one's agent is not a binding act that the principal cannot revoke later. Since agency is not binding, the husband also has the right to revoke. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a person says to another 'Divorce my wife,' the agent has the option to divorce the wife in the council or after it, because agency is not confined to the council. And the husband has the right to revoke because this is agency, and agency is not a binding disposition from which one cannot withdraw. Therefore, the husband can terminate the agency and withdraw from his statement whenever he wishes. (Citations from Hidayah, al-Jawhara, and Hidayah again.)
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines the legal maxim 'Agency is not a binding disposition' (at-tawkeelu laysa bi tasarrufin lazim). The author uses it to clarify a specific case in divorce: if a husband appoints someone as his agent to divorce his wife, the agent may execute the divorce immediately or later, and the husband may revoke the agency at any time. The commentary contrasts this with a case where the husband says to the wife herself 'Divorce yourself'—that is a grant of ownership (tamlik), not agency, and is confined to the council. The key point is that agency, unlike tamlik, does not create a binding obligation; the principal retains the right to rescind.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkeel): The act of appointing someone as an agent to perform an act on one's behalf.
تصرف لازم (tasarruf lazim): A binding legal disposition that cannot be unilaterally revoked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a husband revoke his agent's authority to divorce, according to the principle in this lesson?
A) Because agency is a binding contract that requires mutual consent to annul.
B) Because tawkeel (agency) is not a binding disposition, so the principal retains the right to withdraw.
C) Because the agent must act immediately in the council.
D) Because the husband has no right to revoke once the agent accepts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that agency is not binding ensures that the principal retains full control and can revoke the agent's authority at any time before the act is executed.

Intention Does Not Operate in the Unmentioned
Explaining that intention cannot affect what is not explicitly mentioned in speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that intention does not operate in the unmentioned and apply it to determine the validity of an unexpressed intent in contractual speech, particularly in talaq.

Core Idea
A hidden intention cannot create legal effect if the spoken words do not express it.

In Hanafi law, the validity of many acts depends on what is explicitly said. A person’s inner thoughts or intentions cannot fill gaps in speech. This lesson examines the maxim 'Intention does not operate in the unmentioned' through a detailed divorce scenario. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly assessing contracts and delegations, especially when parties claim intentions not reflected in their words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its literal scope
Read the Arabic maxim and its translation. Understand that it restricts the legal effect of intention to things actually mentioned in speech.
The maxim applies to all contractual and unilateral declarations, not just divorce.
2
Analyze the condition: intention requires explicit mention
The maxim implies a condition: for intention to be legally operative, the intended matter must be explicitly stated in the utterance (madhkoor). If it is unmentioned (ghayr al-madhkoor), the intention is void.
This is a limit on the role of niyyah in non-ibadat contexts.
3
Apply to the talaq scenario
Trace the dialogue: husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' (absolute wish). Wife responds 'I wish if you wish' (conditional wish). Husband then says 'I wish' intending divorce. Note that the word 'divorce' does not appear in either party's final statements.
The wife's response changed the nature of the wish from absolute to conditional, voiding her authority.
4
Conclude the ruling
Since the husband intended divorce but the word 'divorce' was not mentioned in his speech, his intention is ineffective. Consequently, no divorce occurs.
This conclusion directly applies the maxim: intention does not operate in the unmentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۴) إِنَّ النِّيَّةَ لَا تُعْمَلُ فِى غَيْرِ الْمَذْكُورِ.
ترجمہ: نیت غیر مذکور میں عمل نہیں کرتی۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو چیز عبارت میں مذکورہ نہ ہو اگر کوئی اس غیر مذکور شے کی نیت کرتا ہے تو نیت کرنا درست نہ ہوگا اور اس کا کوئی اعتبار نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ نیت اس چیز میں مؤثر اور کارگر ہوتی ہے جو پہلے سے موجود ہو۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا انتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ شِئْتِ " تجھے طلاق ہے اگر تو چاہتی ہے، پھر عورت نے جواب میں کہا شِئْتُ إِنْ شِئْتَ " میں چاہتی ہوں اگر تو چاہتا ہے۔ پھر شوہر نے یوں کہا شِئْتُ " کہ میں چاہتا ہوں اور اس سے طلاق کی نیت کر لی تو یہ اختیار باطل ہو گا اور طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی، اس لئے کہ شوہر نے عورت کی طلاق کو مشیت مرسلہ یعنی غیر معلق مشیت پر موقوف کیا تھا، مگر عورت نے اسے شوہر کی مشیت پر متعلق کر کے مشیت مرسلہ کو مشیت معلقہ کر دیا، شوہر نے بیوی کو جو مشیت مرسلہ حوالہ کی تھی بیوی نے اُسے شوہر کی مشیت پر معلق کر کے مشیت معلقہ کر کے اُسے ضائع کر دیا، اس لئے طلاق کا اختیار اس کے ہاتھ سے نکل جائے گا۔ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں نہ بیوی کے کلام میں طلاق کا لفظ مذکور ہے، اور نہ شوہر کے کلام میں۔ شوہر نے غیر مذکور کی نیت کی اور غیر مذکور کی نیت درست نہیں ہوتی، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی شوہر کا طلاق کی نیت کرنا درست نہ ہوگا، جب نیت درست نہ ہوئی تو طلاق بھی واقع نہ ہوگی۔
(74) Indeed, intention does not operate in the unmentioned. Translation: Intention does not operate in what is not mentioned. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if something is not mentioned in the expression, and someone intends that unmentioned thing, the intention is not valid and will have no consideration, because intention is effective in something that is already present. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain: if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced if you wish,' and the wife responds with 'I wish if you wish,' then the husband says 'I wish' intending divorce, then this authority is void and divorce does not occur. Because the husband had suspended her divorce on an absolute wish, but the wife made it conditional on the husband's wish, thereby changing the absolute wish to a conditional one, and thus lost the authority of divorce from her hand. In this scenario, neither the wife's speech nor the husband's speech contains the word 'divorce'; the husband intended the unmentioned, and intention in the unmentioned is not valid. Since the intention is not valid, divorce does not occur.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces the legal maxim 'Intention does not operate in the unmentioned' (an-niyyatu la ta'malu fi ghayr al-madhkoor). It applies when a person's words do not explicitly refer to something, yet they claim to have intended it. In Hanafi law, such hidden intention has no legal effect. The commentary applies this to a complex case of delegated divorce: the husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' giving the wife authority to choose. But instead of exercising that authority freely, she ties her wish to the husband's wish. The husband then says 'I wish' (intending divorce), but since the word 'divorce' is not mentioned in either party's speech, his intention is ineffective. The wife's alteration also voids her authority because she departed from the absolute wish the husband gave her.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart; in this context, it refers to a hidden intention that is not expressed in speech.
غير المذكور (ghayr al-madhkoor): Something not explicitly mentioned or stated in the utterance.
مشيت مرسلة (masheeyah mursalah): An unconditional or absolute wish, not tied to any condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario given in the passage, why does the husband's intention to divorce not result in a valid divorce?
A) Because the husband did not say 'I divorce you' explicitly.
B) Because the wife's response nullified the husband's authority.
C) Because the husband's intention was for something not mentioned in either his or his wife's speech, and intention does not operate in the unmentioned.
D) Because the wife did not agree to the divorce.

Key Takeaway
Intention is legally operative only when its object is explicitly mentioned in the spoken words; otherwise, it is ignored.

Suspension on a Past Condition is Immediate
Definition and application of a key Hanafi maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of ta'leeq on a past condition and apply it to determine when a conditional divorce becomes effective immediately.

Core Idea
Conditioning a ruling on a past event triggers immediate effect — the condition is irrelevant.

You have learned about delegated divorce (tafwid) and the conditional 'I wish' exchange. Now we encounter a more general principle: if a condition is already accomplished, the conditional statement is not a true suspension but an immediate pronouncement. This maxim cuts through many ambiguous statements and prevents manipulation of timing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Learn ta'leeq (suspension on a condition) and tanjiz (immediate execution). Understand that ta'leeq requires a future, uncertain condition.
The condition must be *future* for a true suspension.
2
State the maxim
The maxim: 'Suspension on a past condition is immediate' (at-ta'leequ bi shay'in ka'inin tanjiz). Internalise that if the condition already exists, the ruling happens now.
The past condition can be known or unknown to the parties.
3
Apply the maxim to a simple scenario
Husband says: 'You are divorced if the sun has already set.' The sun has set. The condition is past, so the divorce is effective immediately.
The statement is not a postponement; it is an immediate divorce.
4
Connect to the Hidayah divorce case
In the earlier case, the wife says 'I wish if such-and-such matter has passed.' This is a conditional on a past event, so her authority is exercised immediately — not suspended.
This contrasts with 'I wish if you wish,' which is a future condition and voids the delegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۵) إِنَّ التَّعْلِيْقَ بِشَيْطٍ كَائِنِ تَنْجِيزُ .
ترجمہ : گزری ہوئی شرط پر کسی چیز کی تعلیق تجیز ہو جاتی ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی حکم ایسی شرط پر معلق کیا جو پہلے سے زمانہ ماضی میں موجود ہو تو اس صورت میں یہ حکم فی الحال واقع ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں اگر جواب میں یہ کہا شِئْتُ إِنْ كَانَ لَأَمْرِ قَدْ مَضَى "میں نے چاہا اگر فلاں معاملہ گزر گیا ہو تو بیوی نے شوہر أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ شِئت فَقَالَتْ شِئت إِنْ شِئت فَقَالَ الزَّوْجُ (شِئت) حَالَ كَوْنِهِ (يَنْوِى الطَّلَاقَ لَا يَقَعُ شَيْءٌ لِأَنَّهُ عَلَّقَ طَلَاقَهَا بِالْمَشِيئَةِ بِالْمُرْسَلَةِ وَهِيَ أَتَتْ بِالْمُعَلَّقَةِ فَيَخْرُجُ الْأَمُرُ مِنْ يَدِهَا بِالِاشْتِغَالِ بِمَا لَمْ يُفَوِّضُ إِلَيْهَا مِنْ الشَّرْطِ وَإِنْ نَوَى الطَّلَاقَ إِذْ لَيْسَ فِي كَلَامِهِ وَلَا فِي كَلَامِهَا ذِكْرُ الطَّلَاقِ فَبَقِىَ قَوْلُهُ شِئْتِ مُبْهَما ، وَالنِّيَّةُ لَا تَعْمَلُ فِي غَيْرِ الْمَذْكُورِ. مجمع الأنهر كتاب الطلاق، فصل فى المشيئة، ج ا ص ۴۱۴، ط: دار إحياء التراث العربي الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب تفويض الطلاق، فصل في المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۶، ط: رحمانيه
(75) Indeed, suspension on something that has occurred is an immediate execution. Translation: Suspending something on a past condition makes it immediate. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if a ruling is suspended on a condition that already existed in the past, then the ruling occurs immediately. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that in the above scenario, if the wife responds 'I wish if such-and-such matter has passed,' then the situation changes. The Arabic text from Al-Hidayah states: The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' and she says 'I wish if you wish,' then the husband says 'I wish' intending divorce—nothing occurs because he suspended her divorce on an absolute wish, and she came with a conditional wish, so the matter leaves her hand by occupying herself with what was not delegated to her from the condition. And if he intended divorce, since there is no mention of divorce in his speech nor in her speech, his statement 'I wish' remains ambiguous, and intention does not operate in the unmentioned. (Citations from Majma' al-Anhar and Al-Hidayah.)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the maxim 'Suspension on a past condition is immediate' (at-ta'leequ bi shay'in ka'inin tanjiz). If someone says 'If such-and-such has happened, then X,' and the condition is already true (in the past), the statement is treated as an immediate pronouncement, not a future contingency. The commentary then links back to the earlier divorce case: if the wife, instead of saying 'I wish if you wish,' says 'I wish if such-and-such matter has passed' (referring to a past event), her speech would be an immediate exercise of authority, and the divorce would occur instantly. However, as previously discussed, in the case where she says 'I wish if you wish,' the conditional language voids the delegated authority. The Arabic quote from Al-Hidayah reiterates that the husband's intention for divorce is ineffective because the word divorce is not uttered.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'leeq): Suspending the occurrence of a ruling on a condition that may or may not be fulfilled.
تنجيز (tanjiz): Making a legal disposition effective immediately, without condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The divorce is suspended until she breaks her fast again.
B) The divorce occurs immediately because the condition is already fulfilled.
C) The statement is invalid because fasting is not related.
D) The divorce depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that suspension on a past condition is immediate prevents a conditional statement with an already-fulfilled condition from being a true suspension; the ruling takes effect instantly.

The Principle of Ta'liq with an Existing Condition: Immediate Effect
Explains the ruling on conditional divorce when the condition is already fulfilled

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a conditional divorce becomes immediate when the condition is already fulfilled, and explain why a conditional response from a spouse invalidates delegated authority.

Core Idea
A past condition makes a conditional divorce immediate; an altered delegation voids the power.

This lesson from al-Hidayah unravels a subtle rule in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife with an unconditional wish, her conditional response destroys that delegation. But if the condition is already a past reality, the divorce strikes instantly. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying ta'liq and tanjiz.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation scenario
The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' – this is an unconditional delegation (tafwid). The wife's conditional response 'I wish if you wish' alters the nature of the wish from unconditional to conditional.
2
Analyze the invalidation
The wife's addition of a condition changes the unconditional will into a conditional one, which was not delegated. The original delegation is wasted, and the husband's later 'I wish' with intent does not revive it.
Intention does not operate on an unspoken term (al-niyyah la ta'malu fi ghayr al-madhkur).
3
Understand the principle of tanjiz
The principle: 'التعليق بشرط كائن تنجيز' – if a ruling is suspended on a condition that already exists (past or present), the suspension becomes immediate, and the ruling takes effect at once.
The condition must be already realized, not future.
4
Apply the principle to an example
If the wife says 'I wish if such and such event has passed' (a past event), the divorce occurs immediately because the condition is present. Similarly, 'I wish if the sky exists' triggers instant divorce.
5
Distinguish valid from invalid ta'liq
Valid immediate: condition is past/existing. Invalid: condition is future (e.g., 'if my father wishes') or the wife adds a condition to a delegated unconditional will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا انتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ شِنتِ " تجھے طلاق ہے اگر تو چاہتی ہے، پھر عورت نے جواب میں کہا شِئْتُ إِنْ شفتَ “ میں چاہتی ہوں اگر تو چاہتا ہے۔ پھر شوہر نے یوں کہا شفت “ کہ میں چاہتا ہوں اور اس سے طلاق کی نیت کر لی تو یہ اختیار باطل ہو گا اور طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی، اس لئے کہ شوہر نے عورت کی طلاق کو مشیت مرسلہ یعنی غیر معلق مشیت پر موقوف کیا تھا، مگر عورت نے اسے شوہر کی مشیت پر متعلق کر کے مشیت مرسلہ کو مشیت معلقہ کر دیا ،شوہر نے بیوی کو جو مشیت مرسلہ حوالہ کی تھی بیوی نے اُسے شوہر کی مشیت پر معلق کر کے مشیت معلقہ کر کے اُسے ضائع کر دیا، اس لئے طلاق کا اختیار اس کے ہاتھ سے نکل جائے گا۔ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں نہ بیوی کے کلام میں طلاق کا لفظ مذکور ہے، اور نہ شوہر کے کلام میں ۔ شوہر نے غیر مذکور کی نیت کی اور غیر مذکور کی نیت درست نہیں ہوتی ، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی شوہر کا طلاق کی نیت کرنا درست نہ ہوگا، جب نیت درست نہ ہوئی تو طلاق بھی واقع نہ ہوگی۔
(۷۵) إِنَّ التَّعْلِيْقَ بِشَيْطٍ كَائِنِ تَنْجِيزُ .
ترجمہ : گزری ہوئی شرط پر کسی چیز کی تعلیق تجیز ہو جاتی ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی حکم ایسی شرط پر معلق کیا جو پہلے سے زمانہ ماضی میں موجود ہو تو اس صورت میں یہ حکم فی الحال واقع ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں اگر جواب میں یہ کہا شِئْتُ إِنْ كَانَ لَأَمْرِ قَدْ مَضَى “میں نے چاہا اگر فلاں معاملہ گزر گیا ہو تو بیوی نے شوہر أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ شِئت فَقَالَتْ شِئت إِنْ شِئت فَقَالَ الزَّوْجُ (شِئت) حَالَ كَوْنِهِ (يَنْوِى الطَّلَاقَ لَا يَقَعُ شَيْءٌ لِأَنَّهُ عَلَّقَ طَلَاقَهَا بِالْمَشِيئَةِ بِالْمُرْسَلَةِ وَهِيَ أَتَتْ بِالْمُعَلَّقَةِ فَيَخْرُجُ الْأَمُرُ مِنْ يَدِهَا بِالِاشْتِغَالِ بِمَا لَمْ يُفَوِّضُ إِلَيْهَا مِنْ الشَّرْطِ وَإِنْ نَوَى الطَّلَاقَ إِذْ لَيْسَ فِي كَلَامِهِ وَلَا فِي كَلَامِهَا ذِكْرُ الطَّلَاقِ فَبَقِىَ قَوْلُهُ شِئْتِ مُبْهَما ، وَالنِّيَّةُ لَا تَعْمَلُ فِي غَيْرِ الْمَذْكُورِ. مجمع الأنهر كتاب الطلاق، فصل فى المشيئة، ج ا ص ۴۱۴، ط: دار إحياء التراث العربي الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب تفويض الطلاق، فصل في المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۶، ط: رحمانيه

اصول ہدایہ جلد ثانی کے جواب میں اپنی مشیت کو ایسی چیز پر معلق کیا جو زمانہ ماضی میں ہو چکی ہے تو اس صورت میں اس پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ، اس لئے کہ ماضی میں واقع شدہ کسی کام پر شرط کو معلق کرنے سے وہ شرط نجز بن جاتی ہے، لہذا یہاں بھی تعلیق طلاق کی شرط معلق نہیں رہے گی بلکہ منجز بن جائے گی اور منجز سے فی الحال طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے، لہذا یہاں بھی فی الحال طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ تقریب الی الفہم کے لئے اس کی مثال جیسے عورت نے کہا شِئْتُ إِذَا كَانَ السَّمَاءُ مَوْجُوْدًا “ کہ میں طلاق واقع کرنا چاہتی ہوں اگر آسمان موجود ہے، تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ، اس لئے کہ بیوی نے وقوع طلاق کوایسی شے پر معلق کیا جو پہلے سے موجود ہے۔ اور ہر وہ حکم جوایسی شے پر معلق ہو تو وہ فی الحال دائر ہوتا ہے، لہذ امذکورہ صورت میں بھی وقوع طلاق کا حکم فی الحال دائر ہوگا اور طلاق واقع ہوگی۔
(كَذَا كُلُّ تَعْلِيقِ بِمَعْدُومٍ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَتْ شِئْتُ إِنْ شَاءَ أَبِي، أَوْ شِئْتُ إِنْ كَانَ كَذَا لِأَمْرٍ لَمْ يَجِي بَعْدُ لِمَا مَرَّ أَنَّ الْمَأْتِى بِهِ مَشِيئَةٌ مُعَلَّقَةٌ فَلا يَقَعُ الطَّلَاقُ وَيَبْطُلُ الْأَمْرُ (بِخِلَافِ الْمَوْجُودِ) فَإِنَّهَا لَوْ قَالَتْ قَدْ شِئت إِنْ كَانَ كَذَا لِأَمْرِ قَدْ مَضَى طَلْقَتْ لِأَنَّ التَّعْلِيقَ بِشَرْطٍ كَائِنِ تَنْجِيز. درر الحكام كتاب الطلاق باب التفويض، ج ١ ص ٣٧٦ الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب تفويض الطلاق، فصل فى المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۷، ط: رحمانيه
The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, is explaining that if the husband says to his wife 'You are divorced if you wish', and the woman responded 'I wish if you wish', then the husband said 'I wish' intending divorce, then this delegation is invalid and divorce does not occur, because the husband suspended her divorce on an unconditional will, but the woman attached it to the husband's will, turning the unconditional will into a conditional one. The husband had delegated an unconditional will to the wife, and she attached it to the husband's will, making it conditional and thus wasting it; therefore the authority of divorce leaves her hand. In the above case, the word 'divorce' is not mentioned in either her speech or his. The husband intended something not mentioned, and intention regarding something not mentioned is not valid. So his intention of divorce is not valid, and divorce does not occur. (75) Indeed, suspending on an existing condition makes it immediate. Translation: Suspending something on a past condition is immediate. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if a ruling is conditioned on a condition that already existed in the past, then that ruling takes effect immediately. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, explains that in the above case, if she said in response 'I wish if such and such matter had passed', then the wife... [Arabic text] ... Majma' al-Anhar, Kitab al-Talaq, Fasl fi al-Mashiyya, vol. 1, p. 414, Dar Ihya' al-Turath al-Arabi; al-Hidaya, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab Tafwid al-Talaq, Fasl fi al-Mashiyya, vol. 2, p. 396, Rahmaniyya. In response, she attached her will to something that happened in the past, so in that case divorce occurs immediately, because attaching a condition to something that has already occurred makes that condition immediate. Here too, the condition of suspending divorce will not remain suspended but becomes immediate, and from an immediate condition divorce occurs immediately. For ease of understanding, an example: if the woman said 'I wish if the sky exists', then divorce occurs, because the wife suspended the occurrence of divorce on something that already exists. And any ruling that is suspended on such a thing takes effect immediately. In the above case too, the ruling of divorce will take effect immediately. (Likewise, every suspension on a non-existent [condition] is like when she said 'I wish if my father wishes', or 'I wish if such and such matter that has not yet occurred', because what she brought is a conditional will, so divorce does not occur and the matter is invalid. Contrary to the existing [condition], for if she said 'I have wished if such and such matter that has passed', she is divorced, because suspending on an existing condition is immediate. Durar al-Hukkam, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Tafwid, vol. 1, p. 376; al-Hidaya, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab Tafwid al-Talaq, Fasl fi al-Mashiyya, vol. 2, p. 397, Rahmaniyya.)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) when the condition is something that has already occurred. The author of al-Hidayah, here commenting on the principle 'التعليق بشرط كائن تنجيز' (suspending on an existing condition makes it immediate), first discusses a scenario where the husband gives his wife the option to divorce herself by saying 'You are divorced if you wish'. If the wife responds by making her wish conditional on the husband's wish (e.g., 'I wish if you wish'), then the delegation becomes invalid because she has altered the nature of the delegated authority. The husband's subsequent 'I wish' with the intention of divorce does not effect divorce because the word 'divorce' is not mentioned in either party's speech, and intention cannot create a ruling in the absence of a verbal expression. This follows the Hanafi principle that niyyah (intention) does not operate on something not mentioned (al-niyyah la ta'malu fi ghayr al-madhkur). The passage then introduces the core principle: if a condition for a ruling is already fulfilled in the past, the conditional statement becomes immediate (tanjiz). For example, if the wife says 'I wish if such and such matter happened' (referring to an event that has already occurred), then the divorce takes effect immediately. This is because the condition is not a future event but an existing reality, so the suspension is equivalent to an immediate pronouncement. The passage illustrates this with the example of 'I wish if the sky exists', which is always true, so divorce occurs instantly. The Arabic text from Durar al-Hukkam further clarifies that if the condition is non-existent (like 'if my father wishes' or a future event), the divorce does not occur because the will is conditional; but if the condition is an existing past event, divorce does occur.

Key Terms
تعلیق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a ruling (like divorce) on the fulfillment of a condition.
مشیت مرسلہ (mashiyyah mursalah): An unconditional will or desire, not attached to any condition.
مشیت معلقہ (mashiyyah mu'allaqah): A conditional will, where the wish is made dependent on another condition.
تنجیز (tanjiz): Making a ruling immediate; when a conditional statement becomes effective immediately because the condition is already realized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced if you wish' and she responds 'I wish if such and such had happened' (a past event), what is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The divorce occurs immediately.
B) The divorce occurs only when the past event is confirmed.
C) The divorce does not occur because the condition is not fulfilled.
D) The wife's wish is invalid and no divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce becomes immediate if the condition is already fulfilled; a delegated authority is voided if the spouse adds an unauthorized condition.

The Effect of 'Kullama' in Divorce: Repetition of Acts
Explains that 'kullama' in conditional divorce necessitates repetition of the act of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the word 'kullama' necessitates repetition of actions and apply it to determine that a wife can give herself multiple divorces (up to three) when delegated with 'kullama'.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'whenever you wish,' each wish triggers a new divorce — 'kullama' means repetition.

The word 'kullama' (whenever) carries a powerful legal effect in Hanafi divorce law: it implies generality and repetition of the conditioned action. In this lesson, we examine a ruling from al-Hidayah that illustrates this principle with a common delegation scenario. Understanding this will prevent confusion about how many divorces occur when a wife is given the right to divorce herself using 'kullama'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key word and its linguistic property
Locate the word 'kullama' in the statement and note that it means 'whenever' or 'every time'. In Arabic, it indicates generality (ʿumūm) — it covers all instances of the condition.
Generality is established when the action can be repeated.
2
Understand the principle of repetition
The principle states that 'kullama' necessitates repetition of the act it is attached to. Here, the act is divorce (al-ṭalāq). Because the condition is general, each occurrence of the condition (the wife's wish) must be met with a separate divorce.
This is unlike a single conditional statement without 'kullama'.
3
Apply to the divorce scenario
The husband says: 'You are divorced whenever you wish.' The wife expresses her wish once → one divorce occurs. She expresses it again → a second divorce occurs. She can continue until three divorces, as that is the maximum allowed in Islam.
The wife cannot give more than three total, regardless of how many times she wishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۶) إِنَّ كَلِمَةَ كُلَّمَا تُوُجِبُ تَكْرَارَ الْأَفْعَال.
ترجمه كلمه كُلَّمَا افعال کے تکرار کو ثابت کرتا ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ لفظ كُلما " یہ اس بات کا موجب ہے کہ فعل میں تکرار ہو یعنی ایک فعل کو علی سبیل الانفراد یکے بعد دیگرے کرنے کا تقاضا کرتا ہے، کیونکہ کلمہ حلما میں تعمیم ہے اور تمیم اس وقت ثابت ہوگی جب کسی فعل میں تکرار پایا جائے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا انتِ طَالِقٌ كُلَّمَا شِئْتِ “ کہ تجھے طلاق ہے جب بھی تو چاہے، تو اب بیوی کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ اپنے آپ کو ایک طلاق دے، پھر ایک طلاق دے یہاں تک کہ تین طلاقیں دے سکتی ہے، کیونکہ شوہر نے بیوی کو كُلَّمَا" کے ساتھ طلاق کا اختیار دیا ہے اور كُلَّمَا افعال کے تکرار کو ثابت کرتا ہے، لہذا یہاں بھی حلما فعل طلاق کے تکرار کو ثابت کرے گا لہذا بیوی یکے بعد دیگرے اپنے
(كَذَا كُلُّ تَعْلِيقِ بِمَعْدُومٍ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَتْ شِئْتُ إِنْ شَاءَ أَبِي، أَوْ شِئْتُ إِنْ كَانَ كَذَا لِأَمْرٍ لَمْ يَجِي بَعْدُ لِمَا مَرَّ أَنَّ الْمَأْتِى بِهِ مَشِيئَةٌ مُعَلَّقَةٌ فَلا يَقَعُ الطَّلَاقُ وَيَبْطُلُ الْأَمْرُ (بِخِلَافِ الْمَوْجُودِ) فَإِنَّهَا لَوْ قَالَتْ قَدْ شِئت إِنْ كَانَ كَذَا لِأَمْرِ قَدْ مَضَى طَلْقَتْ لِأَنَّ التَّعْلِيقَ بِشَرْطٍ كَائِنِ تَنْجِيز. درر الحكام كتاب الطلاق باب التفويض، ج ١ ص ٣٧٦ الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب تفويض الطلاق، فصل فى المشيئة، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۷، ط: رحمانيه
(76) Indeed, the word 'kullama' necessitates the repetition of actions. Translation: The word 'kullama' establishes the repetition of actions. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the word 'kullama' necessitates repetition of the act, i.e., it requires doing one act individually one after another, because the word 'kullama' implies generality, and generality is established when repetition occurs in an action. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, explains that if the husband says to his wife 'You are divorced whenever you wish', then the wife has the option to give herself one divorce, then another divorce, until she can give three divorces, because the husband delegated to her the authority of divorce with 'kullama', and 'kullama' establishes repetition of actions; therefore here too 'kullama' will establish repetition of the act of divorce, so the wife can one after another her own... (Likewise, every suspension on a non-existent [condition] is like when she said 'I wish if my father wishes', or 'I wish if such and such matter that has not yet occurred', because what she brought is a conditional will, so divorce does not occur and the matter is invalid. Contrary to the existing [condition], for if she said 'I have wished if such and such matter that has passed', she is divorced, because suspending on an existing condition is immediate. Durar al-Hukkam, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Tafwid, vol. 1, p. 376; al-Hidaya, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab Tafwid al-Talaq, Fasl fi al-Mashiyya, vol. 2, p. 397, Rahmaniyya.)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on the use of the word 'kullama' (whenever) in conditional divorce. The principle is that 'kullama' implies repetition of the action it refers to. The author of al-Hidayah applies this to divorce: if a husband says 'you are divorced whenever you wish', the wife is permitted to divorce herself multiple times, one after another, up to three divorces. This is because 'kullama' indicates generality and repetition, so each time she wishes, she can execute a divorce. The passage also includes an Arabic textual reference that reiterates the earlier principle about existing vs. non-existing conditions, which serves as a reminder of the distinction. The Urdu explanation emphasizes that the word 'kullama' is comprehensive and necessitates sequential repetition, unlike a single conditional formulation. The reference to Durar al-Hukkam and al-Hidayah confirms the authoritative standing of this ruling within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
كُلَّمَا (kullama): The Arabic word meaning 'whenever' or 'every time', which necessitates repetition of the act it is attached to.
تكرار الافعال (tikrar al-af'al): Repetition of actions, i.e., performing an act multiple times consecutively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Up to two divorces.
C) Up to three divorces, one after another.
D) As many as she wishes, unlimited.

Key Takeaway
The word 'kullama' in conditional divorce necessitates repetition, allowing the wife to execute multiple divorces sequentially up to three.
