Delegation in the Description of Divorce Necessitates the Origin
Principle on delegation of divorce description requiring origin existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'anti taliqun kayfa shi't', explain the linguistic reasoning of Abu Hanifa, and distinguish it from the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
Delegation of the description of divorce requires prior existence of the divorce itself.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle but important issue in Hanafi divorce law: what happens when a husband uses the word 'kayfa' (how) in delegating divorce? The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, turns on a precise linguistic point. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to analyse legal language in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central case
The husband says: 'You are divorced as you wish' (anti taliqun kayfa shi't). The question: Does divorce occur immediately, or is it suspended until the wife expresses her wish?
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their linguistic argument
They argue that 'kayfa' asks about the state absolutely, so the divorce must be suspended on her wish to give her full discretion. They analogise to words like 'in shi't' (if you wish).
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his linguistic argument
He distinguishes: 'kayfa' is specifically for seeking a description, not the origin. Example: 'How did you become?' asks about health/sickness. So the delegation is only about the description (the type of divorce), not the act itself. Description requires the pre-existence of the thing described.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, one revocable divorce occurs immediately. Under Sahibayn, the wife can choose the type (e.g., revocable or irrevocable) and the divorce only occurs when she chooses.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position for fatwa, as preferred by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ التَّفْوِيْضَ فِي وَصْفِ الطَّلَاقِ يَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ أَصْلِهِ. لأَنَّ كَلِمَةَ كَيْفَ لِلسُّؤَالِ عَنْ الْحَالِ مُطْلَقًا فَلا بُدَّ مِنْ تَعْلِيقِ الأَصْلِ بِمَشِيئَتِهَا لِتَبْتَ لَهَا الْمَشِيئَةُ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَحْوَالِ، كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ أَنتِ طَالِقٌ إِنْ شِئتِ أَوْ حَيْثُ شِئتِ أَوْ أَيْنَ شِئتِ. وَلِأبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ كَلِمَةَ كَيْفَ لِطَلَبِ الْوَصْفِ لَا لِطَلَبِ الأَصْلِ، يُقَالُ كَيْفَ أَصْبَحْتَ: أَي عَلَى أَي وَصْفِ مِنْ الصِّحَّةِ وَالسَّقَمِ وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ، فَكَانَ التَّفْوِيضُ فِي وَصْفِ الطَّلاقِ، وَالتَّفْوِيضُ فِي وَصْفِهِ يَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ أَصْلِهِ، وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ كَيْفَ لِطَلَبِهِ وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، وَوُجُودُ الطَّلَاقِ بِوُقُوعِهِ وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ.
Indeed, delegation in the description of divorce necessitates the existence of its origin. Because the word 'kayfa' is for asking about the state absolutely, so it is necessary to suspend the origin (divorce) to her wish so that the wish is established for her in all states, as if he said 'You are divorced if you wish' or 'whenever you wish' or 'wherever you wish.' And for Abu Hanifa: The word 'kayfa' is for seeking the description, not for seeking the origin. One says 'How did you become?' i.e., upon which description of health or sickness or other than that. So the delegation was in the description of divorce, and delegation in its description necessitates the existence of its origin, otherwise 'kayfa' would be for seeking it and that is not the case. And the existence of divorce is by its occurrence, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq) when the husband uses the word 'kayfa' (how). The principle states that delegating the description of divorce necessitates that the origin (the divorce itself) exists first. The proof is linguistic: 'kayfa' is used to ask about a description (e.g., 'How are you?' asks about health), not to bring about the act. Therefore, when a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced as you wish' (anti taliqun kayfa shi't), the divorce occurs immediately (as a revocable divorce) before the wife exercises her wish, because the description (the type of divorce) can only be predicated on an existing divorce. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that 'kayfa' asks about the state absolutely, so the divorce should be suspended on her wish, like using 'in' or 'mata'. But Abu Hanifa distinguishes: 'kayfa' specifically asks for a description, not the act itself. The commentary from Al-Hidayah and Al-Inayah confirms Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the right to divorce herself or choose the type of divorce.
وصف (wasf): Description or attribute of a thing; here specifically the type or legal characterization of divorce (e.g., revocable, irrevocable).
كيف (kayfa): The interrogative particle 'how', used here to inquire about the state or description of the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Anti taliqun kayfa shi't', what is the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) No divorce occurs until the wife wishes.
B) One revocable divorce occurs immediately.
C) Three divorces occur immediately.
D) The wife has the choice to give herself one or two divorces.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'anti taliqun kayfa shi't', the divorce occurs immediately as one revocable divorce; only the description (type) is delegated to the wife's choice.

The Scope of Delegated Divorce: 'Min' and 'Ma'
Principle on using 'min' and 'ma' in divorce delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa restricts the wife's authority to a maximum of two divorces when told 'Divorce yourself from three as you wish', and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument.

Core Idea
When 'min' and 'ma' combine, both must be given effect: the result is a number that is both partial and general — two.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of two common Arabic particles in the context of divorce delegation. The linguistic analysis directly determines the legal outcome: how many divorces can the wife effect? Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi usul principles to contractual language.

Guided Path
1
State the Case
Identify the husband's utterance: 'Tilliqi nafsaki min thalathin ma shi'ti' (Divorce yourself from three as you wish). The issue is how many divorces the wife may lawfully effect.
The phrase 'min thalathin ma shi'ti' is the linguistic focus.
2
Identify the Contested Element
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow the wife to give herself one, two, or three divorces. Abu Hanifa limits her to one or two only. The contested element is whether 'min' indicates partiality (tab'iid) or clarification (bayan).
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's Linguistic Proof
Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' literally indicates partiality when it precedes something with parts, and divorce has parts (one, two, three). 'Ma' indicates generality. Combining both, the intended number must be a part of three (less than three) and general (more than one), i.e., two. Acting on both literal meanings is possible.
The key is that both particles can be given effect without metaphorical interpretation.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's Counter-Proof
The Sahibayn argue that 'ma' is for generality and 'min' is for clarification (bayan). Thus, the wife may choose any number from the set of three, including three itself. They prioritize 'ma' and reinterpret 'min' to avoid restriction.
Their interpretation requires taking 'min' in a non-literal sense (bayan) where context permits.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the wife cannot effect three divorces in one sitting; she can only give one or two. Under the Sahibayn, she could give three immediately. This affects whether the divorce is revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in). The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
The practical consequence: three divorces would make her haram for him until she marries another. Under Abu Hanifa, she can use one or two and leave a third for later.

Source Text

Indeed, the word 'min' is literally for partiality (tab'iid) and 'ma' is for generality (ta'mim), so both are acted upon. Al-Hidayah: 'Divorce yourself from three as you wish' - she divorces less than three, so she does not have the power to effect three according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And on this difference is (the case) 'choose from three as you wish'. For them (the Sahibayn), 'ma' is for generality and 'min' is for clarification (bayan). And for him (Abu Hanifa), 'min' is literally for partiality when it enters upon something with parts, and divorce is from that, and 'ma' is for generality. It is possible to act upon both by making the intended meaning a general part (juz' 'amm), and two is like that because it is general with respect to one and partial with respect to three.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the delegation of divorce using the words 'min' (from) and 'ma' (what/whatever). The principle states that 'min' is literally for partiality (tab'iid) and 'ma' is for generality (ta'mim), and both must be given effect if possible. The case is: a husband says to his wife 'Tilliqi nafsaki min thalathin ma shi'ti' (Divorce yourself from three as you wish). According to Abu Hanifa, the wife can give herself one or two divorces, but not three. This is because 'min' indicates a part of the three (so less than three), and 'ma' indicates generality (any number she wishes) – but the combination yields a number that is both a part (less than three) and general (more than one), i.e., two. The Sahibayn argue that 'ma' is for generality and 'min' is for clarification (bayan), so the wife can give herself any number up to three, including three. Abu Hanifa counters that 'min' is literally for partiality when it precedes something that has parts, and divorce has parts (one, two, three). Acting upon both literal meanings gives the result of two divorces. The commentary from Al-Hidayah confirms this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
من (min): The preposition 'from'; in this context, it indicates partiality (tab'iid) according to Abu Hanifa when used before something with parts.
ما (ma): The particle 'what' or 'whatever'; here it indicates generality (ta'mim) – any number the wife wishes.
تبعيض (tab'iid): Denoting a part or portion of a whole; partiality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) One or two, but not three.

Key Takeaway
When linguistic particles 'min' and 'ma' combine in delegation, the intended number must be both a part (less than three) and general (more than one), yielding two as the maximum.

Applying Usul: How 'Min' and 'Ma' Determine Talaq Count in Delegation
Derives talaq ruling from usul principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'min' is literal for partiality and 'ma' for generality to derive the number of divorces a delegated wife can effect.

Core Idea
When 'min' and 'ma' appear together, act on both literal meanings: a part (two) that is general relative to one.

A simple phrase like 'Divorce yourself from three as you wish' can yield different rulings depending on how we interpret two key Arabic particles. This passage shows how Imam Abu Hanifa prioritizes the literal meanings of 'min' (partiality) and 'ma' (generality) to determine that the wife can only give one or two divorces, not three. The principle is not just about divorce—it reveals a core Hanafi usal approach: stick to the literal as long as possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Delegation Phrase
The husband says: 'طلقی نفسك من ثلاث ما شئت' (Divorce yourself from three as you wish). Note the presence of both 'min' and 'ma'.
The phrase is a conditional delegation of talaq authority.
2
Classify 'min' and 'ma'
According to Hanafi usul, 'min' is literal (haqiqah) for tab'id (partiality) when it enters upon something with parts. 'Ma' is literal for 'umum (generality).
If a literal meaning is possible, we do not turn to metaphorical interpretations.
3
Apply Both Literal Meanings
Acting on both: the number of divorces must be a part (tab'id) of three, and general ('umum) relative to the options. The only number satisfying both is two: it is a part of three and general compared to one.
One is not general (it is specific). Three is not a part (it is the whole).
4
Derive the Ruling
Since two satisfies both, the wife can give one or two divorces by the generality of 'ma', but not three. This is Imam Abu Hanifa's position.
The Sahibayn interpret 'min' as bayan (clarification), leading to a different ruling.
5
Contrast the Sahibayn's View
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that 'min' is for bayan, so the phrase means 'choose from three', allowing up to three. The Hanafi response: we prefer acting on both literals when possible.
The thamarah (practical effect) is that three divorces become irrevocable (ba'in) according to them.

Therefore, as long as it is possible to apply it to the literal meaning, there is no need to go to metaphor. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if a husband delegated divorce to his wife in these words: 'Divorce yourself from three as you wish,' then in this case the wife has the authority to effect one or two divorces, but not three. This is because in this statement the words 'min' and 'ma' appear. 'Min' literally means partiality, and 'ma' means generality. Acting upon both, i.e., generality and partiality, occurs when one out of three remains and two divorces occur. Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh principle applied to a specific talaq case. The principle: The word 'min' is literal (haqiqah) for tab'id (partiality), and 'ma' is literal for 'umum (generality). In Hanafi usul, when a word is literal in a meaning, it must be applied to that meaning unless there is a compelling reason to interpret otherwise. Here, the author of Hidayah uses this principle to resolve a dispute about the effect of a divorce delegation. If a husband says 'Divorce yourself from three as you wish' (طلقی نفسك من ثلاث ما شئت), the wife can only effect one or two divorces, not three. This is because: (1) 'min' indicates partiality, meaning some of the three, not all; (2) 'ma' indicates generality, meaning whatever number she chooses. Acting upon both literally: the number must be a part (some) of three and general relative to one. Two fits because it is a part of three (not the whole) and is general relative to one (includes the option of one). Three would be the whole, contradicting the partiality of 'min'. Hence, two divorces occur. Imam Abu Hanifa holds this view. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they interpret 'min' as bayan (clarification) and 'ma' as generality, so she can choose any number up to three. But Imam's view is preferred because it preserves the literal meanings.

Key Terms
مِنْ (min): Literal for tab'id (partiality) when used with a noun having parts.
مَا (ma): Literal for umum (generality), indicating all possible options.
تَبْعِيض (tab'id): The meaning of partiality, i.e., a part of something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One divorce only
B) Two divorces only
C) Three divorces only
D) One or two divorces

Key Takeaway
When 'min' and 'ma' appear together in a talaq delegation, the Hanafi school (following Imam Abu Hanifa) applies their literal meanings to limit the wife to two divorces, preserving the partiality of 'min' and the generality of 'ma'.

Attribution to the Cause of Ownership: Principle and Talaq Application
States a principle about attribution to the cause of ownership and its talaq application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that attribution to the cause of ownership is equivalent to attribution to ownership itself and apply it to determine the validity of conditional talaq.

Core Idea
Attribution to the cause of ownership carries the same legal weight as attribution to ownership itself.

This passage from al-Hidayah introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi fiqh: when a legal consequence is attached to the cause of ownership (such as marriage or purchase), it is treated as if attached to ownership itself. This principle governs the validity of suspended divorces (talaq ta'liq) and manumission conditions. We will explore the principle and see how it resolves whether a conditional talaq takes effect upon marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling principle
The author states that attribution to the cause of ownership is like attribution to ownership itself. Understand what this means in terms of legal effect: any ruling that applies when something is attributed to ownership also applies when attributed to its cause.
This is a legal maxim (qa'ida fiqhiyya) used in various chapters.
2
Apply to talaq: the effective condition
When a man says to a non-mahram woman, 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' marriage is the cause of ownership (sabab al-milk). The talaq is attributed to the cause, so it takes effect immediately upon marriage.
Marriage is the cause of acquiring milk al-nikah (marital authority).
3
Contrast with an ineffective condition
If he says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' the condition is attached to an act unrelated to ownership or its cause. Even after marriage and entry, no talaq occurs because the condition was not linked to ownership or its cause.
At the time of the condition, the man was not the owner and did not attribute to the cause.
4
Extract the underlying condition
For a suspended talaq to be valid, the swearer must either own the woman at the time of the condition or attribute the divorce to the cause of ownership. Otherwise, the condition is ineffective.
This is a condition (shart) for the validity of the ta'liq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْإِضَافَةَ إِلَى سَبَبِ الْمِلْكِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْإِضَافَةِ إِلَيْهِ . ترجمہ: بسبب ملک کی طرف منسوب کرنا ملکیت کی طرف منسوب کرنے کے درجے میں ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کسی شے کو ملکیت کے سبب کی طرف منسوب کرنا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ اس نے اس شے کو اپنی ملکیت کی طرف منسوب کیا ہے، تو جو حکم منسوب الی الملک کی صورت میں مرتب ہوتا ہے وہی حکم منسوب الی سبب الملک کی صورت میں بھی ہوگا، مثلاً اگر کوئی غلام سے یہ کہے کہ إِنِ اشْتَرَيْتُكَ فَأَنْتَ حُرِّ اگر میں نے تجھے خریدا تو تو آزاد ہے، تو اس قائل کا یہ کلام بمنزلہ اس کلام کے ہے کہ إِنْ مَلَّكْتُكَ فَأَنْتَ حُرٌّ “ کیونکہ شراء ملک کا سبب ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے طلاق کو نکاح کی طرف منسوب کر دیا ، مثلاً کسی اجنبی عورت سے یہ کہا کہ إِنْ تَزَوَّجْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ “ کہ اگر میں نے تیرے ساتھ نکاح کیا تو تجھے طلاق ہے، تو جیسے ہی یہ شخص اس عورت سے نکاح کرے گا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اس لئے کہ اس شخص نے طلاق کو سبپ ملک کی طرف منسوب کیا ہے کیونکہ نکاح سبب ہے ملک کا ، اور ہر وہ شے جو سبپ ملک کی طرف منسوب ہو تو گویا ملکیت کی طرف منسوب ہونے کی مانند ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی یہ طلاق ملکیت کی طرف منسوب ہونے کی مانند ہوگی اور ایسی صورت میں طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے، لہذ امذکورہ صورت میں بھی طلاق بعد النکاح واقع ہوگی۔ لیکن اگر کسی شخص نے کسی اجنبیہ عورت سے کہا اگر تم گھر میں داخل ہوئی تو تمہیں طلاق ہے، اس کے بعد اس شخص نے اس عورت سے نکاح کیا اور وہ گھر میں داخل ہوئی تو اس پر طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ،اس لئے کہ حالف کا مخلوف علیہ کا مالک ہونا ضروری ہے ، اور اگر مالک نہ ہو تو سبپ ملک کی طرف اس کی نسبت کرے، یہاں حالف مالک بھی نہیں ہے اور سبپ ملک یعنی نکاح کی طرف بھی نسبت الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الأيمان في الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۸، ط: رحمانیه
Indeed, attribution to the cause of ownership is like attribution to ownership itself. Translation: Attributing something to the cause of ownership is equivalent to attributing it to ownership. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that attributing something to the cause of ownership is as if one has attributed it to his ownership; so the ruling that applies in the case of attribution to ownership will also apply in the case of attribution to the cause of ownership. For example, if someone says to a slave, 'If I buy you, you are free,' this statement is like saying, 'If I make you my property, you are free,' because purchase is a cause of ownership. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if someone attributes divorce to marriage, such as saying to a strange woman, 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' then as soon as he marries her, divorce will occur. This is because he has attributed divorce to the cause of ownership (marriage), and anything attributed to the cause of ownership is like being attributed to ownership itself. So in this case, divorce will occur after marriage. But if someone says to a strange woman, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' then after he marries her and she enters the house, divorce does not occur. This is because the swearer must be the owner of the thing sworn about; if he is not the owner, he must attribute it to the cause of ownership. Here, he is neither the owner nor has he attributed to the cause (marriage). Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Ayman fi al-Talaq, vol. 2, p. 398, Rahmaniyyah.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage introduces a key usul principle: attribution to the cause of ownership (sabab al-milk) carries the same legal weight as attribution to ownership itself. The principle is applied in talaq. If a man says to a non-mahram woman, 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' then upon marriage (the cause of acquiring ownership over her), divorce takes effect immediately. This is because marriage is the cause of ownership (milk al-nikah), and the condition is fulfilled when the cause occurs. However, if the same man says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' and later marries her and she enters, no divorce occurs. The reason: the swearer must either own the thing (here, the wife) at the time of the condition or attribute it to the cause of ownership. In this case, he is not yet the owner (since marriage hasn't occurred when the condition is uttered), and he did not attribute the divorce to the cause of ownership (he attributed it to house entry). Therefore, the condition is not attached to ownership or its cause, so no talaq occurs. This distinguishes between effective and ineffective conditions in talaq ta'liq.

Key Terms
الْإِضَافَة (al-idafah): Attribution or linking a condition to a cause.
سَبَب (sabab): The cause that produces ownership, e.g., purchase, marriage.
مِلْك (milk): Ownership, here referring to marital authority (milk al-nikah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to a non-mahram woman, 'If you enter this house, you are divorced.' Then he marries her, and she enters the house. According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs immediately upon marriage
B) Divorce occurs when she enters the house after marriage
C) No divorce occurs
D) Divorce occurs only if he intended it upon marriage

Key Takeaway
For a suspended talaq to be valid, it must be linked either to the husband's ownership or to the cause of ownership; otherwise, the condition is ineffective.

Dispute: Whether the Second Husband Cancels Less Than Three Divorces
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reset of divorce count

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Hanafi imams on whether the second marriage resets the count of divorces, and apply the dispute to a concrete scenario.

Core Idea
The second husband may or may not reset partial divorces — the ruling depends on which Hanafi authority you follow.

This passage reveals a classic intra-Hanafi disagreement: when a woman who has been divorced once or twice marries another man and is divorced again, does her first husband regain the full right to three divorces? The answer splits the school. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying talaq rulings in cases of remarriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing positions
Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two Shaykhs) hold that the second husband cancels any number of divorces less than three, while Muhammad and Zufar hold that he only cancels three divorces.
The two Shaykhs are referred to as 'عندهم'.
2
Understand the core principle
The term 'yahdim' means to cancel or destroy the effect of previous divorces, resetting the count for the first husband upon remarriage.
Consummation of the second marriage is a standard condition, though not mentioned in this passage.
3
Analyze the concrete example
Work through the example: husband says 'You are divorced three times if you enter the house,' then gives two immediate divorces; wife marries another, is divorced, then returns to first husband and enters. According to the two Shaykhs, three divorces fall; according to Muhammad and Zufar, only one falls.
The example shows how immediate and conditional divorces interact.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the number of divorces that occur after remarriage: full three vs. the remaining count.
This affects the finality of the divorce.
5
Note the absence of tarjih
The text does not state which view is mufta bihi, but in later Hanafi practice, the view of the two Shaykhs is often given weight.
Be aware of the ikhtilaf and consult reliable fatwa sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۰) إِنَّ الزَّوْجَ الثَّانِي يَهْدِمُ مَا دُونَ الثَّلاثِ عِنْدَهُمَا وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ وَزُفَرَ لَا يَهْدِمُ.
ترجمہ شیخین کے نزدیک زوج ثانی تین سے کم طلاق کو کالعدم کر دیتا ہے اور امام محمد اور امام زفر کے نزدیک (زوج ثانی تین سے کم طلاق کو ) کالعدم نہیں کرتا۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب کوئی آدمی اپنی بیوی کو ایک یا دو طلاقیں دیدے پھر وہ عورت کسی دوسرے شخص سے شادی کرے پھر یہ شوہر ثانی طلاق دیدے، پھر وہ عورت شوہر اول سے شادی کرے تو شیخین کے نزدیک اب بھی شوہر اول کو تین طلاقوں کا اختیار ہے، یعنی شوہر ثانی تین سے کم طلاقوں کو بھی ختم کر دیتا ہے شیخین کے نزدیک ، جبکہ امام محمد رحمہ اللہ اور امام زفر رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک شو ہر اول کو اب صرف مابھی کا اختیار ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہا أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا إِنْ ذَخَلْتِ الدَّارَ پھر شوہر نے اس کو دو طلاقیں تنجیز آدیدیں،اس کے بعد اس عورت سے کسی دوسرے شخص نے شادی کی پھر شوہر ثانی نے بھی اس کو طلاق دی، پھر عدت گزارنے کے بعد اس نے شوہر اول سے شادی کی اور گھر میں داخل ہوگئی تو شیخین کے نزدیک تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی جبکہ اما محمد اور امام زفر رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک مابقی یعنی ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی، اختلاف کی وجہ مذکورہ اصول ہے۔
(80) Indeed, the second husband cancels what is less than three according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and according to Muhammad and Zufar, he does not cancel. Translation: According to the two Shaykhs, the second husband nullifies less than three divorces, and according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar, he does not nullify. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that when a man gives his wife one or two divorces, then that woman marries another person, then this second husband divorces her, then that woman remarries the first husband, then according to the two Shaykhs, the first husband still has the right to three divorces, i.e., the second husband removes even less than three divorces according to them, whereas according to Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) and Imam Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him), the first husband will only have the remaining (i.e., the number of divorces left after the previous ones). The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced three times if you enter the house,' then the husband gives her two divorces immediately, then another man marries her, then the second husband divorces her, then after the waiting period she marries the first husband and enters the house, then according to the two Shaykhs, three divorces occur, whereas according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar, only the remaining one divorce occurs. The reason for the difference is this principle.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a second marriage on the count of divorces. The principle states that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two Shaykhs), a second marriage cancels any number of divorces less than three, meaning that when a woman divorces once or twice, marries another man, and then returns to her first husband, the first husband retains the full right to pronounce three divorces. However, according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar, the second marriage does not cancel less than three; instead, the first husband only has the remaining number of divorces from his initial count. The author provides a practical example: a husband says, 'You are divorced three times if you enter the house,' then he immediately gives two effective divorces. The wife subsequently marries another man who divorces her, and after her waiting period, she remarries the first husband and enters the house. According to the majority (Sahibayn), three divorces occur because the second marriage reset the count. According to Muhammad and Zufar, only the remaining one divorce occurs. This dispute arises from differing interpretations of how much the second husband 'destroys' (yahdim) the prior divorces. The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but in Hanafi practice, the view of the Sahibayn is often given weight.

Key Terms
زوج ثاني (zawj thānī): The second husband whom a divorced woman marries after her first husband's divorce.
يهدم (yahdim): He cancels or destroys the effect of previous divorces, resetting the count.
تنجيز (tanjīz): Immediate, unconditional pronouncement of divorce (as opposed to ta'liq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if a husband gives his wife one divorce, she marries another man who divorces her, and then she remarries the first husband, how many divorces does the first husband have the right to give now?
A) None, because the one divorce remains uncancelled.
B) One, because only one divorce was given.
C) Three, because the second husband cancels less than three divorces.
D) Two, because the second husband halves the count.

Key Takeaway
The effect of a second marriage on the divorce count is not uniform across Hanafi authorities; the ikhtilaf turns on whether the reset applies to all or only to the full three.

Conditions for Valid Conditional Divorce or Emancipation
Stating the conditions for valid conditional divorce or freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios that make ta'liq valid and apply the rule when ownership is lost to determine the consequence.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce or freeing is valid only if the swearer owns or will own the subject.

This passage from Al-Hidayah and Al-Ikhtiyar lays out the Hanafi conditions for a valid ta'liq (conditional pronouncement). It explains when the consequence takes effect and when it does not, even if the oath is fulfilled. Understanding these rules prevents common errors in marital and slave emancipation matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Ta'liq (conditional divorce/freeing) is valid and binding when the swearer is the owner of the subject or attributes the condition to the cause of ownership.
2
List the two valid scenarios
First: immediate ownership (e.g., husband to wife, master to slave). Second: future ownership (e.g., to a stranger: 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or 'Every slave I buy is free').
3
Understand the effect of loss of ownership
If the swearer loses ownership after the oath (e.g., divorces or sells), the oath remains valid. If the condition occurs after loss of ownership, the oath is dissolved but no divorce or freedom takes effect.
4
Apply to a scenario
A husband tells his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' Then he divorces her (she becomes a stranger). Later she enters the house. Since the condition occurred outside ownership, the oath dissolves without effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَصِحُ التَّعْلِيقُ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْحَالِفُ مَالِكًا كَقَوْلِهِ لا مُرَأَتِهِ: إِنْ دَخَلْتِ الدَارَ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ، أَوْ يَقُولُ لِعَبْدِهِ: إِنْ كَلَّمْتَ زَيْدًا فَأَنْتَ حُرِّ، أَوْ يُضِيفُهُ إِلَى مِلْكِ كَقَوْلِهِ لَّا جُنَبِيَّةٍ: إِنْ تَزَوَّجْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ، أَوْ كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ أَوْ كُلُّ عَبْدِ أَشْتَرِيهِ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ وَزَوَالُ الْمِلْكِ لَا يُبْطِلُ الْيَمِينَ، فَإِنْ وُجِدَ الشَّرْطُ فِي مِلْكِ انْحَلَّتْ وَوَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ، وَإِنْ وُجِدَ فِي غَيْرِ مِلْكِ انْحَلَّتْ وَلَمْ يَقَعُ شَيْء.
الاختيار لتعليل المختار: كتاب الطلاق، تعليق الطلاق، ج ۳ ص ۱۴۰، ط: مطبعة المجلس القاهرة الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الأيمان في الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۴۰۰، ط: رحمانیه
إِذَا قَالَ إِنْ دَخَلْت الدَّارَ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا ثُمَّ نَجْزَهَا وَقَالَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا فَتَزَوَّجَتْ بِزَوْجٍ =
And the conditional oath is not valid unless the swearer is the owner, like his saying to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' or he says to his slave: 'If you speak to Zaid, you are free,' or he attributes it to ownership, like his saying to a stranger: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' or 'Every slave I buy is free.' And the loss of ownership does not invalidate the oath. So if the condition occurs within ownership, the oath is dissolved and divorce occurs; if it occurs outside of ownership, the oath is dissolved and nothing occurs. Al-Ikhtiyar li-Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Book of Divorce, Chapter on Conditional Divorce, vol. 3, p. 140, Cairo Council Press; Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter on Oaths in Divorce, vol. 2, p. 400, Rahmaniyya edition. When he says: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times,' then he... and he said 'You are divorced three times' then she married a husband...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage summarizes the Hanafi ruling on the validity of conditional divorce or emancipation (ta'liq). It states that a conditional oath is valid only under two scenarios: (1) The swearer is the owner at the time of the condition (e.g., a husband saying to his wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' or a master to his slave 'If you speak to Zaid, you are free'). (2) The condition is attributed to the cause of ownership (e.g., saying to a stranger 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or 'Every woman I marry is divorced' or 'Every slave I buy is free'). The passage clarifies that if the swearer loses ownership (e.g., divorces his wife or sells the slave) after the oath, the oath is not invalidated; it remains pending. If the condition occurs while the subject is owned, the divorce or freedom takes effect. If the condition occurs after ownership has ceased, the oath is dissolved but no divorce or freedom occurs. The passage then cites two authoritative Hanafi sources: Al-Ikhtiyar and Al-Hidayah. The final line appears incomplete, likely providing an example of a conditional three divorces and subsequent marriage to another husband, which connects to the earlier principle (80) about second husband.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): A conditional pronouncement of divorce or freeing, where the effect is tied to a future event.
يمين (yamīn): Oath; here it refers to the conditional statement that includes a condition and a consequence (divorce or freedom).
انحلت (inḥallat): The oath is dissolved or discharged upon occurrence of the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his slave, 'If you speak to Zaid, you are free.' He then sells the slave. Later, the slave speaks to Zaid. What is the ruling?
A) The slave becomes free because the oath was valid and the condition occurred.
B) The oath is dissolved but no freedom occurs because the condition occurred after ownership ended.
C) The oath is invalidated because the slave was sold.
D) The slave becomes free only if the buyer also consents.

Key Takeaway
For a valid ta'liq, the swearer must be the owner or attribute to future ownership; if the condition occurs after ownership is lost, the oath dissolves without legal consequence.

Second Husband Nullifies Fewer Than Three: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of a second marriage on prior divorces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad/Zufar regarding whether a second husband nullifies fewer than three divorces, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of this dispute.

Core Idea
A second husband wipes the slate clean for the first husband, but only in the Shaykhayn's view.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of a second marriage after a divorce. The core question: when a wife receives fewer than three divorces, remarries, and returns, does the first husband start anew or only retain remaining divorces? Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify thamarah al-khilaf and the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the ruling concerns a wife divorced fewer than three times who marries another man, is divorced by him, and returns to the first husband. The dispute is whether the second husband's marriage nullifies the prior divorces.
The act is not a specific ritual but a legal consequence of the second marriage.
2
State the Shaykhayn position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the second husband nullifies any number less than three, so the first husband regains full capacity to issue three divorces.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view.
3
State the Muhammad/Zufar position
Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar hold that the second husband only nullifies three divorces; fewer than three remain. Thus the first husband only retains the remaining number of divorces.
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply to the textual example
In the example: a husband gives two divorces conditionally, then immediately; wife marries, returns, and enters the house. Under Shaykhayn, three divorces fall due to the condition; under Muhammad/Zufar, only one falls.
The condition triggers the remaining divorces after the second husband's effect.
5
Note the tarjih (relied-upon view)
The later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Al-Kasani, Al-Hidayah) prefer the Shaykhayn's view. The mufta bihi is that the second husband nullifies less than three.
This determines how fatwa is given today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۰) إِنَّ الزَّوْجَ الثَّانِي يَهْدِمُ مَا دُونَ الثَّلاثِ عِنْدَهُمَا وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ وَزُفَرَ لَا يَهْدِمُ. 
ترجمہ شیخین کے نزدیک زوج ثانی تین سے کم طلاق کو کالعدم کر دیتا ہے اور امام محمد اور امام زفر کے نزدیک (زوج ثانی تین سے کم طلاق کو ) کالعدم نہیں کرتا۔ 
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب کوئی آدمی اپنی بیوی کو ایک یا دو طلاقیں دیدے پھر وہ عورت کسی دوسرے شخص سے شادی کرے پھر یہ شوہر ثانی طلاق دیدے، پھر وہ عورت شوہر اول سے شادی کرے تو شیخین کے نزدیک اب بھی شوہر اول کو تین طلاقوں کا اختیار ہے، یعنی شوہر ثانی تین سے کم طلاقوں کو بھی ختم کر دیتا ہے شیخین کے نزدیک ، جبکہ امام محمد رحمہ اللہ اور امام زفر رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک شو ہر اول کو اب صرف مابھی کا اختیار ہو گا۔ 
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہا أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا إِنْ ذَخَلْتِ الدَّارَ پھر شوہر نے اس کو دو طلاقیں تنجیز آدیدیں،اس کے بعد اس عورت سے کسی دوسرے شخص نے شادی کی پھر شوہر ثانی نے بھی اس کو طلاق دی، پھر عدت گزارنے کے بعد اس نے شوہر اول سے شادی کی اور گھر میں داخل ہوگئی تو شیخین کے نزدیک تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی جبکہ اما محمد اور امام زفر رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک مابقی یعنی ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی، اختلاف کی وجہ مذکورہ اصول ہے۔ 
الاختيار لتعليل المختار: كتاب الطلاق، تعليق الطلاق، ج ۳ ص ۱۴۰، ط: مطبعة المجلس القاهرة الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الأيمان في الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۴۰۰، ط: رحمانیه إِذَا قَالَ إِنْ دَخَلْت الدَّارَ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا ثُمَّ نَجْزَهَا وَقَالَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا فَتَزَوَّجَتْ بِزَوْجٍ آخَرَ ثُمَّ عَادَتْ إِلَيْهِ فَدَخَلَتْ الدَّارَ لَمْ تَطْلُقُ عِنْدَنَا خِلَافًا لِزُفَرَ وَالشَّافِعِيِّ فِي قَوْلِ أَمَّا لَوْ أَبَانَهَا بِهِنتَيْنِ قَبْلَ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ الدَّارَ وَالْمَسْأَلَهُ بِحَالِهَا ثُمَّ تَزَوَّجَهَا بَعْدَ زَوْجِ آخَرَ ثُمَّ دَخَلَتْ الدَّارَ طَلْقَتُ ثَلاثًا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخَيْنِ، وَأَصْلُهُ أَنَّ الزَّوْجَ الثَّانِيَ يَهْدِمُ مَا دُونَ الثَّلَاثِ عِنْدَهُمَا فَتَعُودُ إِلَيْهِ بِالثَّلَاثِ ثُمَّ بِدُخُولِهَا الدَّارَ طَلُقَتْ ثَلاثًا وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ وَزُفَرَ وَالْأَئِمَّةِ الثَّلَاثَةِ لَا يَهْدِمُ الزَّوْجُ مَا دُونَهَا فَتَعُودُ إِلَيْهِ بِمَا بَقَی.
مجمع الأنهر في شرح ملتقى الأبحر : كتاب الطلاق، تعليق الطلاق، ج ۱ ص ۴۲۵، ط: دار إحياء التراث العربي 
الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الأيمان فى الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۴۰۱، ط: رحمانیه
(80) Indeed, the second husband nullifies what is less than three, according to the two Shaykhs; and according to Muhammad and Zufar, he does not nullify. Translation: According to the Shaykhayn, the second husband nullifies divorce less than three; according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar, he does not nullify. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that when a man gives his wife one or two divorces, then that woman marries another man, then that second husband divorces her, then she remarries the first husband, then according to the Shaykhayn, the first husband still has the option of three divorces, i.e., the second husband also nullifies less than three according to the Shaykhayn, while according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar, the first husband will only have the remaining number of divorces. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband said to his wife, 'You are divorced thrice if you enter the house,' then the husband gave two divorces in an immediate manner, then the woman married another man, then the second husband divorced her, then after the waiting period she married the first husband and entered the house, then according to the Shaykhayn three divorces occur, while according to Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar, only the remaining one divorce occurs. The reason for the difference is the mentioned principle.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a core intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a second marriage on prior divorces. The ruling in question: if a man gives his wife fewer than three divorces (one or two), and she subsequently marries another man, is divorced by him, and after her iddah returns to the first husband, how many divorces remain with the first husband? The text states that according to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the second husband nullifies everything less than three, meaning that upon remarriage, the first husband regains full capacity to issue all three divorces. In contrast, Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar hold that the second husband only nullifies three divorces, not fewer; thus the first husband retains only the remaining number of divorces he had not used. The passage then illustrates this with a practical example: a husband makes a conditional pronouncement of three divorces (if she enters the house), but before the condition is fulfilled, he gives two immediate divorces. The wife then marries another, is divorced, and after returning to the first husband, enters the house. The Shaykhayn rule that all three divorces fall due to the condition, because the second husband wiped the slate clean. Muhammad and Zufar say only one divorce falls (the remainder). The text cites Al-Ikhtiyar and Al-Hidayah to support the discussion. This dispute is well-known; the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the view of the Shaykhayn, that the second husband nullifies any number less than three, as stated in later authoritative works like Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
زوج ثانی (zawj thānī): The second husband that a divorced woman marries after her first marriage is dissolved.
يهدم (yahdimu): To demolish or nullify; here it means that the second husband's marriage and divorce effectively erase the prior divorces, so the first husband starts anew.
مادون الثلاث (mā dūna al-thalāth): What is less than three divorces, i.e., one or two divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if a husband gives his wife two divorces, and she later marries another man, is divorced by him, and then returns to the first husband, how many divorces does the first husband still have the right to give?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorces, because the marriage is permanent

Key Takeaway
The second husband nullifies fewer than three divorces according to the Shaykhayn, which is the relied-upon view, restoring the first husband's full capacity for divorce.

The Hanafi Definition of Intercourse and Its Role in Conditional Divorce
Defining intercourse and applying it to conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of intercourse and apply it to determine when a conditional divorce takes effect.

Core Idea
Intercourse in Hanafi law is defined exclusively as insertion of the male organ into the female organ, triggering full legal consequences.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes the precise legal definition of jima' (intercourse). In Hanafi fiqh, this definition is crucial for determining when conditional divorces, dower obligations, and penalties apply. The author then illustrates the rule with a concrete example: a husband conditionally divorcing his wife upon intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
The passage defines intercourse as 'idkhāl al-farj fī al-farj' (insertion of the genitals into the genitals). Understand that mere contact is insufficient; actual penetration is required.
This is a technical definition distinct from everyday language.
2
Apply to a conditional divorce scenario
Consider a husband who says, 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced.' The condition is tied to the occurrence of intercourse as legally defined.
The condition is suspended on a future act.
3
Determine the trigger point
The passage states that as soon as the circumcised parts meet (intiqā' al-khitanayn), meaning the glans disappears into the vagina, the condition is fulfilled and divorce occurs immediately.
No additional duration or ejaculation is needed.
4
Identify post-divorce consequences
If intercourse occurs after a final divorce, it is considered zina with shubha (doubt) due to the prior marriage. The hadd penalty is dropped, but the dower equivalent (mahr mithl, called 'uqr) becomes obligatory as a financial penalty.
This illustrates the legal effects of the definition beyond divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۱) إِنَّ الْجِمَاعَ إِدْحَالُ الْفَرْجِ فِي الْفَرْجِ .
ترجمہ: ایک شرم گاہ کو دوسری شرم گاہ میں داخل کرنے کا نام جماع ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جماع نام ہے شوہر کی شرمگاہ کا عورت کی شرمگاہ میں داخل ہونے کا، یعنی جب شوہر اپنی شرمگاہ کو عورت کی شرمگاہ میں داخل کر دے اگر چہ ٹھہراؤ نہ ہو تو اس پر جماع کا حکم مرتب ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے یہ کہا إِذَا جَامَعْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلاثًا “ کہ اگر میں نے تیرے ساتھ جماع کیا تو تجھے تین طلاق، اس کے بعد شوہر نے جماع کیا تو جیسے ہی انتقائے ختانین ہو جائے یعنی عورت کی شرمگاہ سے مرد کی شرمگاہ ہل جائے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی، اگر شوہر ایک گھڑی ٹھہرے تو عقر یعنی مہر مثل واجب نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ مہر مثل یا حد اس جماع سے واجب ہوں گی جو طلاق کے بعد واقع ہو، یہ جو مباشرت ہوئی وہ سابقہ عقد نکاح کی وجہ سے ہے۔ ہاں اگر یہ شخص اپنے عضو کو نکال کر دوبارہ داخل کرتا ہے تو اس پر مہر مثل واجب ہوگا، کیونکہ جماع طلاق کے بعد پایا گیا ہے، اور طلاق کے بعد جماع کرنے کی وجہ سے مذکورہ ولی حرام اور نا جائز ہوئی اور وہی حرام دو چیزوں سے خالی نہیں ہوتی ، (۱) حد (۲) جرمانہ کی صورت میں مہر مثل ۔ یہاں شبہ کی وجہ سے حد ساقط ہو جائے گی لیکن جر مانہ مہرمشل لا زم ہو گا۔ 
مجمع الأنهر في شرح ملتقى الأبحر : كتاب الطلاق، تعليق الطلاق، ج ۱ ص ۴۲۵، ط: دار إحياء التراث العربي
الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الأيمان فى الطلاق، ج ۲ ص ۴۰۱، ط: رحمانیه
وَالْحُرْمَةِ إِذْ مَعْنَى الْجِمَاعِ حُصُولُ الالْتِدَادَ بِمُمَاسَّةِ الْفَرْجَيْنِ وَقَدْ وُجِدَ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يَجِبُ
(81) Indeed, intercourse is the insertion of the genitals into the genitals. Translation: The name of intercourse is the insertion of one genitals into the other genitals. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that intercourse is defined as the husband's genitals entering the woman's genitals; when the husband inserts his genitals into the woman's genitals, even if there is no pause, the ruling of intercourse applies. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the husband said to his wife, 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced thrice,' and then the husband had intercourse, as soon as the circumcised parts meet (the man's genitals disappear into the woman's genitals), divorce occurs. If the husband remains for a moment, 'uqr (dower equivalent) is not obligatory because the dower equivalent or hadd becomes obligatory from intercourse that occurs after divorce; this intercourse occurred due to the previous marriage contract. However, if this person removes his organ and reinserts it, then the dower equivalent becomes obligatory, because the intercourse occurred after divorce, and that act of intercourse after divorce is forbidden and impermissible, and that forbidden act is not free from two things: (1) hadd, (2) penalty in the form of dower equivalent. Here, due to doubt, the hadd is dropped, but the penalty of dower equivalent becomes binding.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the legal meaning of 'jima' (intercourse) for Hanafi fiqh. The ruling: intercourse is the insertion (idkhal) of the male organ (farj) into the female organ (farj). This is a technical definition used to determine when legal consequences apply, such as divorce, dower, or hadd. The text explains that mere contact (mumassa) without insertion is not sufficient. The purpose of mentioning this definition in the context of conditional divorce is to clarify when a condition like 'if I have intercourse with you' is fulfilled. The author then applies this to a case: a husband says, 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced three times.' When he inserts his organ into hers, even momentarily (without staying), divorce occurs immediately upon meeting of the circumcised parts (takhtim). The text also discusses the question of dower (mahr mithl) and criminal penalty (hadd). If the intercourse occurs after divorce, it is zina (fornication), but due to the shubha (doubt) of the previous marriage, the hadd penalty is dropped, but the dower equivalent (mahr mithl) is still obligatory as a financial penalty. The passage concludes with a quote from Majma' al-Anhur and Al-Hidayah to reinforce the definition: the meaning of intercourse is the occurrence of concealment (iltidad) through the contact of the two genitals.

Key Terms
جماع (jimā'): Intercourse, defined textually as insertion of the male genitals into the female genitals, even momentarily.
إدحال الفرج في الفرج (idkhāl al-farj fī al-farj): Insertion of the genitals into the genitals, the technical legal definition of intercourse.
انتقائے ختانين (intiqā' al-khitanayn): The meeting of the two circumcised parts, i.e., the disappearance of the glans into the vagina, a key indicator of insertion.
عقر ('uqar): The dower equivalent (mahr mithl) that becomes obligatory for intercourse after divorce due to the forbidden nature of the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, at what point does intercourse legally occur triggering a conditional divorce?
A) When the husband and wife are alone together
B) When the male organ is fully inserted into the female organ, even momentarily
C) When there is skin contact between the two
D) Only after ejaculation

Key Takeaway
Intercourse in Hanafi fiqh is technically defined as insertion of the male organ into the female organ, and this definition is used to determine legal outcomes in divorce, dower, and penal matters.

Definition of Jima' and Its Effect on Conditional Divorce
Definition and application of jima' to conditional divorce with dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of jima', explain why conditional divorce takes effect at mere insertion, and compare the positions of the Shaykhayn and Muhammad regarding the second husband's effect on remaining divorces.

Core Idea
Jima' is mere insertion; conditional divorce fires at insertion, and the second husband's consummation resets the divorce count according to the Shaykhayn.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes the Hanafi definition of jima' (intercourse) as the insertion of the male organ into the female organ, irrespective of completion or ejaculation. This definition has immediate consequences for conditional divorce: if a husband conditions divorce on intercourse, the divorce occurs at the moment of insertion. The passage then delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a second husband's consummation 'demolishes' less than three divorces, affecting the outcome when a wife re-enters a condition after remarrying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of jima'
Locate the explicit statement: 'إِنَّ الْجِمَاعَ إِدْحَالُ الْفَرْجِ فِي الْفَرْجِ' – jima' is insertion of the private part into the private part. Note that this is a literal, physical definition without requiring completion or ejaculation.
This definition is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is foundational for many rulings.
2
Apply the definition to conditional divorce
In a conditional divorce (e.g., 'if I have intercourse with you, you are divorced'), the divorce takes effect at the moment of insertion, not at ejaculation or withdrawal. The condition is fulfilled by the act of insertion itself.
Contrast with other schools that may require full penetration or emission.
3
Analyze the discussion on hadd vs mahr
The passage explains that even though insertion occurs, if there is shubha (doubt) such as a mistaken belief of marriage, the criminal hadd (punishment for zina) is not applied. However, a compensatory mahr (called 'uqr) becomes wajib to protect the respected vulva.
This shows the Hanafi principle of prioritizing compensation when punitive measures are prevented.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the second husband
The scenario: a wife is divorced twice before entering a house (condition), then marries another husband, consummates, and later returns to the first husband. According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the second husband demolishes less than three divorces, so the wife returns with a full set of three, and entering the house triggers three divorces. According to Muhammad, Zufar, and others, the second husband only demolishes three divorces, so she returns with one remaining divorce, and entering triggers only one.
Dispute is based on the purpose of the second husband: to remove total prohibition (Shaykhayn) vs. to only remove the effect of three divorces (Muhammad).

Source Text

إِنَّ الْجِمَاعَ إِدْحَالُ الْفَرْجِ فِي الْفَرْجِ. وَالْحُرْمَةِ إِذْ مَعْنَى الْجِمَاعِ حُصُولُ الالْتِدَادِ بِمُمَاسَّةِ الْفَرْجَيْنِ وَقَدْ وُجِدَ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ وَاحِدٌ وَهُوَ قَضَاءُ الشَّهْوَةِ فَكَانَ الْجِمَاعُ وَاحِدًا مِنْ وَجْهٍ وَأَوَّلُهُ غَيْرُ مُوجِبٍ لِلْحَدِّ فَامْتَنَعَ وُجُوبُهُ فَوَجَبَ الْعُقْرُ إِذْ الْبُضْعُ الْمُحْتَرَمُ لَا يُصَانُ إِلَّا بِضَمَانِ جَابِرٍ أَوْ بِحَدٍّ زَاجِرٍ فَإِذَا امْتَنَعَ الْحَدُّ لِلَّهِ تَعَيَّنَ الْمَهْرُ، لِأَنَّهُ يَجِبُ مَعَ الشُّبْهَةِ. وَجْهُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ أَنَّ الْجِمَاعَ إِدْخَالُ الْفَرْجِ فِي الْفَرْجِ وَلَمْ يُوجَدْ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ الطَّلْقَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ وَالْعِتْقِ، لِأَنَّ الْإِدْخَالَ لَا دَوَامَ لَهُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ لِدَوَامِهِ حُكْمُ الِابْتِدَاءِ، وَلِهَذَا لَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يُدْخِلُ دَابَّتَهُ الْإِصْطَبْلَ وَهِيَ فِيهِ لَا يَحْنَثُ بِإِمْسَاكِهَا فِيهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا أَخْرَجَ ثُمَّ أَوْلَجَ، لِأَنَّهُ وُجِدَ الْجِمَاعُ فِيهِ حَقِيقَةً بَعْدَ ثُبُوتِ الْحُرْمَةِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ نَظَرًا إِلَى اتِّحَادِ الْمَجْلِسِ وَالْمَقْصُودِ وَهُوَ قَضَاءُ الشَّهْوَةِ فَإِذَا امْتَنَعَ الْحَدُّ وَجَبَ الْمَهْرُ لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا. أَمَّا لَوْ أَبَانَهَا بِثِنْتَيْنِ قَبْلَ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ الدَّارَ وَالْمَسْأَلَةُ بِحَالِهَا ثُمَّ تَزَوَّجَهَا بَعْدَ زَوْجٍ آخَرَ ثُمَّ دَخَلَتِ الدَّارَ طَلُقَتْ ثَلَاثًا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخَيْنِ، وَأَصْلُهُ أَنَّ الزَّوْجَ الثَّانِيَ يَهْدِمُ مَا دُونَ الثَّلَاثِ عِنْدَهُمَا فَتَعُودُ إِلَيْهِ بِالثَّلَاثِ ثُمَّ بِدُخُولِهَا الدَّارَ طَلُقَتْ ثَلَاثًا وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ وَزُفَرَ وَالْأَئِمَّةِ الثَّلَاثَةِ لَا يَهْدِمُ الزَّوْجُ مَا دُونَهَا فَتَعُودُ إِلَيْهِ بِمَا بَقِيَ.
Verily, jima' is the insertion of the private part into the private part. And (the discussion of) prohibition: since the meaning of jima' is the occurrence of contact by touching of the two private parts, and it has occurred, except that the hadd is not obligatory because the purpose is one, which is the fulfillment of desire, so the intercourse is one in one respect, and its beginning does not necessitate the hadd, so its obligation is prevented, so the compensation (mahr) becomes obligatory, because the respected vulva is only protected by a compensating guarantee or by a deterrent hadd. When the hadd for Allah is prevented, the mahr is determined, because it is obligatory even with doubt. The face of the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that jima' is the insertion of the private part into the private part, and that has not occurred after the three divorces and emancipation, because insertion has no continuance such that its continuance has the ruling of the beginning. Hence, if one swore not to bring his animal into the stable and it is already inside, he does not break his oath by keeping it inside, unlike if he took it out and then inserted it, because jima' is truly found after the establishment of prohibition, except that the hadd is not obligatory considering the unity of the session and the purpose, which is the fulfillment of desire. So when the hadd is prevented, the mahr becomes obligatory due to what we mentioned. As for if he divorced her with two (divorces) before she entered the house, and the case remains as it is, then she married another husband and then entered the house, she is thrice divorced according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Their basis is that the second husband demolishes what is less than three according to them, so she returns to him with three (divorces), then upon entering the house she is thrice divorced. According to Muhammad, Zufar, and the three imams, the husband does not demolish what is less than it, so she returns to him with what remains.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins by defining jima' as the insertion of the male organ into the female organ. The author applies this definition to a conditional divorce: if a husband says, 'If I have intercourse with you, you are thrice divorced,' then divorce occurs immediately upon insertion, even without completion. The text then discusses a scenario where after three divorces, the couple reunite illegally (with shubha), and the issue of hadd and mahr arises. The hadd is not imposed due to the shubha (doubt) of the session's unity, but mahr is obligatory as compensation. The passage includes a dispute between the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad, Zufar, and the three imams regarding whether a second husband after two divorces can 'demolish' those divorces, affecting the outcome of a conditional divorce upon entering a house. According to the Shaykhayn, the second husband reduces the count to zero, so the wife returns with three divorces, and entering the house triggers three. According to Muhammad et al., the second husband does not diminish the count, so she returns with one remaining divorce, and entering triggers only one.

Key Terms
الْجِمَاع (al-jima'): Insertion of the male organ into the female organ; even without completion or ejaculation.
الْعُقْر (al-'uqr): Compensation (mahr mithl) paid for intercourse under shubha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a conditional divorce 'if I have intercourse with you' take effect?
A) At ejaculation
B) At insertion of the male organ into the female organ
C) Only after withdrawal
D) When both parties intend intercourse

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of jima' as mere insertion is decisive for conditional divorce, and the dispute over the second husband's effect on remaining divorces illustrates how different interpretations of purpose can yield different rulings.

The Principle of Istithna in Talaq: Speaking of the Remainder
Defines istithna as speaking of the remainder after exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of istithna to determine the number of divorces when an exception is made using 'illa'.

Core Idea
Istithna reduces the count; only the remainder after the exception is effective.

The principle of istithna (exception) is crucial in Islamic law, especially in divorce (talaq) where a husband may specify a number and then make an exception. This lesson explains the Hanafi rule that when you say 'three except one,' it is as if you said 'two.' We will explore the conditions and implications of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement
Recognize the phrase containing a number and an exception using 'illa' (e.g., 'thrice divorced except one').
The exception must be immediate and connected.
2
Subtract the exception
Remove the excepted number from the stated number mathematically.
3
Determine the remainder
If the remainder is a positive number less than the original, it is the effective count.
4
Rule on excess
If the exception equals or exceeds the original number, the entire statement is void (no divorce occurs).
Example: 'three except three' is void.
5
Apply to divorce
In talaq, the remainder number of divorces takes effect; the statement is valid only if a positive remainder exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْإِسْتِثْنَاءَ تَكَلَّمَ بِالْحَاصِل بَعْدَ الْإِسْتِثْنَاءِ.
Verily, istithna is speaking of what remains after the exception.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is a ruling statement defining the legal principle of istithna (exception). The passage states that when someone makes an exception, the legal effect applies only to the remainder after subtracting the excepted part. For example, if a husband says to his wife, 'You are thrice divorced except one,' it is as if he said 'You are twice divorced,' so two divorces occur. If he says 'except two,' one remains. If he says 'except three,' the entire statement is void because nothing remains. This principle is applied in conditional divorce cases.

Key Terms
الْإِسْتِثْنَاء (al-istithna): A legal exception using 'illa' to exclude part of a statement; the ruling applies only to the remaining amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife, 'You are thrice divorced except two,' how many divorces occur?
A) Three
B) Two
C) One
D) None

Key Takeaway
Istithna in talaq means only the remainder after subtraction counts; if the exception equals or exceeds the original, no divorce occurs.

Ruling on Hadd and Mahr After Triple Divorce and Manumission
States that no hadd but mahr is due for intercourse after triple divorce and manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd and mahr for intercourse after triple divorce and manumission, and explain the qiyas used to derive it.

Core Idea
No hadd for intercourse that began before complete prohibition; mahr is due as compensation.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a nuanced scenario: a man has intercourse with a woman after she has been divorced three times and then freed. The immediate question is whether the fixed punishment (hadd) for zina applies, and whether a dower (mahr) is due. The ruling clarifies that hadd is lifted due to the continuity of the act, but mahr becomes obligatory to protect the woman's sanctity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
What is the ruling on hadd and mahr for intercourse after triple divorce and manumission? Recognize that the act began before the prohibition was fully established.
2
Locate the ruling
No hadd of zina; mahr is wajib. The source states this clearly.
3
Understand the definitional basis
Intercourse is defined as insertion of the penis into the vagina. The act is considered one continuous session; the initial insertion occurred when intercourse was permissible.
The ruling relies on the fact that insertion has no continuity at the moment of prohibition.
4
Follow the analogy
The text analogizes to a person who swears not to put his animal into a stable while it is already inside. Keeping it there does not break the oath. Similarly, continuing intercourse that began before the complete prohibition does not constitute a new act of zina.
The illa (effective cause) is unity of session and purpose.
5
Recognize the legal maxim
The respected vulva is protected either by a deterrent hadd or a compensatory mahr. When hadd is prevented, mahr becomes wajib.
This maxim can be found in the source text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ وَاحِدٌ وَهُوَ قَضَاءِ الشَّهْوَةِ فَكَانَ الْحِمَاعُ وَاحِدًا مِنْ وَجْهِ وَأَوَّلُهُ غَيْرُ مُوجِبِ لِلْحَدِّ فَامْتَنَعَ وُجُوبُهُ فَوَجَبَ الْعُقْرُ إِذْ الْبُضْعُ الْمُحْتَرَمُ لَا يُصَانُ إِلَّا بِضَمَانِ جَابِرٍ أَوْ بِحَدٌ زَاجِرٍ فَإِذَا امْتَنَعَ الْحَد لِله تَعَيَّنَ المَهْرُ ، لأنَّهُ يَجِبُ مَعَ الشُّبُهَةِ وَجُهُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ أَنَّ الْجِمَاعَ إِدْخَالُ الْفَرْجِ فِي الْفَرْجِ وَلَمْ يُوجَدُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ الطَّلْقَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ وَالْعِتْقِ، لِأَنَّ الْإِدْخَالَ لَا دَوَامَ لَهُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ لِدَوَامِهِ حُكْمُ الابْتِدَاءِ ، وَلِهَذَا لَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يُدْخِلُ دَابَّتَهُ الْإِصْطَبُلَ وَهِيَ فِيهِ لَا يَحْتَتُ بِإِمْسَاكِهَا فِيهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا أَخْرَجَ ثُمَّ أَوْلَجَ ، لأنَّهُ وُجِدَ الْجِمَاعُ فِيهِ حَقِيقَةٌ بَعْدَ ثُبُوتِ الْحُرُمَةِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ نَظَرًا إِلَى اتِّحَادِ الْمَجْلِسِ وَالْمَقْصُودِ وَهُوَ قَضَاءِ الشَّهْوَةِ فَإِذَا امْتَنَعَ الْحَدُّ وَجَبَ الْمَهُرُ لِمَا ذَكَرُنَا.
Hadd is [not applied] because the purpose is one, which is satisfying lust, so the intercourse is considered one in some ways, and its first part does not necessitate hadd, so its obligation is prevented, and the dower becomes obligatory because the respected vulva is not protected except by a compensatory guarantee or a deterrent hadd. So when hadd for Allah is prevented, the mahr is determined, because it is obligatory along with doubt. The position of Zahir al-Riwayah is that intercourse is insertion of the penis into the vagina, and that was not found after the three divorces and manumission, because insertion has no continuity such that its continuity would have the ruling of initiation. For this reason, if a person swore not to put his animal into the stable and it was already inside, he is not considered to have broken his oath by keeping it there, contrary to if he took it out and then inserted it. Because intercourse was found in reality after the prohibition was established, but hadd is not required on account of the unity of session and purpose, which is satisfying lust. So when hadd is prevented, mahr is obligatory for what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a man has intercourse with a woman after she has been divorced three times and then manumitted (i.e., set free). The ruling is that the fixed punishment (hadd) for zina is not applied, but a dower (mahr) is obligatory. The reasoning is that the intercourse, though real, is considered as one act with a single purpose - satisfying lust. The first part of the act does not trigger hadd because the prohibition was not fully established at the start. Since the hadd is prevented due to doubt (shubha), the mahr becomes due because the woman's chastity is protected by either a compensatory payment (mahr) or a deterrent punishment (hadd). When hadd is prevented, mahr takes its place. The passage also notes the Zahir al-Riwayah position that intercourse is defined as insertion of the penis into the vagina, and this was not fully realized after the divorces and manumission because the act began before the complete prohibition. An analogy is drawn with a person who swears not to put his animal in the stable when it is already inside: keeping it there does not break the oath, but taking it out and re-entering does. Similarly, here the initial part of intercourse occurred when the woman was still permissible, so the later part under prohibition does not constitute a fresh new act of zina. Thus, no hadd, but mahr is due.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment for zina.
المهر (al-mahr): The compulsory payment (dower) due to a woman from a man for sexual access.
العقر (al-'uqr): Synonym for mahr in this context; the compensatory payment for sexual access without valid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a man who has intercourse with a woman after she has been divorced three times and then manumitted?
A) Hadd of zina is applied
B) Mahr is obligatory, but no hadd
C) Neither hadd nor mahr
D) The marriage is reinstated

Key Takeaway
No hadd is applied for intercourse on the same session if the act began before the complete prohibition; mahr becomes obligatory as compensation.

Istithna' in Divorce: The Rule of the Remainder
Defines istithna' and applies it to talaq with three scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of istithna' (exception) to determine the number of divorces that occur when a husband's statement includes an exception.

Core Idea
Istithna' means the ruling attaches to what remains — an exception of the whole from the whole is void.

This passage from al-Hidayah introduces a fundamental linguistic-legal principle: istithna' (exception) is about the remainder after the excepted part is removed. The author then applies this to divorce, showing how a husband's exception reduces the number of divorces — unless he tries to except the entire number, in which case the exception fails and the full number takes effect.

Guided Path
1
Define the Hanafi principle of istithna'
Identify the definition: istithna' is 'speaking about the remainder after the exception' (تكلم بالباقي). This means the legal effect applies only to what remains after subtracting the excepted part.
Contrast with definitions that treat istithna' as extraction (إخراج).
2
Apply to a simple debt example
See how 'I owe ten dirhams except one' is treated as 'I owe nine dirhams.' The exception reduces the original number, and the speech is about the remainder.
3
Transfer the rule to talaq statements
Take the husband's statement 'You are divorced three times except one.' The exception subtracts one from three, leaving two. The statement is treated as 'You are divorced twice.' Two divorces occur.
Similarly, 'except two' leaves one; 'except three' leaves zero – but zero remainder is invalid.
4
Recognize the boundary: exception of whole from whole
When the husband says 'except three,' the remainder is nothing. Since istithna' requires a remainder to be spoken about, the exception is legally invalid. The original statement ('thrice') stands, and three divorces occur.
Hanafis reject exception of whole from whole; some other schools may allow it in certain contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثناء (۸۲) إِنَّ الْإِسْتِثْنَاءَ تَكَلَّمَ بِالْحَاصِل بَعْدَ الْإِسْتِثْنَاءِ . ترجمہ: استثناء کے بعد حاصل شدہ مقدار کے تکلم کا نام استثناء ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ استثناء تكلم بالباقی کا نام ہے، یعنی استثناء کے بعد جو کچھ بچ جائے اس پر حکم مرتب ہو گا۔ مثلا عَلَيَّ لِفُلَانِ عَشَرَةُ دَرَاهِمَ إِلَّا وَاحِدَةً “یہ ایسا ہے گویا کہ قائل نے یوں کہا عَلَيَّ لِفُلَانِ تِسْعَةُ ذَرَاهِمَ“ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی سے یہ کہا أُنتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا إِلَّا وَاحِدَةً “ تو اس صورت میں دو طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی کیونکہ شوہر نے مذکورہ صورت میں تین سے ایک کو استثناء کیا ہے اور استثناء تكلم بالباقی کا نام ہے، تو شوہر کا یہ کلام ایسا ہے گویا کہ اس نے یوں کہا انتِ طالق ثنتین تو ایسی صورت میں دو طلاقیں واقع ہوتی ہیں اور اگر شوہر نے انتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا إِلَّا ثِنْتَيْنِ ، میں تین سے دو طلاقیں کم کی گئیں تو ایک طلاق باقی رہی ، لہذا ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی ، اور اگر انْتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا إِلَّا ثَلاثًا ، تو اس صورت میں چونکہ کل سے کل کا استثناء کیا ہے، اور یہ درست نہیں ہے، تو انتِ طَالِقٌ ثَلاثًا “ کی وجہ سے بیوی پر تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی۔
Chapter on Istithna'. (82) Indeed, Istithna' is speaking about the remainder after the exception. Translation: Istithna' is the name for speaking about the amount remaining after the exception. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that Istithna' is the name for speaking about the remainder; i.e., after the exception, the ruling applies to what remains. For example, 'I owe Zaid ten dirhams except one' is as if the speaker said 'I owe Zaid nine dirhams.' The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced three times except one,' then in this case two divorces occur because the husband excluded one from three, and Istithna' is speaking about the remainder. So his speech is as if he said 'You are divorced twice.' And if the husband says 'You are divorced three times except two,' then one divorce remains, so one divorce occurs. And if he says 'You are divorced three times except three,' then since he excluded the whole from the whole, and this is not valid, three divorces occur due to the statement 'You are divorced three times.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage explains the legal principle of istithna' (exception) and its application in divorce. The author defines istithna' as speaking about what remains after the exception. This is a foundational rule in Hanafi legal reasoning: when a statement contains an exception, the legal effect attaches to the part that remains after subtracting the exception. The example from debt illustrates this clearly: saying 'ten dirhams except one' is treated as saying 'nine dirhams.' The author then applies this to talaq. If a husband says 'You are divorced three except one,' the exception subtracts one, leaving two divorces effective. Similarly, 'except two' leaves one divorce effective. However, if the exception is of the whole from the whole (e.g., 'except three'), the exception is invalid because nothing remains to be the subject of the speech. In that case, the original statement without exception takes effect, and three divorces occur. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that an exception must leave something positive behind; otherwise it is nugatory.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Speaking about the remainder after the exception; i.e., the legal effect attaches to what remains after subtracting the excepted part.
تكلم بالباقي (takallum bil-baqi): The statement is considered to be about what remains after the exception, not about the original quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced three times except one,' how many divorces occur according to al-Hidayah?
A) Three divorces
B) Two divorces
C) One divorce
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
Istithna' operates on the remainder: exception of part is valid, exception of whole from whole is invalid and the original number stands.
