Wife of a Farr Inherits in Terminal Illness Divorce
States a ruling with condition and rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance for a wife divorced in terminal illness and identify the condition of iddah for its application.

Core Idea
A terminally ill husband cannot disinherit his wife by divorce if she is still in iddah.

In Hanafi law, a terminally ill divorce may be motivated by a desire to deprive the wife of inheritance. To protect her rights, the school rules that such a husband is a 'farr' (one who flees), and his wife inherits if she is still in her iddah at his death. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its ratio legis, and the crucial condition of iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A man divorces his wife while terminally ill. What is the ruling? The wife inherits from him.
The husband is called a farr because he 'flees' from giving her inheritance.
2
Understand the rationale
Why does the wife inherit? Because at the onset of terminal illness, the rights of potential heirs become attached to the property. The husband cannot unilaterally cancel those rights.
Marriage remains partially intact (من وجه) for inheritance purposes during iddah.
3
Note the condition
The wife inherits only if she is still in her iddah at the time of the husband's death. If iddah has expired, the marriage is fully severed and she loses the inheritance.
Iddah is the waiting period after divorce during which remarriage is prohibited.
4
Distinguish from other schools
Other schools may not grant inheritance after a ba'in divorce even in terminal illness. The Hanafi position is a protective measure.
This ikhtilaf stems from differing views on whether divorce can sever inheritance rights after illness onset.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۳) إِنَّ اِمْرَأَةَ الْفَارِ تَرِتْ. ترجمہ: میراث سے محروم کرنے والی کی بیوی وارث ہوگی۔ تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ امرأة فاریعنی جو شخص مرض الموت میں اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دے تو یہ شخص طلاق دے کر وراثت سے بھاگ رہا ہے، اس لئے اس کو فائ“ کہتے ہیں ،اس کی بیوی کو وراثت ملے گی ، کیونکہ جب یہ آدمی مرض موت میں مبتلا ہوا تو ورثاء کے حقوق اس کے مال کے ساتھ متعلق ہو گئے ، اب یہ اس حق کو ختم کرنا چاہتا ہے، حالانکہ یہ اس کے اختیار میں نہیں ہے،لہذا بیوی وارث ہوگی ۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی کو مرض الموت کی حالت میں طلاق بائن دیدی پھر وہ مر گیا ، جبکہ عورت عدت میں ہے تو اس صورت میں عورت کو وراثت ملے گی ، کیونکہ یہ شخص فار ہے اور فار کی بیوی کو وراثت ملتی ہے اور نکاح من وجہ باقی رہتا ہے، البتہ اگر مذکورہ صورت میں یہ آدمی عدت کے گزرنے کے بعد مر گیا تو پھر عورت کو وراثت نہیں
(83) Indeed, the wife of the farr inherits. Translation: The wife of the one who deprives [by fleeing] inheritance will inherit. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that 'wife of a farr' refers to a man who divorces his wife while in terminal illness; this man, by divorcing, is fleeing from inheritance, so he is called a farr. His wife will inherit because when this person was afflicted with terminal illness, the rights of the heirs became attached to his property. Now he wants to terminate this right, but it is not within his power. Therefore, the wife inherits. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if someone gives his wife a ba'in divorce while in terminal illness, then he dies while the woman is in her iddah, then in this case the woman will receive inheritance, because this person is a farr and the wife of a farr inherits, and the marriage remains partially intact. However, if in the mentioned case the man dies after the iddah has passed, then the woman does not inherit.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses the rule of the wife of a 'farr' (literally, one who flees). In Hanafi law, if a man divorces his wife while terminally ill (i.e., in marad al-mawt), he may be attempting to deprive her of inheritance. Such a person is called a farr, and the ruling is that his divorced wife still inherits from him if she is still in her iddah at the time of his death. The reasoning is that upon the onset of terminal illness, the rights of potential heirs attach to the property. The husband cannot unilaterally extinguish those rights by divorce. The marriage is considered to continue in some sense (من وجہ) for inheritance purposes. If the wife's iddah has expired before the husband's death, then the inheritance right is lost because the link of marriage is fully severed.

Key Terms
الفار (al-farr): A person who divorces his wife in terminal illness with the intent to deprive her of inheritance.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period after divorce during which a woman cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man divorces his wife with a ba'in divorce while terminally ill and dies after her iddah has expired. Does the wife inherit?
A) Yes, because he was a farr
B) No, because the iddah expired
C) Yes, because divorce in illness is invalid
D) No, because ba'in divorce severs all ties

Key Takeaway
A terminally ill husband cannot disinherit his wife through a ba'in divorce if she remains in her iddah at his death; the wife inherits as a protective measure.

The Wife of a Man Fleeing from Inheritance: Imra'at al-Fār
Explains that the wife of a man who divorces in death illness inherits due to attached rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wife's inheritance when divorced in death illness and identify the rationale based on attachment of rights.

Core Idea
A terminal divorce cannot defeat the wife's inheritance right—the marriage endures min wajh.

This lesson explains a special Hanafi ruling that protects the wife's inheritance rights when a husband divorces her during his terminal illness. The principle of 'imra'at al-fār' (wife of a fleeing man) ensures that the husband cannot use divorce to deprive her of her share. Understanding this requires grasping the concept of rights attaching upon death illness and the role of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the husband's act: giving a bā'in divorce during terminal illness. The ruling: the wife inherits if she is in her 'iddah at his death.
2
Understand the rationale: attachment of rights
When terminal illness begins, the rights of all potential heirs become attached to the estate. The husband cannot unilaterally extinguish these rights by divorce.
This is the key legal hook: the divorce is ineffective for inheritance.
3
Apply to the scenario
If the wife is in 'iddah at death, she inherits because the marriage is considered partially subsisting (min wajh). If the 'iddah has expired, the marriage is fully severed and she does not inherit.
The condition of 'iddah is crucial: after 'iddah, the wife is a stranger.
4
Note the Hanafi reliance on istihsan
This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent injustice, overriding the general rule that bā'in divorce ends inheritance.
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi method.

And this is not correct. Due to 'You are divorced three times,' three divorces occur. (83) Indeed, the wife of the fleeing man inherits. Translation: The wife of the one who deprives of inheritance will inherit. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the 'woman of the fleeing man' refers to a person who gives divorce to his wife during death illness; this person is fleeing from inheritance through divorce, so he is called 'fār'. His wife will inherit, because when this person falls into death illness, the rights of the heirs become attached to his wealth. Now he wants to extinguish this right, whereas it is not in his control. Therefore the wife will inherit. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if someone gives a bā'in divorce to his wife during death illness, then he dies while the woman is in her 'iddah, then in this case the woman will inherit, because this person is a fār, and the wife of a fār inherits, and the marriage remains partially valid. However, if in the mentioned case this person dies after the 'iddah has passed, then the woman does not inherit.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of divorce in illness. The principle is that the wife of a man who divorces her while terminally ill inherits from him if she is still in her 'iddah at his death. The reasoning is that such a divorce is an attempt to flee from the wife's inheritance right, which has already attached to his estate upon the onset of terminal illness. Since the husband cannot validly defeat this right by his own action, the divorce is considered ineffective for inheritance purposes, and the marriage is deemed partially subsisting (min wajh). This is based on istihsan. The passage also distinguishes the case where the 'iddah has expired: then the marriage is completely severed, and no inheritance is due.

Key Terms
فار (fār): A person who divorces his wife during terminal illness to deprive her of inheritance.
امرأة الفار (imra'at al-fār): The wife of a man who divorces in death illness; she inherits despite the divorce.
مرض الموت (maraḍ al-mawt): A disease from which death is feared, causing the attachment of inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Yes, only if she was not the cause of the illness.

Key Takeaway
A wife divorced in terminal illness inherits if she remains in iddah at the husband's death, based on the principle that rights attached upon illness cannot be defeated.

The Rule of Exception (Istithnā') in Divorce: All from All is Invalid
Defines the rule of exception (istithnā') in divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an exception in a divorce pronouncement is invalid and state the resulting ruling.

Core Idea
An exception that excludes everything is void; the original divorce statement stands in full.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of an exception (istithnā') in divorce depends on whether any part of the original statement remains after the exception. This lesson explores a key rule from Al-Hidayah and its commentary: if a husband says 'You are divorced three times except three times,' the exception is invalid because it leaves nothing. Consequently, the wife is divorced three times. Understanding this principle prevents misuse of exceptions to nullify divorce pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the rule
Recognize that the passage defines the legal effect of an exception (istithnā') in statements, particularly in divorce oaths.
The rule applies to any statement with an exception, not only divorce.
2
Understand the definition of istithnā'
The passage defines istithnā' as 'speech about what remains after the exception.' This implies that the exception must leave something of the excepted-from to be valid.
The word 'thuniyā' is synonymous with istithnā'.
3
Apply the rule: valid vs. invalid exception
An exception is valid if it excludes only a part, leaving the rest. It is invalid if it excludes the entire subject, leaving nothing to which the original statement can refer.
The Quranic example (1000 years except 50) shows a valid exception leaving 950 years.
4
Examine the divorce example
The passage gives the case: 'You are divorced three times except three times.' Since the exception excludes all three divorces, it is invalid, and the original three divorces take effect.
This ruling is consistent across all Hanafi authorities (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الأصل أن الاستثناء تكلم بالحاصل بعد الثنيا بضم الثاء المثلثة وسكون النون وهو اسم بمعنى الاستثناء ، ومعناه إن صدر الكلام بعد الاستثناء يصير عبارة عما وراء الاستثناء يدل عليه قوله تعالى (فَلَبِتْ فِيهِمُ أَلْفَ سَنَةٍ إِلَّا خَمْسِينَ عَامًا) معناه: لبث فيهم تسع مائة وخمسين عاماً (هو الصحيح) احترز عما قال البعض: إنه إخراج، وفيه معنى المعارضة، وهو صفة الأصول. (ومعناه) أى معنى الثنيا أنه تكلم بالمستثنى منه، إذ لا فرق بين قول القائل لفلان على درهم، وبين قوله عشرة إلا تسعة فيصح استثناء البعض من الجملة، لأنه يبقى التكلم بالبعض بعده ولا يصح استثناء الكل من الكل، لأنه لا يبقى بعده شيء ليصير متكلما به وصارفا اللفظ إليه الضمير في بعده، يرجع إلى استثناء الكل، وفي: به يرجع إلى شيء ، وكذا في إليه وهذا كما إذا قال لامرأته: أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا ثلاثاً تطلق ثلاثاً لبطلان الاستثناء. البناية شرح الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الأيمان في الطلاق، فصل في الاستثناء، ج ۵ ص ۴۲۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية: كتاب الطلاق باب طلاق المريض، ج ۲ ص ۴۰۳، ط: رحمانیه
The principle is that exception (istithnā') is speech about what remains after the exception (al-thuniyā). 'Al-thuniyā' (with dammah on the thā' and sukūn on the nūn) is a noun meaning exception. Its meaning is that the utterance after the exception becomes an expression of what is beyond the exception. This is indicated by His statement: 'So he stayed among them a thousand years except fifty years' – meaning: he stayed among them nine hundred and fifty years. This is the correct view, avoiding what some said: that it is exclusion, and in it is the meaning of opposition, and it is a description of principles. And its meaning, i.e., the meaning of al-thuniyā, is that he speaks of the excepted-from. For there is no difference between one saying 'So-and-so owes me a dirham' and his saying 'ten except nine'. Thus it is valid to except part from a whole, because speech about the part remains after it. And it is not valid to except the whole from the whole, because nothing remains after it for him to speak about and to direct the word to. The pronoun in 'after it' refers to excepting the whole, and in 'to it' refers to something, and similarly in 'to it'. And this is like when a man says to his wife: 'You are divorced three times except three times' – she is divorced three times due to the invalidity of the exception.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Al-Bināyah commentary on Al-Hidāyah, explaining the rules of exception (istithnā') in oaths, specifically in divorce. It clarifies that an exception must leave something of the excepted-from. If someone says 'You are divorced three times except three times', the exception is invalid because it excludes the entire subject, leaving nothing to which the statement applies. Consequently, the wife is divorced three times. The example from the Quran (1000 years except 50) shows that after exception, what remains is 950 years. This principle is essential in understanding how divorce pronouncements with exceptions are evaluated: a valid exception must exclude part, not all.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): A linguistic exception that modifies a preceding statement to exclude part.
ثنيا (thuniyā): The noun form of exception, referring to the excluded part or the act of excepting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced three times except three times.' What is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) She is not divorced because the exception negates all divorces.
B) She is divorced three times because the exception is invalid.
C) She is divorced once because of ambiguity.
D) The divorce is suspended until clarification.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an exception that excludes the entire subject is invalid, leaving the original statement fully operative.

The Cause of Flight: Extending Inheritance to High-Risk Situations
Expands the flight principle to high-risk situations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the cause of flight (firār) in Hanafi fiqh and apply it to scenarios where death is the dominant expectation, including non-illness situations.

Core Idea
Flight is established by any high-death-risk situation, not just illness.

In Hanafi inheritance law, a man who divorces his wife while death is imminent (firār) does so to disinherit her. By istihsān, the Hanafi school treats the divorce as void for inheritance purposes. This lesson explores the cause ('illah) behind this ruling and its extension beyond terminal illness to situations like duels, executions, and sieges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue: flight (al-firār)
Understand that flight is when a husband divorces his wife in a life-threatening situation to deprive her of inheritance.
2
Determine the cause ('illah) of flight
The cause is the wife's right becoming attached to the husband's wealth due to the husband being in a state where death is the dominant expectation. This right attaches through a life-threatening illness or its equivalent.
The cause is the high probability of death, not the specific illness.
3
Expand the cause to analogous situations
The Hanafi school uses qiyās (analogy) to extend the ruling from terminal illness to any situation with a similar high risk of death, such as being led to execution by qisās or stoning, or facing a duel.
The key is 'ma fī ma'nā al-maraḍ' – what resembles illness in terms of facing destruction.
4
Apply to specific cases from the text
Analyze the cases: a man who sends another forward for a duel, or is brought forward to be killed: divorce in that state makes the wife an heir if he dies. But if he is besieged or in the battle line (where safety may also be possible), she does not inherit because death is not the dominant expectation.
The decisive factor is whether the situation is predominantly fatal.
5
Understand the legal mechanism: istihsān
The ruling is based on istihsān, treating the divorce as legally nonexistent for inheritance purposes, just as murder can deprive a killer of inheritance as a penalty.
Istihsān allows departing from strict analogy to prevent injustice.

إِنَّ حُكْمَ الْفِرَارِ قَدْ يَثْبُتُ بِمَا هُوَ فِى مَعْنَى الْمَرَضِ فِي تَوَجِهِ الْهَلَاكِ . ترجمه: فرار کا حکم شوہر کے مال سے عورت کے حق کے متعلق ہونے سے ثابت ہوتا ہے اور یہ حق اس مرض سے متعلق ہوتا ہے جس سے اکثر ہلاکت کا اندیشہ ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب بھی فرار کے حکم کی علت پائی جائے گی تو فرار کا حکم ثابت ہوگا اور چونکہ فرار کی علت یہ ہے کہ آدمی ایسی حالت میں اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دے کہ جس میں موت غالب رہتی ہو، ایسی حالت میں طلاق سے فرار ثابت ہوگا، اب یہ علت جہاں بھی پائی جائے گی تو فرار کا حکم یعنی بیوی کے لئے وراثت ثابت ہوگی۔اس کے برخلاف وہ حالت جس میں سلامتی غالب ہو اس میں طلاق دینے سے فرار ثابت نہیں ہو گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی شخص نے دوسرے کو مقابلے کے لئے میدان میں آگے کیا، یا اس کو آگے کیا تا کہ قصاصاً قتل کیا جائے ، یا رجم کیا جائے اور اسی حالت میں اس شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دیدی تو بیوی وراث نہیں بنے گی ، اگر وہ شخص اسی سبب سے یا کسی اور سبب سے مر گیا، اس لئے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں اس آدمی نے اپنی بیوی کو ایسی حالت وَمَنْ بَارَزَ رَجُلًا أَوْ قَدِمَ لِيَقْتُلَ بِقَوَدٍ أَوْ رَجُمٍ فَأَبَانَهَا وَرِثَتُ إِنْ مَاتَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَجْهِ أَوْ قُتِلَ، وَلَوْ مَحْصُورًا أَوْ فِي صَفٌ الْقِتَالِ لَا وَأَصْلُهُ مَا مَرَّ أَنَّ امْرَأَةَ الْفَارِّ تَرِتْ إِذَا مَاتَ وَهِيَ فِي الْعِدَّةِ اسْتِحْسَانًا بِأَنْ تَجْعَلَ الْبَيْنُونَةَ مَعْدُومَةً حُكْمًا كَمَا جُعِلَتْ الْفَرَابَةُ الثَّابِتَةُ مَعْدُومَةً حُكْمًا بِالْقَتْلِ جَزَاء لِظُلْمِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَثْبُتُ حُكْمُ الْفَرَارِ إِذَا تَعَلَّقَ حَقَّهَا بِمَالِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ حَقُّهَا بِهِ بِمَرَضِ يُخَافُ مِنْهُ الْهَلَافُ غَالِبًا بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صَاحِبَ فِرَاشٍ وَهُوَ الَّذِي لَا يَقُومُ بِحَوَائِجِهِ فِي الْبَيْتِ كَمَا يَعْتَادُهُ الأصحاء وَإِنْ كَانَ يَقْدِرُ عَلَى الْقِيَام بتكليف وَالَّذِى يَقُضى حَوَائِجَهُ فِي الْبَيْتِ وَهُوَ يَشْتَكِي لَا يَكُونُ فَارًا لأنَّ الْإِنْسَانَ قَلَّ مَا يَخُلُو عَنهُ. تبيين الحقائق كتاب الطلاق باب المريض، ج ۲ ص ۲۴۸، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب طلاق المريض، ج ۲ ص ۴۰۳، ط: رحمانیه
Indeed, the ruling of flight (al-firār) may be established by what is in the meaning of illness in terms of facing destruction. Translation: The ruling of flight is established by the wife's right being attached to the husband's wealth, and this right attaches due to an illness from which death is feared in most cases. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that whenever the cause of the ruling of flight is found, the ruling of flight will be established. Since the cause of flight is that a man divorces his wife in a state where death predominates, in such a state flight is established from the divorce. Now wherever this cause is found, the ruling of flight, i.e., inheritance for the wife, will be established. In contrast, in a state where safety predominates, divorce does not establish flight. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a person sends another forward for a duel, or sends him forward to be killed by qisās or by stoning, and in that state he divorces his wife, then the wife does not become an heir even if he dies from that cause or another, because in that case the man gave divorce in a state... And whoever duels a man or is brought forward to be killed by qisās or stoning, and he divorces her, she inherits if he dies in that manner or is killed. But if he is besieged or in the battle line, she does not inherit. The basis of this is what has passed: that the wife of a fār inherits if he dies while she is in 'iddah, by istihsan, by considering the separation as nonexistent in legal effect, just as the established relationship was considered nonexistent in legal effect by killing as a penalty for his injustice. And the ruling of flight is only established when her right is attached to his wealth, and her right is only attached to it by an illness from which destruction is feared, by him being bedridden – he who cannot fulfill his household needs as healthy people are accustomed, even if he is able to stand with difficulty. The one who performs his household needs while complaining is not considered a fār, because a person rarely is free from that.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage extends the principle of flight (firār) to situations other than illness that carry a high risk of death. The cause ('illah) is the attachment of the wife's right to the husband's wealth, which occurs when the husband is in a state where death is predominantly feared. This includes conditions like being in a duel, being led to execution by qisās or stoning, or being in a situation where death is likely. However, if the husband is merely besieged or in the battle line (where safety may also be possible), flight is not established. The text then quotes from Tabyīn al-Ḥaqā'iq, clarifying that the legal effect of flight (inheritance) applies only when the wife's right has attached to the husband's wealth, which happens when he is bedridden and unable to attend to his own needs. A person who is ill but still active is not considered a fār. This ruling is based on istihsān, treating the divorce as void for inheritance purposes, just as murder can make the killer lose inheritance as a penalty.

Key Terms
فرار (firār): The act of divorcing one's wife in a life-threatening situation to deprive her of inheritance.
سبب (sabab): The cause ('illah) of flight, i.e., the attachment of the wife's right to the husband's wealth.
صاحب فراش (ṣāḥib firāsh): One who is bedridden and unable to attend to personal needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man is in a duel and divorces his wife. He dies from a wound in the duel. According to Hanafi fiqh, does his wife inherit?
A) Yes, because the duel is a situation with high death risk.
B) No, because it is not an illness.
C) Yes, only if she is in 'iddah after divorce.
D) No, because he died from an external cause.

Key Takeaway
The cause of flight is the high probability of death, not the specific nature of the threat, so the ruling applies to all analogous situations.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Principle of Firar: Evasion of Inheritance through Divorce
Sets the ruling and conditions for firar in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on firar and identify the objective conditions that trigger inheritance despite a valid irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
A man cannot disinherit his wife by divorcing her on his deathbed—the law nullifies the divorce for inheritance.

This passage from Al-Hidayah deals with a critical principle: the evasion of inheritance through divorce. When a husband divorces his wife while facing imminent death, the Hanafi school, by istihsan (juristic preference), allows her to inherit, treating the divorce as non-existent for inheritance purposes. We will examine the ruling, its proof from the case of mubarazah, and the precise conditions that establish the state of firar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
First, note that the divorce is valid (sahih) but inheritance is granted by istihsan, overriding the usual qiyas that would cut off inheritance. The basis is the principle of preventing the husband's evasion (firar) of the wife's inheritance right.
The ruling applies only if the wife is in 'iddah at the husband's death.
2
Analyze the Proof: The Mubarazah Case
The proof is an analogy (qiyas khafi) to the case of a man who duels or is led to execution and divorces his wife; she inherits if he dies from that cause. This establishes the 'illa (effective cause) as the husband's action to deprive the wife in a situation where death is the dominant probability.
This is a case of istihsan overriding qiyas.
3
Define the Condition: Marad al-Mawt
The passage defines the state of likely death as being bedridden and unable to attend to one's own needs, even if able to stand with difficulty. Mere complaint or pain that does not prevent normal activity does not qualify.
The test is objective: what is the dominant probability? Death must be more likely than survival.
4
Distinguish Scenarios: Dominant Death vs. Safety
The text lists scenarios where death is dominant (being at sea in a storm, falling into a predator's mouth, progressive illness) and where safety is dominant (siege, battle line, minor injuries). The ruling of firar applies only to the former.
If the condition is static or improving, it falls under the second category.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a valid bain divorce always cuts off inheritance, regardless of the husband's state. The Hanafi school uses istihsan to prevent injustice, and this is the mufta bihi position.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is clear: inheritance is granted in Hanafi, denied in Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ حُكْمَ الْفِرَارِ قَدْ يَثْبُتُ بِمَا هُوَ فِى مَعْنَى الْمَرَضِ فِي تَوَجِهِ الْهَلَاكِ . وَمَنْ بَارَزَ رَجُلًا أَوْ قَدِمَ لِيَقْتُلَ بِقَوَدٍ أَوْ رَجُمٍ فَأَبَانَهَا وَرِثَتُ إِنْ مَاتَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَجْهِ أَوْ قُتِلَ، وَلَوْ مَحْصُورًا أَوْ فِي صَفِّ الْقِتَالِ لَا وَأَصْلُهُ مَا مَرَّ أَنَّ امْرَأَةَ الْفَارِّ تَرِثُ إِذَا مَاتَ وَهِيَ فِي الْعِدَّةِ اسْتِحْسَانًا بِأَنْ تَجْعَلَ الْبَيْنُونَةَ مَعْدُومَةً حُكْمًا كَمَا جُعِلَتِ الْفَرَابَةُ الثَّابِتَةُ مَعْدُومَةً حُكْمًا بِالْقَتْلِ جَزَاءً لِظُلْمِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَثْبُتُ حُكْمُ الْفِرَارِ إِذَا تَعَلَّقَ حَقُّهَا بِمَالِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ حَقُّهَا بِهِ بِمَرَضٍ يُخَافُ مِنْهُ الْهَلَافُ غَالِبًا بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صَاحِبَ فِرَاشٍ وَهُوَ الَّذِي لَا يَقُومُ بِحَوَائِجِهِ فِي الْبَيْتِ كَمَا يَعْتَادُهُ الْأَصِحَّاءُ وَإِنْ كَانَ يَقْدِرُ عَلَى الْقِيَامِ بِتَكْلِيفٍ وَالَّذِي يَقْضِي حَوَائِجَهُ فِي الْبَيْتِ وَهُوَ يَشْتَكِي لَا يَكُونُ فَارًّا لِأَنَّ الْإِنْسَانَ قَلَّمَا يَخْلُو عَنْهُ . وَالْأَصْلُ الْمَذْكُورُ هُوَ ثُبُوتُ حُكْمِ الْفِرَارِ بِمَا هُوَ فِي مَعْنَى الْمَرَضِ فِي تَوَجُّهِ الْهَلَاكِ الْغَالِبِ وَعَدَمُ ثُبُوتِهِ فِيمَا كَانَ الْغَالِبُ مِنْهُ السَّلَامَةَ، فَمِنَ الْأَوَّلِ النَّازِلُ فِي السَّفِينَةِ وَالرَّاكِبُ فِي السَّفِينَةِ وَبَقِيَ عَلَى لَوْحٍ، وَكَذَا فِي الْمُحِيطِ وَفِي جَوَامِعِ الْفِقْهِ كَثَانٍ فِي سَفِينَةٍ فَاضْطَرَبَتِ الْأَمْوَاجُ، وَكَانَ الْغَالِبُ مِنْهُ الْغَرَقُ فَهُوَ كَمَرَضِ الْمَوْتِ، وَكَذَا الْوَاقِعُ فِي فَمِ السَّبُعِ وَالْمَسْلُولُ وَالْمَفْلُوجُ وَالْمَقْعَدُ مَا دَامَ يُزَادَ مَا بِهِ فَهُوَ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ، وَصَاحِبُ جُرْحٍ وَقُرْحَةٍ أَوْ وَجَعٍ لَمْ يُصِبْهُ عَلَى الْفِرَاشِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الصَّحِيحِ فِي الطَّلَاقِ وَغَيْرِهِ.
Indeed, the ruling of firar (evasion) is established by that which is in the meaning of illness in the direction of destruction. And whoever duels a man or is brought forward to be killed by qisas or stoning, and divorces his wife, she inherits if he dies in that manner or is killed. But if he is besieged or in the battle line, she does [not] inherit. Its basis is what has passed: that the wife of the evader (farr) inherits if he dies while she is in the 'iddah, by istihsan, making the separation (baynunah) non-existent legally, just as the established relationship (qarabah) is made non-existent legally by killing as a penalty for his injustice. And the ruling of firar is only established when her right relates to his wealth, and her right only relates to it by an illness from which destruction is generally feared, such that he is bedridden, and he does not attend to his needs in the house as the healthy are accustomed, even if he is able to stand with difficulty. And one who attends to his needs in the house while complaining is not a farr, because a human is rarely free from that. And the mentioned principle is the establishment of the ruling of firar by that which is in the meaning of illness regarding the direction of likely destruction, and its non-establishment in what is likely to be safe. From the first category: the one descending into the ship, the rider in the ship, who remains on a plank. Likewise in Al-Muhit and in Jami' al-Fiqh: a piece of cloth in a ship whose waves struggle, and the likely outcome is drowning, then it is like the illness of death. Likewise the one fallen into the mouth of a beast, the consumptive, the paralyzed, and the bedridden – as long as his condition increases it is from the second category, otherwise from the first. And one with a wound or sore or pain that does not put him to bed is like the healthy in divorce and other matters.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on the Hanafi principle of firar (evasion) in the context of divorce. The ruling: If a man in a state of likely death divorces his wife with a bain (irrevocable) divorce, the wife inherits from him if she is still in 'iddah when he dies, even though the divorce would normally cut off inheritance. This is an istihsan (juristic preference) overriding the qiyas that would deny inheritance. The proof: The principle is derived from the case of a man who engages in a duel (mubarazah) or is led to be executed, and divorces his wife – she inherits if he dies from that cause. The 'illa (effective cause) for firar is the husband's intention to deprive the wife of inheritance by divorcing her in a state where death is the dominant probability. Therefore, the ruling applies when the husband is suffering from a fatal illness (marad al-mawt) that generally leads to death, as defined by being bedridden and unable to attend to his own needs. The passage then classifies various situations: being at sea in a storm, falling into a predator's mouth, etc., where death is dominant. Those where safety is dominant (e.g., siege or battle line where support is present) do not constitute firar. This is from the Hanafi school, specifically the later commentaries (fatawa mutakhkhirin) refining the principles from Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الفِرَار (al-firar): The action of a husband divorcing his wife when death is imminent to deprive her of inheritance.
الْبَيْنُونَة (al-baynunah): The state of separation after a bain divorce, which legally cuts off inheritance but is treated as non-existent in firar.
اسْتِحْسَان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas to achieve equity, used here to allow inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle of firar, under which condition does a divorcee inherit from her deceased husband?
A) Only if the husband explicitly states his intention to deprive her.
B) If the divorce occurred during a state where death is likely, and she is still in her 'iddah.
C) If the husband died in a duel.
D) If the divorce was pronounced during a siege.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, by istihsan, allows a wife to inherit from her husband if he divorces her in a state of dominant probable death, treating the divorce as non-existent for inheritance purposes.

Definition of Raj'ah: Continuation of Marital Ownership
Defines raj'ah as the continuation of marital ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of raj'ah and explain why it is a continuation of marriage, not a new contract.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is the continuation of the suspended marital ownership (milk) during the 'iddah, not a new marriage.

This lesson unpacks the concise Hanafi definition of raj'ah. The single Arabic sentence 'إِنَّ الرَّجُعَةَ اِسْتِدَامَةُ الْمِلْكِ' (Indeed, raj'ah is the continuation of ownership) captures the entire legal nature of revocable return. Understanding this phrase is key to distinguishing raj'ah from a new marriage contract and to grasping when it is valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the core definition
Read the Arabic phrase and its translation. Memorize that raj'ah is 'istidamah al-milk' (continuation of ownership).
This phrase is the foundational axiom.
2
Identify the key term 'milk'
Understand that 'milk' here means marital authority or ownership, which is suspended by a revocable divorce but not extinguished until 'iddah ends.
'Milk' in Hanafi fiqh is the right of marital relations and authority.
3
Analyze 'istidamah' (continuation)
Because raj'ah is a continuation, it does not initiate a new contract. No new dowry, witnesses, or contract are needed.
Contrast with a new marriage that requires all these elements.
4
State the condition for validity
Raj'ah is valid only during the wife's 'iddah after a revocable divorce. After 'iddah, the milk is permanently extinguished.
If the 'iddah expires, a new marriage contract is required.
5
Contrast with a new marriage
List differences: raj'ah is automatic continuation; new marriage requires offer, acceptance, witnesses, and dowry.
This distinction prevents legal confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الرَّجُعَةَ اِسْتِدَامَةُ الْمِلْكِ .
Indeed, raj'ah is the continuation of ownership.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling from the chapter on raj'ah (return) in divorce. The definition: Raj'ah (revocable return) is the legal act of continuing the marital ownership (milk) that was suspended by a raj'ī (revocable) divorce. The ruling: Raj'ah is valid only during the 'iddah period of the wife. Since it is a continuation, not a new marriage, no new contract or dowry is needed. The Hanafi school defines raj'ah as a continuation of the existing marriage, which is why it requires the 'iddah to be ongoing. If the 'iddah expires, the milk is completely terminated, and a new marriage is required. This principle is from the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الرَّجُعَة (al-raj'ah): The husband's act of taking back his wife after a revocable (raj'ī) divorce during her 'iddah, without a new marriage contract.
الْمِلْك (al-milk): Marital ownership/authority over the wife, suspended but not extinguished by a raj'ī divorce until 'iddah ends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is raj'ah (revocable return) valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only before the divorce is pronounced.
B) During the wife's 'iddah after a raj'ī divorce.
C) After the 'iddah has expired with the wife's consent.
D) Only if a new marriage contract is made.

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is the continuation of the marriage, valid only during the wife's 'iddah, and does not require a new contract.
